                                                        xii
EXECUTIVE SUMMARY

1.0    Background

The United Republic of Tanzania (URT) has large stockpiles of obsolete pesticides that
have deteriorated over the years. These materials are considered as hazardous waste.
According to the inventory database, Tanzania has about 700 tons of obsolete pesticides
identified in 149 stores countrywide. Other associated wastes identified include: 3.6 tons
of contaminated materials, 1.2 tons of contaminated equipments, 2755 tons of
contaminated soils and about 382 empty containers. The obsolete pesticides include large
volumes of organo-chlorinated products, notably DDT, which is classified among
persistent organic pollutants (POPs) under the Stockholm Convention. There are also
large quantities of organo-phosphate pesticides with high toxicity. Tanzania is included
among the first seven countries to benefit from the financing through the Africa
Stockpiles Program (ASP) funded by the Global Environment Facility (GEF) through the
WB as implementing agency to the ASP. The objective of the ASP Tanzania Project is to
assist the government in: (a) eliminating inventoried publicly held obsolete pesticide
stocks and associated waste; and (b) implementing measures to reduce and prevent future
related risks. The ASP Tanzania Project has four main components of clean up and
disposal, prevention of accumulation, capacity building and project management. The
National Environment Management Council (NEMC), which is within the Vice
President’s Office (Environmental Affairs), is the executing agency for ASP – Tanzania.

The storage, handling, transportation, disposal of pesticide and site remediation activities
and operations are associated with adverse environmental impacts. Consequently,
Country Environmental and Social Assessment (CESA) was prepared for the
implementation of the ASP programme in the country. The major aim of CESA is to put
in place a decision making tool for environmentally sound management of obsolete
pesticides.


2.0    Country Setting

Tanzania has about 945,000 square kilometers and lies approximately between latitudes
1o and 12o south and longitudes 29o and 40o east. The climate of Tanzania is influenced
by two main factors, of location in terms of latitude and position at the eastern edge of the
African continent. The country is a land of vast plains, plateaus and mountains. Except
for a narrow belt along the coast, most of country land lies 300m above sea level with
much of the country at an altitude higher than 1,000m above sea level. Annual rainfall in
Tanzania ranges from 400mm per annum mainly in the central parts of the country to
2300mm in the highlands and western parts of Lake Victoria (Figure 2.1). Precipitation is
not uniform across the country, but is marked by spatial, annual and inter-annual
variability. In terms of temperature country’s mean annual ranges from 21oC in the
highland areas to 29oC at the sea level. The year-to-year variability is very small and
departures from the long-term means generally insignificant. In all parts of country July
is the coolest month of the year.



                                            xiii
The major river systems found in the country include Rufiji, Pangani, Malagarasi, Ruvu,
Wami, Ruhuhu, Kiwira, Kagera, Mara, and Kilombero. The surface river flow regime
and moisture conditions in the country correspond to the general rainfall pattern. Rivers
and lakes start rising in November/December and experience a maximum in March –
April, with a recession period from May to October - November. Generally, the rainfall
season starts in October/November and ends in September/October. Tanzania has seven
major lakes including Lake Victoria, Eyasi, Nyasa, Manyara, Natron, Rukwa and
Tanganyika. Lake Victoria is the largest Lake in Africa and the largest tropical lake in the
World and the second largest fresh water Lake in the World in terms of surface area. The
main wetlands of Tanzania include Moyowosi swamps and floodplain, Kilombero flood
plain, Usangu, Wembere and Ruvu wetlands. Coastal wetlands are formed by wave
action along the shoreline. It includes mud flats, marshes, mangrove swamps, estuaries
and deltas.

The vegetation types found in Tanzania include forests, woodlands, bushlands and
grassland. Forests cover 47617 sq km or 5% of the country, woodlands 313871 sq km or
33%, bushland 234983 or 25%, grassland 151128 or 16%, mangrove forest 1512 or
0.16% and agriculture 128171 or 14%. Out of this area, 13000 million ha have been
gazetted as forest reserves while about 1.6 million ha of the gazetted forest reserves are
under water catchments management. Tanzania has a rich and diverse spectrum of fauna
including a wide variety of endemic species and sub-species. The most common game
species include buffalo, wildebeest, hippo, elephant, zebra, impala, bushbuck and
reedbuck, and predators such as the leopard and lion are widely distributed throughout
the country. The country boasts of an extensive wildlife protected areas network,
covering about 28% of the total land area. Out of this, 4% fall under the category of
National Parks (NPs), 15% Game Reserves (GRs), 1% the Ngorongoro Conservation
Area (NCA), and 8% Game Controlled Areas (GCAs). About 3% of protected areas
devoted wildlife conservation overlap with forest reserves.

According to the 2002 census, the country’s total population stood at 33.5 million (URT,
2003). The population of the country has nearly trebled in the four decades, growing at an
annual average growth of 3.22% between 1967 and 1978, at the rate of 2.8% between
1978 and 1988 and 2.9% between 1988 and 2002 (URT, 1967, 1978, 1988 and 2002).
The high population increase is found in areas with high employment opportunities
especially urban areas. The urban centers have been increasing over time. The overall
hierarchy of settlements in Tanzania is therefore depicted by 5 cities (Dar es Salaam,
Mwanza, Mbeya Tanga and Arusha), 12 municipalities (Dodoma, Moshi, Morogoro,
Mtwara Mikindani, Songea, Iringa, Singida, Tabora, Shinyanga, Bukoba, Kigoma and
Musoma), 9 towns (such as, Njombe, Korogwe, etc), 86 Townships (Kulaba and Mkai,
2004). Until 2006, reports from the Ministry of Local Government showed that there
were 10,334 registered Villages.

The total road network in Tanzania covers about 85,000 km, of which trunk roads take
12%; regional roads 24%; district roads 24%; urban roads 1%, and unclassified roads
take 35% (URT 2006), whereas 40% of the total road network is bituminized. Trunk



                                            xiv
roads are the national and international through routes, which link several regions and
provide access to important border posts. Analysis on the conditions of trunk and regional
roads in Tanzania shows that 51% of the roads are in good condition, 33% in fair
condition and 11% are in poor condition. Statistics for the year 1972 show that there were
only 121 hospitals country-wide. In the year 2000 there was an increase in health services
in the country, for example there were 529 health centres and 4005 dispensaries. In 2004
there was further increase in heath services to 217 hospitals, 434 health centres and 4,408
dispensaries.


3.0    Policy, legal and Institutional framework

Tanzania has a number of instruments for sustainable development guidance in the
country in terms of policy, legal and institutional arrangement. The national policies,
strategies, acts and the institutional framework as well as international conventions and
treaties that underpin the management of obsolete pesticides were taken into account in
developing CESA. The following are some of the key policies that are relevant to CESA:
National Environmental Policy (1997), Wildlife Policy of Tanzania (1998) (and its
revised version of 2007), Water Policy (2002), National Gender Policy (1999), National
Land Policy (1995), Agriculture and Livestock Policy (1997), National Health Policy
(2003), National Disaster Management Policy (2004).

The main legal frame work instruments includes the Environmental Management Act
(2004) with its associated Regulations of Environmental Impact assessment and
Auditing(2005), The Plant Protection Act (1997), Land Use Commission Act (1984),
The Water Resources Management Act (2009), Merchant Shipping Act (2003), The
Surface and Marine Transport Regulatory Authority Act (2001), the Public Health Act
(2008), Occupational Health and Safety Act (2003), Industrial and Consumer Chemicals
(Management and Control) Act (2003), and the Environmental Management (Soil
Quality Standards) Regulations (2007). In addition to policy and legal instruments, the
following National and International programmes or conventions were taken into
account. These are the National Strategy for growth and Reduction of Poverty (NSGRP)
and the National Environmental Action Plan (1994). The international conventions that
were taken into account include the Basel Convention for the Control of Tranboundary
Movements of Hazardous Waste and their Disposal (1989), The Rotterdam Convention
on the Prior Informed Consent Procedure for Certain Hazardous Chemicals and
Pesticides in International Trade; and the Stockholm Convention on Persistent Organic
Pollutants (POPs).


4.0    Baseline Information

According to the inventory database, Tanzania has about 700 metric tons of obsolete
pesticides, 3.6 metric tons of contaminated materials, 2,000 kg of contaminated building
materials and components, 1.25 tons of contaminated equipment, 2755 tons of
contaminated soils and about 382 empty containers. The stores were classified into three



                                            xv
categories of high, medium and low risk based on toxicity, quantity, surrounding
environmental conditions and the security of the store. The categorization of the stores
followed the FAO Environmental Management Toolkit in which Environmental Factor
(Fe) value higher than 50 have been considered problematic. Related to the risk posed by
the type and quantity of the obsolete pesticides (Fp), an Fp criterion value of above
50,000 was considered to be a high risk factor. The main factors for obsolete pesticide
accumulation are purchase of pesticides in excess of demand, contingency stocks that
remained unused, product ban e.g. DDT; Untimely distribution from central purchasing
stores to different parts of the country; acceptance of inappropriate donations (e.g. near
expired products, improperly labled products with foreign languages like Japanese etc)
and poor storage and management of pesticides stocks. The capacity of the country to
undertake a nationwide safeguarding operation of the obsolete pesticide stocks and
contaminated soil sites can be assessed based on availability of financial resources,
human resources (expertise) required, appropriate technology, experience, equipment and
facilities, appropriate safeguarding premises, types and condition of the obsolete pesticide
stocks and contaminated soils locations sites, and quantities of obsolete pesticide stocks
that need to be safeguarded. The capacity has been found to be adequate for small
operations but inadequate for large operations.


5.0    Environmental and Social Impacts

Obsolete pesticides have caused many, varied and widespread adverse impacts on human
health and the environment. Examples of these adverse impacts include death and
disability among pesticide users and the communities around them, global transport and
bio-accumulation of pesticides, contamination of local water supplies and loss of income
to farmers whose produce contains unacceptable levels of pesticide residues.

Stores containing obsolete pesticides and associated waste (149 in total) have been
categorized into high, medium and low risk. Nine stores were categorised as of high risk
which are at Tropical Pesticides Research Institute (TPRI); Morogoro Farmers
Cooperative Union (MOFACU); Highland Mbarali Estates Farm; Tumbi Agricultural
Research Institute; Lushoto DALDO; Vikuge in the Coast Region (both store and
contaminated site); Uyole Research Farm; Utete former agricultural store; Kingori village
which include Ngyani Cooperative Society in Arumeru district.

Nineteen (19) stores have been categorized as of medium risk and are located at
Vingunguti Plant Health Services (PHS); Tanzania Cotton Board (Kurasini); Kimara
Baruti; Mahonda Sugar Factory in Zanzibar; Utete Boma; Tengeru PHS (contaminated
site); Tanzania Building Agency (TBA); Bagamoyo DALDO; Kilombero Sugar
Company; Arusha Seed Farm; Regional Agricultural Advisor Tanga; TACRI Lyamungo;
Tanzania Flowers in Arusha; Burka Coffee Estate; Mara Co-operative Union; Kolping
Society in Kagera; Urambo Seed Farm; Tunduru DALDO; and TLTC in Iringa. The rest
of the stores were classified as low risk.




                                            xvi
Similarly the seriousness of social and environmental impacts is put in three main groups
based on risk level of the pesticide stores. The impacts are most serious in high risk stores
and low in medium and low risk sites. Typical examples of impacts in high risk stores
include the exposure of the workers due to the leakage of the pesticides and also due to
the generation of fumes and offensive odour. Secondly, the contamination of irrigation,
ground water or soils, food crops and livestock forage. Additionally the workers and the
local communities are exposed to nuisance due to offensive odour that is generated by
obsolete pesticides. In addition some impacts will be generated during the repackaging,
transportation and disposal of the obsolete pesticide.


6.0    Environmental Management Plan (EMP)

The objective of the Environmental Management Plan (EMP) for this CESA is to provide
a strategy for the management of OPs with a specific focus on safeguarding and final
disposal of the OPs and pesticide contaminated empty containers in order to reduce the
impacts and risks posed by poor management of OPs throughout Tanzania.

The EMP covers all aspects related to the safeguarding and disposal operations such as
the prioritisation of sites to be safeguarded, the identification of suitable interim and main
collection centres; repackaging operations including registration; transport; storage and
also it shall address the final disposal options (local/international) of the OP including
compliance with relevant international chemical conventions, codes of practice and
guidelines (e.g Basel Convention; IMDG Code and ADR Agreement, among others).

To ensure that all current and potential future impacts of the OP stores and contaminated
sites are covered, the EMP will be implemented in two steps. Step 1 will focus on
mitigation of effects of the current situation while Step 2 will focus on mitigation of
effects of safeguarding and disposal operations.

To prevent public health and environmental hazards from getting out of hand at OP stores
and contaminated sites that are in critical conditions, emergency safeguarding will be
carried out while awaiting the main safeguarding and disposal measures.

Before the main planned safeguarding operations commence, emergence safeguarding
measures need to be taken to put an end to health and environmental risks from
deteriorated OP storage facilities.

The emergence safeguarding measures will be similar to the ones previously carried out
by NEMC at various OP stores in the country. They include repackaging of chemicals
whose containers are deteriorated. The emergency safeguarding measures include fencing
off OP stores and pesticide contaminated areas, and rehabilitation of seriously dilapidated
OP stores. The types and extents of the emergence safeguarding measures will be
assessed on a case by case basis using data obtained from surveys carried out by NEMC
nationwide previously.




                                            xvii
7.0    Analysis of Alternatives

7.1    Pesticide Safeguarding Alternatives

With respect to the safeguarding needs of the obsolete pesticides, implications of three
alternatives were considered and analysed. The alternatives are: i) no safeguarding action,
ii) safeguarding at the current location of each store, and iii) use of collection centres to
safeguard pesticides moved from the currently dispersed individual stores. To
comprehensively assess the three alternatives, an analysis of their Strengths, Weaknesses,
Opportunities, and Threats (SWOT) was carried out.

Notably the SWOT analysis revealed that, unlike the options of safeguarding at the
current store locations and use of collection centres, the option of no safeguarding action
does not present any meaningful opportunities. This is understandable because the no
action option simply maintains the status quo whose deficiencies are the very reason for
carrying out this project.

Based on the SWOT analysis, it is evident that the use of collection centres for
safeguarding is the option that is most favourable. This opion is the one that is likely to
be most cost-effective and as such the one recommended for adoption in subsequent
decisions in this project.


7.2    Pesticide Transport Means Alternatives

To come up with the best option for transporting the obsolete pesticides within the
country from their eventual safeguarding locations to their disposal place or final
shipping port, three alternatives were identified and analysed. The alternatives are: i)
railway transport, ii) road transport, and iii) a combination of railway and road transport.
For the transportation needs of the obsolete pesticides outside the country, two
alternatives were considered and analysed: i) air freighting and ii) ocean freighting.

The SWOT analysis of the alternative transport means for the OP from the original stores
to the shipping port indicates that road transport is the most favourable option. On the
other hand, the SWOT analysis shows that for transporting the OP from the shipping port
to the ultimate disposal place, ocean freighting is the favourable option.


7.3    Pesticide and Pesticide Container Disposal Alternatives

With a view to objectively determining the best options for the disposal of the obsolete
pesticides and their containers, identification of disposal alternatives was done on the
basis of where rather than how the pesticides and pesticide containers could best be
disposed of. It was assumed that proven and established disposal technologies as well as
appropriately competent and experienced disposal contractors would be employed. The
options for disposing of the pesticides within the country and shipping to another country



                                            xviii
for eventual disposal were considered and assessed using a SWOT analysis. Similarly,
the options for disposing of pesticide containers within the country and shipping to
another country for eventual disposal were considered and analysed.

The SWOT analysis indicates that it is more advantageous to carry out the final disposal
of obsolete pesticides outside than within the country. With respect to the disposal of OP
containers and washwater, however, the SWOT showed that it is better to dispose of them
within than outside the country.


7.4    Cost Considerations for options for safeguarding and disposal of OP

Option 1: A clean sweep of inventorised OP and safeguarding of contaminated soils

considered is significantly more advantageous than the other. Based on this analysis,
therefore, neither Option 1 nor Option 2 deserves preferential treatment.


8.0    Environmental and Social Monitoring Plan

8.1    Background

Monitoring is needed to check if and to what extent the impacts are mitigated benefits
enhanced and new problems addressed. Plans for monitoring have been included in the
Environmental and Social Monitoring Plan. The monitoring plan as for ESMP also
assigns responsibilities for monitoring activities.

Monitoring of safeguarding activities shall be undertaken by members of the Project
Management Unit (PMU) involving (the Field Managers (FMs) from NEMC, National


                                           xix
Project Coordinator (NPC), and Technical Advisor for Disposal (TAD) and Zonal
coordinators (ZC) from MAFS&C. Where resettlement will be necessary, local
government officials will be involved in the monitoring exercise. Non-governmental
Organisations and other Community Services Organisations will constitute the
monitoring team for the safeguarding activities FAO technical Support Missions
monitoring and simultaneously provide backstopping advice and follow up from the FAO
Headquarters etc.


8.2    Project Team Monitoring and Reporting

Field Managers (FMs)

The day to day monitoring of the field activities shall be undertaken by the FMs. There
will be a variety of standard forms to communicate and monitor activities which are
maintained in the database within the project office and files and electronically for
reference, follow-up and auditing. The forms concern:

       Daily briefing
       Project checklists
       Daily progress reports
       Weekly progress reports
       Work plans


Monitoring of the PMU

It is recommended that a suitably qualified independent consultant be procured to
monitor and evaluate the performance of the PMU. The consultant should necessarily be
well qualified in important aspects of OP management based on both academic training
and experience acquired through undertaking similar assignments.


9.0    Environmental and Social Management Training Requirements

Tanzania currently has some capacity in obsolete pesticides management. This capacity
has been developed as a result of training and field experience gained from the
implementation of past initiatives in Tanzania. One of the main objectives of the ASP in
Tanzania is to build on the existing capacity and to establish new capacity in each of the
key components of the project. Specifically, those who will be involved in safeguarding
and disposal activities as elucidated in this CESA document need to be properly trained
to enable them discharge their duties effectively, safely and in an environmentally
friendly manner.


In view of the foregoing, capacity needs to be developed in the following areas:


                                           xx
 (i)     Health and safety,
 (ii)    Environmental protection,
 (iii)   Equipment supply,
 (iv)    Public consultation, management,
 (v)     Environmental policies and programs
 (vi)    Management, planning and budgeting

The envisaged training objectives will be achieved by organizing appropriate induction
courses and training workshops. Beneficiaries of the training will include NEMC staff,
MAFS&C staff, PMU members, and zonal coordinators.




                                          xxi
                                         CHAPTER ONE
1.0 INTRODUCTION

1.1      Background

The United Republic of Tanzania (URT) has large stockpiles of obsolete pesticides that
have deteriorated over the years. These materials are considered as hazardous waste. In
1997-1998, the Government of the Netherlands supported the URT in carrying out a
nationwide inventory of obsolete stockpiles and identified about 1000 metric tons of
obsolete pesticides and some 200 metric tons of veterinary waste, mainly drugs and
vaccines. The obsolete pesticides include large volumes of organo-chlorinated products,
notably DDT, which is classified as Persistent Organic Pollutants (POPs) under the
Stockholm Convention. There are also large quantities of organo-phosphate pesticides
with high toxicity for people and livestock which were scattered over more than 300
stores. The majority of the stores do not meet the basic requirements for environmentally
sound storage of hazardous chemicals as set by FAO and United Nations Environment
Programme (UNEP). The stocks were posing a direct threat to human health and the
environment.

In recognition of this problem identified by the 1997/1998 inventory, URT made an
official appeal for assistance from the international donor community during the FAO
Third Consultation on Prevention and Disposal of Obsolete Pesticide Stocks, which was
held in Rome in March 1998. Tanzania was subsequently included among the first seven
countries to benefit from the financing through the Africa Stockpiles Programme (ASP)
funded by the Global Environment Facility (GEF)1 through the WB as implementing
agency to the ASP2. A Country workshop was held in August 2002 in Dar es Salaam to
discuss and evaluate the scope of the obsolete pesticide problem in the country and made
recommendations regarding appropriate actions to be undertaken. Thereafter, a project
Formulation mission from the FAO Investment Centre and the Plant Production and
Protection Division (AGPP) visited Tanzania in November 2002. Subsequently, a Pre-
appraisal mission was conducted by the WB in March 2003. This was followed by the
ASP Partners meeting at Midrand, Republic of South Africa (RSA) in September 2003,
which was attended by representatives of the Government of URT. This culminated with
an Appraisal mission by the WB in April 2004.

A GEF Trust Fund Grant Agreement was subsequently signed between the Government
and the World Bank and ASP Tanzania Project became effective upon its signature in
December 2006.




1
  Other countries include Ethiopia, Mali, Morocco South Africa, , Tunisia (full disposal) and Nigeria
(preparation only)
2
  The WB was invited by the ASP partners to take the role of implementing agency


                                                     1
1.2    Components of ASP Project in Tanzania

The Tanzania project comprises four inter-related components aimed at removing the
obsolete pesticides and ensuring that the problem does not re-occur. They include:
1) Clean up and disposal: (i) inventory (pesticides, containers, soils and buried
     pesticides), risk assessment, data base development and a register of contaminated
     sites; (ii) preparation of the CESA; (iii) safeguarding of the pesticides (repackaging,
     collection, transportation and interim storage) and (iv) environmentally sound
     disposal of the obsolete pesticides and associated wastes either locally or
     internationally. The clean up and disposal component also includes store cleaning
     and/or decontamination after the obsolete pesticides and associated waste have been
     cleared from the stores.
2) Prevention of Accumulation: (i) communication and awareness raising activities
     on the ASP, (ii) a review and where needed update of the legal and regulatory
     framework for the management of pesticides, (iii) preparation and implementation
     of a pilot scale Integrated Pest Management (IPM) strategy.
3) Capacity Building: The strengthening of the capacity of the Government and other
     sectors to implement the three project components through the provision of training
     by the Technical Support Unit (TSU) based at FAO, World Bank (WB) and other
     training providers.
4) Project Management: This includes resource support to the Government including
     the Project Management Unit (PMU) to implement the project. In addition the TSU
     and the WB will provide project monitoring, reporting and evaluation.

1.3    Management of the Project

The National Environment Management Council (NEMC) which is within the Vice
President’s Office (Environmental Affairs) is the executing agency for ASP – Tanzania.
The Programme is managed through a Project Management Unit (PMU) headed by
Project Coordinator. Other key staff members include two field managers, a prevention
coordinator seconded from the Ministry of Agriculture, Technical Advisor for disposal,
Monitoring and Evaluation Officer and Communication Officer. Six agriculture zonal
coordinators coordinate with the ASP- PMU in project implementation. A procurement
officer is also available within NEMC.

A steering committee is set up to oversee ASP implementation in the country. The
Committee comprises members from the following key institutions: (1) Permanent
Secretary fromVice Presidents Office (Chair), (2) Ministry of Agriculture and Food
Security (Vice Chair), (3) Director General from NEMC (Secretary), (4) Occupational
Safety and Health Authority (OSHA), (5) Tropical Pesticides Research Institute (TPRI),
(6) Government Chemist Laboratory Agency, (7) Department of Environment (Zanzibar)
(8) Tanzania Ports Authority (9) Department of Agriculture, Zanzibar, and (10) Focal
Point for the Basel and Stockholm Convention based at the Division of Environment. A
parallel Forum is set up to include members from NGOs, Civil Society organizations and
Intergovernmental organizations.




                                             2
1.4      Rationale for conducting CESA

Pesticide repackaging, storage, handling, transportation, disposal and site remediation
activities in country operations are associated with adverse environmental impacts.
Because of the significance of the impacts, Country Environmental and Social
Assessment (CESA) and Environmental Management Plan (EMP) need to be prepared
for the safeguarding and disposal activities. The major aim of developing the Country
Environmental and Social Assessment (CESA) and Environmental Management Plan
(EMP) is to put in place a decision making tool for environmentally sound management
of obsolete pesticides.

The project triggers two World Bank policies, i.e. OP 4.01 on Environmental
Assessments, which requires the preparation of a full EA and OP 4.09 on Pest
Management which requires a Pest Management Plan (PMP). However, since the project
itself meets the requirements of a PMP, there will be no need to prepare a self-standing
document. The applicability of two additional policies OP 4.12 on Involuntary
Resettlement and OP 4.04 on Natural Habitats was evaluated by consulting communities
located in vicinity of the obsolete pesticides storage sites and conducting recconaisance
survey.

It is also worth mentioning that it is a legal requirement for countries under the ASP
umbrella to complete a CESA and EMP as defined above before funds can be released
for the safeguarding and disposal of obsolete stocks and associated waste. In view of the
above, conducting the Country-project Environmental and Social Assessment (CESA) is
a critical component of the ASP project in Tanzania.

1.5      Objectives of CESA

Preparation of the CESA is one of the activities under the Clean up and Disposal
component of the ASP project in the United Republic of Tanzania. In respect to the ASP,
the CESA aims to:

      Provide data on the inter-relationship between the locations where pesticides are
      stored and the broader environment in a country;
      Prioritise storage locations based on the risk posed to public health and the
      environment;
      Allow the country to make decisions on the strategy to be adopted for the
      safeguarding of obsolete pesticides (repackaging, transport and storage);
      Allow the country to select the most appropriate disposal strategy for the waste
      identified at the inventory stage based on an assessment of national capacity to treat
      wastes in an environmentally sound manner;
      Develop EMPs for all category of sites (high risk, medium and low risk) which will
      be incorporated into the technical specifications for the tender of the disposal
      contractor, and will guide the development of detailed and operational health and
      safety plans.




                                              3
      Factor in any social issues as determined during the assessment of the inventory data
      and identify any locations where resettlement of inhabitants may be required
      (temporary or long term).

1.6      Methodology

1.6.1 Data Collection and Consultations – Stage I

The environmental and social assessment was carried out using a combination of methods
including literature review, desk research, country project documents, extensive
consultations with NEMC and other stakeholders, and from information to be collected
during the fieldwork.

Analysis was done using a "process approach" and "activity-impact interaction matrices"
to identify, prioritize, and develop mitigation plans. The data collected from the process
was analyzed using a structured approach. This approach is based on the development of
a simple life cycle analysis of pesticides within Tanzania and results in the identification
of the main activities (and associated sub-activities) in the pesticides life cycle.

Work Plan

The following activities were undertaken in developing CESA:
       Baseline Survey – Analysis of the Detailed Inventory. This is a multi-disciplinary
       site survey conducted prior to or in the initial stage of a joint operational
       deployment. The survey included the following:
             (a) A summary of the previous waste management and pesticide disposal
                  initiatives
             (b) A statement on the causes of accumulation as identified by previous
                  studies
             (c) All PSMS data related to the pesticide stores in the country, including
                  the plot of Environmental and Pesticide Risk Factors.
             (d) A preliminary register of all contaminated sites
             (e) An assessment of national management capacity to complete a national
                  safeguarding operation and
             (f) An assessment of current disposal capacity in the country to determine
                  the potential for local treatment of the wastes as identified at
                  inventory.

         Review of Tanzania’s Policies, Legal and Institutional Framework – Area of
         focus included:
               (a) National waste management legislation,
               (b) National transport of hazardous goods regulations (includes repackaging
                    for transportation, loading/unloading trucks, trucking to storage or
                    disposal facility, and possible accidents)
               (d) Environmental Assessment/ Impact Regulations
               (e) Permitting of works, etc


                                              4
       Visit of selected sites – the sites visited include: Vingunguti, Korogwe, TPRI,
       Mbarali, Store A, B and C in Morogoro
       Application of FAO Environmental Management Toolkit (EMTK) i.e. Volume 1
       that is based on the concept of environmental risk assessment and Volume 2
       provided support in the areas of pesticide storage and transport to guide the
       Process of Identifying Risks and Prioritizing Sites
       Identification of Priority Sites: Sites were sorted by Risk Category (High,
       Medium and Low) and representative sites were selected from each group
       Environmental sampling (related to soil and water contamination) and other
       (testing where necessary) at high risk sites was carried out.
       Public Consultation and Education included the following:
              (a) Development and implementation of a comprehensive and integrated
                    public awareness, education and consultations programme to support
                    implementation of the ASP at the national and local levels, as well as
                    efforts aimed at prevention (reducing the stream of obsolete
                    pesticides); and
              (b) Building longer term capacity in the country for engaging citizens and
                    communities in addressing national and local environmental
                    challenges. Public consultation was an integral and an important part
                    during the preparation of CESA
       Development of Prevention Plans – That precluded future stockpiles of obsolete
       pesticides
       Review and Implementation of Country Clean Up - Cleanup and disposal
       activities is based on a detailed inventory of pesticide stockpiles and contaminated
       sites, determining the identity of the contaminant, its quantity and proximity to
       people
       Involuntary Resettlement Plans – if Triggered. Project implementation may call
       for physical relocation of people from contaminated sites. Adequate mitigation
       measures should be planned
       Analysis of Alternatives - Alternatives disposal options were explored. Several
       options are open to the project for the disposal of obsolete pesticides and pesticide
       wastes. During this process, the findings presented in the World Bank report on
       the Disposal Technology Options Study (2008) which provides information about
       the treatment and disposal of obsolete pesticides and associated wastes for
       countries and project partners in the Africa Stockpiles Programme (ASP) were
       used.

1.6.2 Development of Environmental Management Plan (EMP) – Stage 2

Overall Design of the EMP for ASP Tanzania Project

The Environmental Management Plan (EMP) describes the actions (including mitigation
measures, cost, and institutional arrangements) that must be taken in order to ensure that
ASP Tanzania Project implementation is undertaken in an environmentally sound manner
and without adverse socio- economic impacts. The structure of the EMP is linked to the
"staging" of project activities and is driven by the following three issues:


                                            5
       The importance of ensuring that the "inventory activity" is conducted in a manner
       that minimizes risks to the environment, to inventory workers and to local
       communities;
       The importance of successful completion of Stage 1 activities as a precursor to
       starting Stage 2 within the country; and
       The need to conduct a country project environmental and social assessment -
       CESA

The EMP includes formulation of the mitigation measures for each risk category sites and
estimation of the associated costs for the selected measures. This will also include
establishing performance indicators for monitoring the implementation of the mitigation
measures.

The EMP is considered to be a tool that will allow Tanzania country project teams to
identify the risks associated with their proposed safeguarding and disposal strategy and
present the mitigation measures which will be adopted to minimize the potential impact
of those risks on human health and the environment.

The EMP provide a basis for the development of the detailed and site specific operational
health and safety plans. The health and safety plans are very detailed operational work
plans that will be developed for all high risk sites by the disposal contractor (and the
format will be provided in the tender document), and at the generic level for all other
sites by the country teams once the CESA/EMP has been completed. The plan will
explain how the operations must be completed, will incorporate all the mitigations
measures included in the EMP, and describe how these mitigation measures will actually
be implemented in the field.




                                           6
                                   CHAPTER TWO

2.0 COUNTRY SETTING

The country setting covers six main items 1) the physical aspects, in terms of location,
size, climate, relief, and hydrology, 2) the biological aspects of the country in terms of
the flora and fauna, 3) the socio economic aspects of the country including the
population, settlement distribution and general administration of the country, and 4) the
road networks in the country, port facilities and main hospital facilities.

2.1    Location and climate

Tanzania has about 945,000 square kilometers and lies approximately between latitudes
1o and 12o south and longitudes 29o and 40o east. The climate of Tanzania is influenced
by two main factors, of location in terms of latitude and position at the eastern edge of the
African continent. Annual rainfall in Tanzania ranges from 400mm per annum mainly in
the central parts of the country to 2300mm in the highlands and Western parts of Lake
Victoria (Figure 2.1). Precipitation is not uniform across but is marked by spatial, annual
and inter-annual variability. In terms of spatial variability the Regions found in central
parts and northern areas of the country receive the lowest amount of rainfall of between
400mm to 900mm per annum. Regions located in the Coastal and Western parts of the
country receive medium amounts of rainfall, ranging from 900mm to 1200mm per
annum. The Regions that get the highest amount of rainfall include Morogoro, Iringa,
Mbeya and parts of Kilimanjaro, Kagera and Rukwa with the average rainfall exceeding
1200mm per year. In terms of temperature country’s mean annual ranges from 21oC in
the highland areas to 29oC at the sea level. The year-to-year variability is very small and
departures from the long-term means generally insignificant. In all parts of country July
is the coolest month of the year. The warmest month shows more variation. It can be
either before or after the main rainy period. When it is before the rain period then
October or November has the highest mean temperature. This condition occurs over most
of southern Tanzania. In the second case when it is after the rains the maximum
temperatures occur during February or March and this condition prevails along the east
coast and in the north-west of the country. There are many deviations from these general
trends, but the differences between monthly mean temperatures at the same area are
usually very small. In only a very few areas December and January are the hottest months
of the year.




                                             7
Figure 2.1: Long-term (1960 to 1990) annual rainfall distribution

In Tanzania, wind speed and direction vary strongly with local conditions. Generally the
wind velocity is highest during the monsoon seasons and lowest in the intermediate
periods, but there are many exceptions to this rule. From December to March air masses
move from high to low-pressure areas, and eastern Africa is therefore under the influence
of a northeasterly air-stream during this period. This is called the northeast monsoon or
“Kaskazi?. From about June to September, the eastern parts of Africa are under the
influence of a strong air stream from the south-east, the south-east monsoon or “Kusi?. It
carries air from a large high pressure area over South Africa and adjacent parts of the
Indian Ocean, where the winter brings relatively low temperatures, to a very strong low
over Saudi Arabia, which is related to the high summer temperature there.




                                            8
2.2    Relief

The country is a land of vast plains, plateaus and mountains. Except for a narrow belt
along the coast, most of country land lies above 300m above sea level with much of the
country at an altitude higher than 1,000m above sea level (Figure 2.2). The coastal
lowland areas, rising to 100m extends inland for a very short distance except between the
mouths of Ruvu and Rufiji rivers. In the somewhat broadened coastal belt between those
limits lie the Pugu hills, rising to 300m. Alluvial lowlands are found along rivers draining
into the Indian ocean, particularly Wami, Ruvu, Rufiji and the latter’s tributary, the
Kilombero. Except for the river deltas, the coastline of Tanzania is characterized by coral
growth. The low coastal cliffs are cut out of old coral reefs once under the sea, while
offshore growing coral reefs are common.

The hinterland of Tanzania is marked by plateaus, plains and mountains, rising above
1000m above sea level. There are three major plateau areas. The southeast plateau
extends from west of Songea to east of Newala, and northwards to the Great Ruaha
(Ruaha Mkuu). River valleys cut the Handeni-Masai plateau in its eastern part. The
Masai steppe is rolling countryside with occasional inselbergs, bare rocking hills or
groupings of very large boulders. The central Plateau, covering over one third of the
country, lies between the two rift valleys. It is more than 1,000m in altitude, covered for
the most part with miombo woodland and broken here and there by inselbergs. The
Serengeti Plain rises to more than 1,500m.

A distinctive feature of Tanzania is the rift valley, whose form is marked in many places
by long, narrow and deep depressions, often filled with lakes. The Great Rift Valley runs
from near the mouth of the Zambezi River northward through Tanzania, Kenya, and
Ethiopia and across the Red sea to Israel. Where it approaches Tanzania from the south,
at the northern end of Lake Nyasa, it divides into western and eastern rifts. Lake
Tanganyika lies in the western rift, which continues northwards through Lake Kivu in the
Democratic Republic of Congo. Lake Rukwa depression marks a subsidiary rift to the
western one. The eastern rift is largely undistinguishable until Saranda between Dodoma
and Manyoni, but further north it is marked by Lake Manyara, whose western shore is a
steep scarp, and by Lake Natron. Lake Eyasi lies in a subsidiary fissure of the eastern rift.




                                             9
Figure 2.2: Tanzania Physical features

2.3    Hydrology

The hydrological aspects cover the major river systems, lakes and wetlands and ground
water. Figure 2.3 shows some of the major river systems found in the country. It includes
Rufiji, Pangani, Malagarasi, Ruvu, Wami, Ruhuhu, Kiwira, Kagera, Mara, and
Kilombero River. The surface river flow regime and moisture conditions in the country
correspond to the general rainfall pattern.          Rivers and lakes start rising in
November/December and experience a maximum in March – April, with a recession
period from May to October - November. Generally, the hydrological year starts in
October/November and ends in September/October. In the dry northern and central parts
with annual rainfall between 500 – 800 mm, streams are seasonal, and the mean annual
specific runoff is generally below 2 litres per second per square kilometer (l/s/km2). On


                                           10
the highlands, like the south – western highlands, the Uluguru, Ukaguru and Usambara
mountain ranges, and on the slopes of Mountains Kilimanjaro and Meru, as well as in
most of the western parts of the country, where annual rainfall is in the range of 1200 –
1600mm, streams and rivers are perennial.




Figure 2.3: Tanzania hydrological networks




                                           11
About 50% of the country’s surface runoff flows directly into the Indian Ocean from the
major river systems of Pangani, Umba, Sigi, Msangazi, Wami, Ruvu, Rufiji, Mbwenkuru,
Matandu and Ruvuma. The remaining 50% of the country’s runoff drains northwards
into Lake Victoria and then through the Nile River to the Mediterranean; westwards into
Lake Tanganyika and then through the Congo River system to the Atlantic Ocean;
southwards into Lake Nyasa and through the Zambezi river system to the Indian Ocean;
and into internal drainage basins. Based on the drainage pattern the country is divided
into nine drainage basins for sound and sustainable management of water resources. They
are as follows: Pangani, Wami/Ruvu, Rufiji, Ruvuma and southern coast, Lake Nyasa,
internal drainage, Lake Rukwa, Lake Tanganyika, and Lake Victoria. Figure 2.4 shows
the nine water basins of the country.




Figure 2.4: Tanzania Drainage Basins


2.4    Lakes, Oceans and Wetlands

Tanzania has seven major lakes including Lake Victoria, Eyasi, Nyasa, Manyara, Natron,
Rukwa and Tanganyika. Lake Victoria is the largest Lake in Africa; largest tropical lake
in the World and the second largest fresh water Lake in the World in terms of surface
area. Lake Victoria is relatively a shallow lake with a mean depth of 40m, and a
catchment area of 184,000 sq kms. The Lake is a source of the White Nile and is shared
by three countries of Tanzania, Kenya and Uganda. Lake Eyasi is located south of the
Serengeti National Park on the floor of the East African Rift Valley at an elevation of


                                          12
1,040m. It is a shallow lake with a surface area of 1050 sq kms. Lake Manyara, which
has alkaline water, is located in the Lake Manyara National Park and it covers about 89
sq miles. The Lake is famous for its abundances of different types of wildlife including
baboons, hippos, elephants, wildbeast, flamingos and many more. Lake Natron is located
in northern Tanzania, close to Kenya border within the rift valley system. Its water is
saline due to high evaporation rates during the dry season. Depending on the temperature
and rainfall, salinity can reach a pH of 9 to 10.5. The Lake is the only breeding location
for lesser flamingos because the high salinity values acts as barrier against the predators.
Due to its uniqueness, Lake Natron is on the Ramsar list of wetlands of international
importance. Lake Rukwa is found in the southwestern part of the country in the rift valley
zone between Lake Tanganyika and Nyasa at an elevation of about 800 metres. It is an
alkaline Lake with surface area of 5760 sq kms. The lake is famous for its crocodiles and
fishing activities. Lake Tanganyika is located in the western part of the country bordering
Burundi, Zambia and Democratic Republic of Congo. It is believed to be the second
largest freshwater lake in the world by volume and also the second deepest with
maximum depth of 1470 metres. Lake Tanganyika discharges its water to the Atlantic
Ocean through the Congo River system.

2.5     Wetlands

The wetlands in Tanzania can be classified into three main types of Coastal wetlands,
Rift system and Highland drainage basins. Table 2.1 and Figure 2.5 show the main
wetlands of Tanzania including Moyowosi swamps and floodplain, Kilombero flood
plain, Usangu, Wembere and Ruvu wetlands. Coastal wetlands are formed by wave
action along the shoreline and, it include mud flats, marshes, mangrove swamps, estuaries
and deltas. Rift system wetlands are found in rift depressions and are characterized by
salt lakes with inland drainage. Wetland of highlands and drainage basins are found in
the long rivers originating in the highlands that meander through plains forming lakes,
swamps and flood plains. It is estimated that about 5061sq km or 5.5% of Tanzania is
covered by different types of lakes.

2.6     Groundwater

Groundwater availability, mainly controlled by the geology of the country, is variable.
About 75% of the country is underlain by Precambrian Basement complex, which
comprises of hard, consolidated and sometimes metamorphosed rocks. These rocks form
basement aquifers, where they are weathered, fractured or faulted. Other types are Karroo
and younger aquifers, coastal sedimentary formation of limestone and sandstone, and the
alluvial sedimentary sequence which mostly include clay, silt, sand and gravel and
volcanic material found in alluvial plains. Volcanic areas of northern Tanzania as well as
the sedimentary coastal basins are potential groundwater resource areas. Groundwater is a
major supplement for surface water for many parts in the country and is a vital source of
water in the semi-arid areas where surface water is scarce. The Pangani basin has a high
groundwater potential as compared to the other basins. It is in this basin where significant
amounts of groundwater are extracted for irrigation (High Yield boreholes giving more
than 100 m3 per hour have been drilled in Kahe flats). In this basin groundwater recharge
is mainly from rainfall and rivers. The nature of geologic formations existing in basin i.e.
fault zones, contact zones and coastal sediments influence groundwater recharge. In
Wami/Ruvu basin the aquifers are of sand, limestone, and partially fractured granite. The
groundwater potential in the basin is estimated to be high. Wells drilled in the basin can


                                             14
yield up to as high as 28 m3/hour. Geological formations within the basin favor fast
groundwater recharge from rainfall, surface and groundwater. Recharge in the basin is
generally from rainfall. Sand formations, which occupy a large part of the basin, are
porous, thus accelerating the rates of infiltration and percolation respectively. Apart from
the sand formations fault zones also act as media for the basin’s groundwater recharge.
Interpretation of resistivity soundings show that saline water is below 70m.




Figure 2.5: Major wetlands of Tanzania




                                            15
The Rufiji basin is the second with highest groundwater potential. The basin is underlain
by Karoo, especially in Tunduru area, formations having high groundwater potential.
There are also numerous faults in the area covered by rocks of the basement system.
Groundwater recharge in the basin is mainly from rainfall and existing water bodies
including rivers, ponds and man-made reservoirs. In the basin, groundwater recharge is
high in the sedimentary deposits of the coastal zone while in the less permeable rocks the
recharge is in the faulted and fissured zones. The Internal Basin has a high groundwater
potential and contributes significantly to the livelihood of the people living in the basin
because mostly they are dependent on this water resources. Groundwater recharge is
mainly from rainfall and rivers. The recharge areas are the major fault lines which
surround Makutupora, and the porous volcanic formations.

The groundwater potential in Rukwa Basin has a high potential as evidenced by the high
success of wells drilled in the basin. Groundwater recharge is mainly from rainfall, rivers
and other existing water bodies. It is high in recent deposits of sand and clay. The
recharge is also high in faulted and fissured zones within the Dodoma and Ufipa gneisses.
However, the rate of recharge is unknown as no studies have been carried out. In the
Lake Tanganyika basin the groundwater potential is also high in parts of Rukwa region in
the areas bordering Lake Tanganyika and parts part of Tabora Region. This is evidenced
by the success of wells drilled in the basin. However, shallow groundwater potential in
the basin is not very high. Groundwater recharge in the basin is generally from rainfall
and existing water bodies including rivers, ponds and lakes. In the Lake Victoria basin
high groundwater potentials is found in Musoma, Bunda, Ngara, Magu and part of
Bukoba Districts. In the basin, groundwater recharge is mainly from rainfall, rivers and
lakes. The main recharge areas are lake deposits around the lake, which include Bukoba
sandstone and limestone deposits and main rift lines. However, the pattern and annual
recharge rate is not yet established.

2.7     Flora and Fauna

Tanzania is a rich country in terms of vegetation and wildlife abundance. Based on
Africover land cover map of 2002, the main vegetation types in the country includes,
forest, woodlands, bushland and grasslands. The wildlife types in Tanzania include both
the large and small mammals found in the plains, the amphibious wildlife in the water
bodies including the lakes, rivers and wetlands and birds mostly in the wetlands.

2.7.1   Main vegetation types

The vegetation types found in Tanzania include forests, woodlands, bushlands and
grassland. Figure 2.6 shows the major vegetation types of Tanzania. Forests cover
47617 sq km or 5% of the country, woodlands 313871 sq km or 33%, bushland 234983
or 25%, grassland 151128 or 16%, mangrove forest 1512 or 0.16% and agriculture
128171 or 14%. Out of this area, 13000 million ha have been gazetted as forest reserves
while about 1.6 million ha of the gazetted forest reserves are under water catchments
management. Table 2.2 shows the main cover types in Tanzania.

2.8     Wildlife resources

Tanzania has a rich and diverse spectrum of fauna including a wide variety of endemic
species and sub-species. The most common game species, includes buffalo, wildebeest,
hippo, elephant, zebra, impala, bushbuck and reedbuck, and predators such as the leopard
and lion are widely distributed throughout the country. The diversity and degree of
endemism in the country is more pronounced for primates (20 species where 4 are
endemic), antelopes (34 species where 2 are endemic), fish (many species endemic in
Lake Victoria, Tanganyika and Nyasa and other small lakes and rivers), reptiles (290
species where 75 are endemic), amphibians (numerous where 40 are endemic),
invertebrates and plants (around 11,000 species including many that are endemic).
Besides, the diversity and high degree of endemism, Tanzania possesses important
populations of species that are threatened, but are also widespread across Africa. These
include endangered wild dog, black rhinoceros and slender – snorted crocodile,
chimpanzee, cheetah and African elephant.

2.8.1   Wildlife protected areas

Tanzania boasts of an extensive wildlife protected area network, covering about 28% of
the total land area. Out of this, 4% fall under the category of National Parks (NPs), 15%
Game Reserves (GRs), 1% the Ngorongoro Conservation Area (NCA), and 8% Game
Controlled Areas (GCAs). About 3% of protected areas devoted to wildlife conservation
overlap with forest reserves. Also, significant numbers of wildlife abounds in many
unsettled areas popularly known as “open areas? as well as in village lands. Some of
these areas constitute important migratory routes, wildlife corridors and dispersal areas.
Currently, there are 14 national parks, all together covering an area of about 141,090 sq.
km and two new ones are still in the establishment process; 33 game reserves with a total
area of approximately 121,152 sq. km; 43 game controlled areas covering more that
63,815 sq. km and the Ngorongoro Conservation Area with 8,292 sq. km (See Figure
2.7).




                                           17
Figure 2.6: Tanzania land cover map for year 2002




                                         18
Figure 2.7: Protected areas in Tanzania
Source: Wildlife Division 2004




                                          19
The following is a more detailed explanation of the major protected areas:

Arusha National Park

Arusha national Park covers an area of about 137 km2. It is located near Arusha town
between the peaks of Mountains Kilimanjaro and Meru. The park and town derive their
name from the Wa-Arusha people who traditionally lived in this area. The Ngurdoto
Crater, Momela Lakes, the highland montane forest, and the rugged mount Meru (about
4,575 m above sea level) are the four distinctive features of the park. The park has a
wealth of wildlife including colobus monkeys, velver monkeys, bushbacks, buffaloes, red
forest duikers, hippos, elephants and giraffes. Bird life especially waterfowl is abundant
and interesting.

Gombe National Park

It is situated 16 km, north of Kigoma town in western Tanzania. Gombe National park
occupies 52 km2 of land. The beauty of Gombe National Park is unique; it is a park
without roads, where you can walk and experience nature with all your senses. Due to its
altitude, the park’s vegetation varies from evergreen forests of tall trees to open
woodlands and grasslands. Common mammals found in the park are forest species
mostly primates. These include chimpanzees, baboons, blue monkeys, red tailed
monkeys and red colobus monkeys. In a sense, the park is indeed an island of wilderness
surrounded on three sides by cultivation and on the north by the Lake Tanganyika.

Katavi National Park

Covering an area of 2253 km2 Katavi National Park is located in Mpanda Districts,
Rukwa Region about 40 km south east of Mpanda town. The park is famous for its
undisturbed natural face compared to other parks in the country. Major features of
attraction include Lake Katavi with its vast short grass flood plans in the north, palm
fringed Lake Chada in the Southeast and Katuma River. Animals found in the park
include zebras, sable and roan antelopes, elands, leopard, elephants, buffalos and lions.
Waterfowls also abound.

Kilimanjaro National Park

With an area of 756 km2, Kilimanjaro National Park exists to preserve Mount
Kilimanjaro’s outstanding scenic features and its flora and fauna. At an altitude of 5895
m above mean sea level, Mount Kilimanjaro is the highest mountain in Africa, the
highest freestanding mountain in the world and one of the largest volcanoes ever to burst
through the earth’s crust. Attractive features include one extinct volcano, Shira (3962m)
and two dormant volcanoes peak, Mawenzi (5149 m) and Kibo (5895 m).

Also, significant numbers of mammals, birds and insects inhabit the area. Mammals
include elephants, buffalos, elands, duikers, leopards, baboons, blue monkeys and black
and white colobus monkeys. The park is also frequented with colorful birds.



                                           20
Lake Manyara National Park

It is located 125 km west of Arusha town, under the wall of the Great Rift Valley. The
park is about 330 km2 in size of which Lake Manyara covers 230 km2. It contains a
large variety of habitats such as the rift wall, the ground water forest, Acacia woodland,
area of open grassland, to mention but a few thus being able to support large number of
species. The most famous spectacle in the park is the tree-climbing lions, which are
occasionally seen along branches of Acacia trees. Other animals found in this area
include elephants, leopard, impala, hippos as well as millions of birds (pelicans and
flamingos). Lake Manyara national Park has the highest population of elephants per
square km in the country.

Ngorongoro Crater

This remarkable and world famous geographic feature is a “caldera? – a collapsed
volcano. There are several prides of lion, and some of the last surviving rhino to be
found in Tanzania. The alkaline soda lake is home to huge flocks of flamingos, a
remarkable and unforgettable sight. The whole crater has a quite unique almost magical
atmosphere. Also there is a place called Olduvai Gorge found in Ngorongoro crater,
which is a historical site considered as a seat of humanity after the discovery of the
earliest specimen of the human genus, Homo habilis.

Tarangire National Park

Covering approximately 2600 km2, Tarangire National park is second, only to the
Ngorongoro Crater, in the concentrations of wildlife to be seen in the dry season. It lies
to the south of large open grass plains of southern Maasai land, 115 km from Arusha
along the Arusha-Dodoma road. The features of Tarangire which correlate with the type
of animals residing in the area are grassland and flood plains. Tarangire is commonly
famous for its tree-climbing pythons, zebras, hartebeasts, elephants, buffalos, waterbucks,
gazelles, oryx and abundant birdlife.

Ruaha National Park

Covering an area of 10,300 km2, Ruaha National Park is the second largest park in
Tanzania. Ruaha national Park lies in the central part of Tanzania just west of Iringa
highlands. It is also the second largest elephant sanctuary in the country, next to Selous
Game Reserve. The game animals found here are herds of elephant and buffalo. The
exciting Great Ruaha River features are larger number of hippos and crocodiles. During
the dry season the river attracts great number of game: giraffe, warthog and eland. In the
plains, ostrich, cheetah and Grant’s gazelle can be seen. The park offers a paradise of the
outstanding natural world as it lies within a belt which interfaces species of Fauna and
Flora of southern and northern Africa.

Serengeti Nation Park

The Serengeti national Park with 14763 km2 is one of the largest wildlife sanctuaries in
the world and it is the site of one of the most breathtaking events in the animal kingdom,

                                            21
the migration of thousands of wildebeest. It was accepted by the World heritage
Convention as a World heritage Site and Biosphere Reserve in 1981. It is about 329 km
from Arusha town. The national Park has large number of species of plain animals, from
a hare to the elephant and a wonderful selection of birdlife.

Mikumi National Park

The park occupies an area of about 3237 km2. It is found in Mkata river plain bordered by
the Ukiruguru mountain range to the east and Rubebo Mountains to the west. The most
attractive wild life animals include buffaloes, elephants, giraffe, zebra, wildebeests and
wild dogs to mention a few. The park is passed by the main road, the Dar es Salaam
Tunduma. Therefore, a great precaution has to be taken during the transportation of
stocks from the Southern highlands zone to Dar es Salaam port which will be used as an
exit point.

Selous National Reserve

This reserve is one of the most remote and least visited game parks in Africa. It occupies
an area of about 15,000 square miles and it is the largest world game reserve. Interesting
uniqueness of the game is that it can be explored by boat sailing through swamps and
lagoons. It has the world’s largest big games which include elephants, buffaloes,
rhinoceros and it contains African’s concentration of hippopotamus, crocodiles and wild
dogs.


2.9    Social Economic Aspects

2.9.1 Population

According to the 2002 census the country’s total population stood at 33.5 million (URT,
2003). The population of the country has nearly trebled in the four decades, growing at an
annual average growth of 3.22% between 1967 and 1978; at the rate of 2.8% between
1978 and 1988 and 2.9% between 1988 and 2002 (URT, 1967, 1978, 1988 and 2002).
The high growth rates of population in Tanzania is brought about by high fertility, and
declining mortality levels (URT, 2005). The decline in mortality levels is due to
improvement in health, living conditions and nutritional conditions in both rural and
urban areas. According to the 2002 Population and Housing Census, life expectancy at
birth for Tanzanians is 51 years. The population of Tanzania has continued to be
predominantly rural even though the proportion of urban population has been increasing
over time. The proportion of urban population was just 6% in 1967 compared to 18% in
1988 and 23% in 2002. Increasing rural to urban migration contributes significantly to the
growth of urban population in the country.

Just as there has been an increase in population, a similar trend has been observed in
increase in population density. The high increase is observed in areas with better
livelihood opportunities especially in fertile land for agricultural purposes and mining
activities. Also high population increase is found in areas with high employment
opportunities especially urban areas. In 1967 the average density was 14 persons per

                                           22
square kilometer. However, by 2002 this figure had increased to 39 persons per square
kilometer (Figure 2.8). Dar es Salaam has had the highest increase in population density,
in 1967 it was 256 peoples per square kilometer and by 2002 it had an average of 1793.
Population densities in the three municipalities of Dar es Saam have increased more than
four fold between 1967 and 2002.


2.9.2 Settlement distribution

The present settlement pattern of Tanzania is distinctively marked by the two categories
of urban and rural settlements (see Figure 2.9). The urban centers have been increasing
over time. At the time of independence there were only 8 regional towns (urban centres)
while in 1967 already there were 21 settlements classified as urban centres
accommodating 5% of the total national population. In the year 1978, this number had
grown to 52 and further to 286 urban centres in 1988 accommodating approximately 13%
and 17% of the total Country’s population respectively. In 2002, there were a total of 205
urban centres accommodating 23% of the Total Population (NHP, 2002; URT 2002). In
2005, the government promoted Mwanza, Tanga and Mbeya from Municipality to City
status making a total of 4 cities together with Dar es Salaam City. Arusha Municipality
was recently promoted to a city status, making a total of 5 cities in the country.

The overall hierarchy of settlements in Tanzania is therefore depicted by 5 cities (Dar es
Salaam, Mwanza, Mbeya and Tanga and Arusha), 13 municipalities (Dodoma, Arusha,
Moshi, Morogoro, Mtwara Mikindani, Songea, Iringa, Singida, Tabora, Shinyanga,
Bukoba, Kigoma and Musoma), 9 towns (such as, Njombe, Korogwe, etc), 86 Townships
(Kulaba and Mkai, 2004). Until 2006, reports from the Ministry of Local Government
showed that there were 10,334 registered Villages. The pattern of these settlements
follows the general pattern of population distribution country wide.




                                           23
Figure 2.8: Tanzania population density by Region (2002)




                                         24
Figure 2.9: Settlements distribution in Tanzania




                                           25
2.9.3 Road Infrastructures

The total road network in Tanzania (Figure 2.10) covers about 85,000 km, of which trunk
roads take 12%; regional roads 24%; district roads 24%; urban roads 1%, and
unclassified roads take 35% (URT, 2006), whereas 40% of the total road network is
bituminized. Trunk roads are the national and international through routes, which link
several regions and provide access to important border posts. The Trunk roads network is
divided into nine principal corridors, including the main roads in Dar es Salaam City. The
nine trunk roads corridors are briefly explained below.

   1) The TANZAM Corridor which extends from Dar es Salaam – Morogoro –
      Mikumi (with a link of Ifakara to Mahenge) – Iringa – Mafinga (with a link to
      Mgololo) – Makambako – Mbeya (with a link to Itungi Port and Malawi border) –
      toTunduma (Total distance 1,328.5 km).
   2) The North – East Corridor which extends from Dar es Salaam - Chalinze – Segera
      (with a link to Tanga and Horohoro) – Same (with a link to Gonja – Mkomazi) –
      Himo (with a link to Holili and Marangu – Tarakea) –Moshi – Arusha to
      Namanga (Total distance 950km).
   3) Southern coastal corridor, which extends from Dar es Salaam – Lindi – Mtwara
      (566km).
   4) Central corridor from Morogoro on TANZAM – Dodoma – Mwanza – Rusumo
      and Kobero (Burundi border) in the west (1584km).
   5) Lake circuit from Sirari on Kenyan border to Musoma – Mwanza – Bukoba and
      Mutukula on the border with Uganda (1019km).
   6) Southern corridor (1326km) linking Lindi – Mtwara – Songea to Makambako on
      the TANZAM and to Mbambabay on Lake Nyasa.
   7) Great North corridor (1024km) that extends from Iringa on the TANZAM
      Corridor to Arusha - Namanga.
   8) Western corridor (1286km) from Tunduma on the TANZAM Corridor to
      Sumbawanga – Mpanda – Uvinza – Kigoma – Nyakanazi and Nyakahura on the
      Lake Circuit Corridor.
   9) Mid-west corridor extending from the Central corridor in the east to the
      TANZAM corridor in the south west.

Analysis on the conditions of trunk and regional roads in Tanzania shows that 51% of the
roads are in good condition, 33% in fair condition and 11% are in poor condition. Table
2.3 shows the road conditions for roads in Tanzania.

2.9.4 Ports and marine transport

Tanzania has three main seaports namely Dar es Salaam, Mtwara and Tanga, while
smaller seaport facilities exist at Kilwa, Lindi, Mafia, Pangani and Bagamoyo. Among
the three main seaports, Dar es Salaam Port has the highest capacity that enables the port
to handle comparatively larger quantities of freight. In the period 2001 – 2005 a number
of countries had their traffic (import and export) pass through Dar es Salaam Port,
including Zambia, DRC, Burundi, Rwanda, Malawi, Uganda, as well as Tanzania itself.
Principal commodities handled at Dar es Salaam Port in that period include bulk oils,
molasses, Copper (for Zambia and DRC), Tin and Zinc plates as well as other mining and
quarry products. Other commodities were oil seed cake, coffee, cotton and cashew nuts,
maize meal and flour, rice and rice meal, hides and skin, sisal, sugar, tea, tobacco and
other general cargo.




                                              27
Figure 2.10: Roads, railways and airports of Tanzania




                                          28
2.10   Hospital Facilities

Tanzania has about 4,961 health facilities that are owned by both public and private
sector. Out of these the public sector has 409 health centers and 2450 dispensaries. The
health centers and dispensaries are linked to 74 district and regional hospitals and four
specialized teaching hospitals. There are about 65,000 health workers engaged in health
care and delivery. 70% of the health workers belong to the public sector. Table 2.4
shows the type and number of the health facilities in the country.

Table 2.4: Types of health facilities in the country
 S/N       Type of facility                          Number
 1         Consultancy/specialized hospital                 8
 2         Regional hospitals                              17
 3         District Hospitals                              55
 4         Other hospitals                                 84
 5         Health center                                  479
 6         Dispensaries                                  3975
 7         Private laboratories                           223
 8         Nursing homes                                    6
 9         Private X ray units                             27
           Total                                        4,961

The total number of patients who attended the hospitals, dispensaries and health centres
in the country grew from 7,687,329 in 1995 to 16,561,435 in 2000. During the same
period the number of people per hospital decreased slightly from 178,000 to 168,000
indicating that there has been a slight increase in the number hospitals as compared to
population growth (URT, 2004).

In the current situation there is no specialized health centre or hospital to deal with
pesticide poisoning. However, as described above there are hospitals in each district and
regional centers which have a capacity to deal with general health problems including
cases related to pesticides poisoning. Therefore it can be said that in any case of pesticide
poisoning during safeguarding operation in Tanzania it will be dealt with or attended
accordingly.

Tanzania like other countries is prone to a number of natural and human made disasters.
Consequently, a system is in place for either reducing the risks for the occurrence of
disaster or responding to disaster when they occur. At the national level the Disaster
Management Department (DMD) in the Prime Ministers office is responsible for the
overall management of the disasters in the country. At the regional, district and village
levels disaster committees have been set to respond to disasters. Similarly, emergency
units in the Ministry of Home Affairs and Emergency and Response Section in the
Ministry of Health and Social Welfare are in place. Therefore based on the facilities
available and the institution setup for emergency response in the country it can be
concluded that the health sector in collaboration with other government institutions can
cope with disaster related to pesticide accident.
