3.3    World Bank’s Environmental and Social Safeguard Requirements

The World Bank’s Operational Directive 4.01 on Environmental Assessment (now
referred to as Operational Policy and Bank Procedure 4.01) requires that environmental
assessments be undertaken in those categories of projects that have or are likely to have
potentially significant impacts on the environment. Besides identifying the general policy
of the World Bank about the EIA, this document lays down the procedures and guidelines
for conducting EIA of the projects including the aspects to be considered in EIA, public
consultation and information disclosure procedures. It also provides environmental
screening guidelines for classifying the projects into various categories (A, B, C and FI).

Potential adverse environmental impacts of the proposed project are principally related to
pesticide storage, handling, transportation, disposal and site remediation. Because of its
significance, the project is classified as Environmental Category A. A full
(comprehensive) EIA is normally required for all projects falling under Environmental
Category A since such projects may have significant adverse impacts that may be
sensitive, irreversible and diverse. These are mainly new construction projects. The
project triggers two of the World Bank’s safeguard policies; OP 4.01 on Environmental
Assessments, which requires the preparation of a CESA, and OP 4.09 on Pest
Management which require a Pest Management Plan (PMP). Since the project itself
meets the requirements of a PMP, it was not necessary to prepare a self-standing
document. The applicability of two additional policies OP 4.12 on Involuntary
Resettlement and OP 4.04 on Natural Habitats have been evaluated on a case by case
basis, as it is conceivable that project implementation may affect established access rights
of people to contaminated sites, or include activities that represent a significant risk on
natural habitats.

For all Category A and B projects proposed for financing, during the EA process, the
borrower is required to consult project-affected groups and local nongovernmental
organizations (NGOs) about the project's environmental aspects and take their views into
account. For Category A projects, the borrower must consult these groups at least twice:



                                            52
(a) shortly after environmental screening and before the terms of reference for the EA are
finalized; and (b) once a draft EA report is prepared. In addition, the borrower shall
consult with such groups throughout project implementation as necessary to address EA-
related issues that affect them. This CESA has complied with this requirement.

For meaningful consultations between the borrower and project-affected groups and local
NGOs on all Category A and B projects proposed for financing, the borrower is obliged
to provide relevant material in a timely manner prior to consultation and in a form and
language that are understandable and accessible to the groups being consulted. This
project has abided to this requirement through holding consultative meetings in Kiswahili
language with people residing in the vicinity of the OPs storage facilities for registering
their views and concerns pertinent to the implementation of the proposed project.
Consultations have also been done with relevant government ministries, agencies and
departments as well as NGOs for soliciting their views, concerns and aspirations on the
social and environmental impacts of the proposed project. A stakeholders’ workshop was
also held to discuss the CESA report for ensuring that it has addressed all their key social
and environmental issues.

Regarding resettlement, the Bank guidelines prescribe measures to minimize the negative
impacts and ensure that the displaced community benefits from the project. Therefore the
Policy requires that displaced person should be:

      Compensated for their losses at full replacement cost prior to the actual move;
      Assisted with the move and supported during the transition period in the resettlement
      site;
      Assisted in their effort to improve their former living standards, income earning
      capacity, and production levels, or at least restore them.
      Integrated socially and economically into host communities so that adverse impacts
      on host communities are minimized. The best way of achieving this integration is for
      resettlement to be planned through consultation involving affected people and future
      hosts and affected people.

In addition, land, housing, infrastructure, and other compensation should be provided to
the adversely affected population, indigenous groups, ethnic minorities, and pastoralists
who may have customary right to the land and other resources taken for the project. The
Guidelines stipulate that the absence of legal title to land by such groups should not be a
bar to compensation.

3.4      National Strategies/Programmes

The purpose of this sub-section is to determine how environment and pesticide issues are
addressed in various national strategies/programmes.

3.4.1    The National Strategy for Growth and Reduction of Poverty (NSGRP)




                                             53
The NSGRP paper recognizes that sound environmental management is critical for the
attainment of the NSGRP and Millennium Development Goals targets. It also recognises
the role of other sectors in poverty eradication and the need for mainstreaming
environment as one of the crosscutting issues into those sectors.

3.4.2   National Environment Action Plan (NEAP, 1994)

The NEAP provides analysis and guidelines for dealing with environmental management
problems at sectoral level. It also addresses the six key problems outlined in the National
Environmental Policy (1997), which are land degradation; lack of access to good quality
water for both urban and rural inhabitants; environmental pollution; loss of wildlife
habitats and biodiversity; deterioration of aquatic systems; and deforestation. It gives
strategic approach for mainstreaming environmental issues into decision-making process
and the need for defining sector policies and action plans. The proposed project will
therefore contribute in elimination of pollution sources by safeguarding and disposing
obsolete pesticides properly.

3.5     International Conventions

3.5.1   The Basel Convention on the Control of Transboundary Movements of Hazardous
        Waste and their Disposal (1989)

The Basel Convention is a global treaty that grew out of the need to stop the dumping by
the developed countries of hazardous waste in developing countries who were ill-
prepared to deal with its impacts. The Convention covers toxic, poisonous, explosive,
corrosive, flammable, ecotoxic and infectious wastes that are being moved from one
country to another (transboundary movements). Countries, which have ratified the
Convention are obliged to ban the import of hazardous wastes. They should minimise the
production of hazardous wastes and cooperate to ensure that wastes are treated and
disposed of in an environmentally sound manner. The primary objective of the
Convention is to minimise, with the aim of eliminating, the generation and transboundary
movement of hazardous waste. The Convention also aims at preventing illegal trafficking
in waste. The purpose of the Basel Convention is to:

        reduce transboundary movements of hazardous wastes;
        minimise the production of hazardous and toxic wastes;
        ensure that disposal of wastes is done in environmentally sound manner and as
        close to the possible source as possible; and,
        assist developing countries in the environmentally sound management of
        hazardous and other wastes they generate.

By providing access to information and technical assistance on best practice guidelines
and procedures for waste handling, storage and destruction, the Convention promotes the
environmentally sound management and disposal of hazardous waste. The Basel
Convention does not cover radioactive waste or waste discharged from ships. The
Convention entered into force in 1992. In 1995, the Ban Amendment was introduced to



                                            54
strengthen the Basel Convention by prohibiting the export of hazardous waste, for any
reason, from a member state of the OECD to non-OECD countries. In 1999, a protocol on
liability and compensation was adopted.

The Secretariat of the Convention cooperates with national authorities in developing
national legislation, setting up inventories of hazardous wastes, strengthening national
institutions and preparing hazardous waste management plans. Regional Centres for
Training and Technology Transfer have been established to assist developing countries to
gain the skills and tools necessary to properly manage and dispose of their wastes in an
environmentally sound way.

The Convention requires that a competent authority and a Focal Point be identified. The
amount of staff time required to administer the Convention will depend on the volume of
waste being held or transported. As a minimum, a country may need to allocate some
time to customs officials, a Focal Point and possibly a scientist/engineer. Each year,
member countries, are required to avail information on efforts to reduce the amount of
hazardous wastes and information on the generation, export and import of hazardous
wastes covered by the Convention.

The Government of the United Republic of Tanzania ratified the Basel Convention in
1993. Division of Environment in the Vice President’s Office is the Designated National
Authority (DNA) in overseeing the implementation of the provisions of the Basel
Convention in the Country.

The Convention obliges member countries to ensure that disposal of wastes is done in
environmentally sound manner and as close to the possible source as possible. Therefore,
the proposed project is part of and supports the implementation of the Basel Convention.
Should it be absolutely necessary to export the obsolete pesticides for treatment
elsewhere outside the country for treatment because of inadequate capacity to handle the
pollutants locally, provisions of the Basel Convention will strictly be adhered to.

3.5.2   The Rotterdam Convention on the Prior Informed Consent Procedure

The Rotterdam Convention on the Prior Informed Consent Procedure for Certain
Hazardous Chemicals and Pesticides in International Trade, more commonly known
simply as the Rotterdam Convention, is a multilateral treaty to promote shared
responsibilities in relation to importation of hazardous chemicals. The convention
promotes open exchange of information and calls on exporters of hazardous chemicals to
use proper labeling, include directions on safe handling, and inform purchasers of any
known restrictions or bans. Parties can decide whether to allow or ban the importation of
chemicals listed in the treaty, and exporting countries are obliged to make sure that
producers within their jurisdiction comply.The objectives of the Convention are:

    to promote shared responsibility and cooperative efforts among Parties in the
    international trade of certain hazardous chemicals in order to protect human health
    and the environment from potential harm;



                                           55
    to contribute to the environmentally sound use of those hazardous chemicals, by
    facilitating information exchange about their characteristics, by providing for a
    national decision-making process on their import and export and by disseminating
    these decisions to Parties.

The text of the Rotterdam Convention on the Prior Informed Consent (PIC) Procedure for
Certain Hazardous Chemicals and Pesticides in International Trade was adopted on 10
September 1998 by a Conference of Plenipotentiaries in Rotterdam, the Netherlands. The
Convention entered into force on 24 February 2004. To achieve its objectives the
Convention includes two key provisions, namely the Prior Informed Consent (PIC)
procedure and information exchange.

The Convention covers pesticides and industrial chemicals that have been banned or
severely restricted for health or environmental reasons by Parties and which have been
notified by Parties for inclusion in the PIC procedure. One notification from each of two
specified regions triggers consideration of addition of a chemical to Annex III of the
Convention. Severely hazardous pesticide formulations that present a hazard under
conditions of use in developing countries or countries with economies in transition may
also be nominated for inclusion in Annex III.

There are 40 chemicals listed in Annex III of the Convention and subject to the PIC
procedure includes 25 pesticides, 4 severely hazardous pesticide formulations and 11
industrial chemicals. Many more chemicals are expected to be added in the future. The
Conference of the Parties decides on the inclusion of new chemicals. Once a chemical is
included in Annex III, a "decision guidance document" (DGD) containing information
concerning the chemical and the regulatory decisions to ban or severely restrict the
chemical for health or environmental reasons is circulated to all Parties for scrutiny and
endorsement.

The Convention promotes the exchange of information on a very broad range of
chemicals. It does so through:

    the requirement for a Party to inform other Parties of each national ban or severe
    restriction of a chemical;
    the possibility for a Party which is a developing country or a country in transition to
    inform other Parties that it is experiencing problems caused by a severely hazardous
    pesticide formulation under conditions of use in its territory;
    the requirement for a Party that plans to export a chemical that is banned or severely
    restricted for use within its territory, to inform the importing Party that such export
    will take place, before the first shipment and annually thereafter;
    the requirement for an exporting Party, when exporting chemicals that are to be used
    for occupational purposes, to ensure that an up-to-date safety data sheet is sent to the
    importer; and
    labeling requirements for exports of chemicals included in the PIC procedure, as well
    as for other chemicals that are banned or severely restricted in the exporting country.




                                            56
A country becomes a Party when it lodges with the depositary its instrument of
ratification, acceptance, approval or accession to the Convention. Each Party including
member states must designate one or more national authorities, which are the primary
contact points for matters related to the operation of the Convention and are authorized to
perform the administrative functions required by the Convention.

The Rotterdam Convention was ratified by the Government of the United Republic of
Tanzania in 2002. The Designated National Authority (DNA) for overseeing the
implementation of the provisions of the Rotterdam Convention in the Country for
pesticides is Ministry of Agriculture, Food Security and Cooperatives while for consumer
and industrial chemicals the DNA is Government Chemist Laboratory Agency. The
Convention handbook will be one of the guiding documents during the planning and
implementation phases of the proposed project on safeguarding and disposal of obsolete
pesticides.

3.5.3   The Stockholm Convention on Persistent Organic Pollutants (POPs)

The Stockholm Convention on Persistent Organic Pollutants (POPs) is a global treaty to
protect human health and the environment from chemicals that remain intact in the
environment for long periods, become widely distributed geographically and accumulate
in the fatty tissue of humans and wildlife. POPs include the organochlorine pesticides:
DDT, endrin, dieldrin, aldrin, chlordane, toxaphene, heptachlor, hexachlorobenzene,
mirex; and the industrial chemicals and by-products: PCBs, dioxins and furans. These
initial twelve chemicals, the 'poisons without passports' were chosen because they have
the common hazardous characteristics of toxicity, persistence and bioaccumulation, and
are capable of travelling vast distances via water and air. Several discontinued or little-
used pesticides (including aldrin, heptachlor and DDT) are also included in the first list of
substances identified as POPs.

Exposure to Persistent Organic Pollutants (POPs) can lead to serious health effects
including certain cancers, birth defects, dysfunctional immune and reproductive systems,
greater susceptibility to disease and even diminished intelligence. Given their long range
transport, no one government acting alone can protect its citizens or its environment from
POPs. In response, the Stockholm Convention, which was adopted in 2001 and entered
into force in 2004, requires Parties to take measures to eliminate or reduce the release of
POPs into the environment. The Convention is administered by the United Nations
Environment Programme and based in Geneva, Switzerland.

The Convention sets out several objectives including:
      the elimination from commerce of identified POPs and others that may be
      identified in the future;
      encouraging the transition in commerce to safer alternatives;
      identifying additional POPs;
      the clean-up of old stockpiles and equipment containing POPs; and,
      encouraging all stakeholders to work towards a POP-free environment.




                                             57
The screening criteria for POPs include identity, persistence, bio-accumulation, long-
range transport and adverse effects. Any country that is a party to the Convention can
submit a proposal to the secretariat for consideration.

All criteria must be met and be regarded as scientifically justified by the Persistent
Organic Pollutants Review Committee (POPRC) before it can accept a proposal to list a
new chemical as a POP. Following acceptance of a proposal, a risk-benefit profile is
prepared and possible control measures considered.

Depending on the risk profile and the risk management evaluation, the POPRC will
recommend to the Conference of Parties (COP) whether the chemical should be subject
to any controls. The COP formally makes the decision to list the chemical and specify its
related control measures.

To become a Party to the Convention a State or regional economic integration
organization needs to submit its instrument of ratification, acceptance, approval or
accession to the depositary. Parties nominate official contact points for the purpose of
administrative functions and all official communications under the Convention. Parties
are also required to nominate national focal points for the purpose of information
exchange pursuant to Article 9 of the Convention. The Government of the United
Republic of Tanzania ratified the Stockholm Convention in 2004. Division of
Environment in the Vice President’s Office is the Designated National Authority (DNA)
in overseeing the implementation of the provisions of the Stockholm Convention in the
Country.

The proposed project supports the implementation of the Stockholm Convention since it
involves clean-up of old stockpiles and equipment containing pesticides, which is one of
the main objectives of the Convention. The proposed project on safeguarding and
disposal of obsolete pesticides will assist the Country in meeting its obligations to
remove POPs as required under the Stockholm Convention. In addition, the Stockholm
Convention will inevitably be used in guiding the planning as well as the implementation
phases of the proposed project because the Government of the United Republic of
Tanzania has ratified the Convention.

3.6     Institutional Framework for Environmental Management in Tanzania

3.6.1   Overall management responsibility

The institutional arrangement for environmental management in Tanzania is well spelt
out in the EMA (2004). There are seven (7) institutions mentioned by the act, of which
the minister responsible for environment is the overall in-charge for administration of all
matters relating to the environment.
The legal institutions for environmental management in the country include;
     National Environmental Advisory Committee;
     Minister responsible for Environment;
     Director of Environment;



                                            58
       National Environment Management Council (NEMC);
       Sector Ministries;
       Regional Secretariat;
       Local Government Authorities (City, Municipal, District, Township, Ward,
        Village, sub-village “Mtaa and Kitongoji?)

However, only the functions of National Environmental Advisory Committee, Minister
responsible for Environment, Director of Environment, and National Environment
Management Council (NEMC) will be discussed here since they are considered to be
more relevant to the proposed Project.

3.6.2   National Environmental Advisory Committee

The National Environmental Advisory Committee is comprised of members with
experience from various field of environmental management in the public, private sector
and the civil society. The advisory committee advises the Minister on any matter related
to environmental management. Other functions include:
     Examine any matter that may be referred to it by the Minister or any sector
        Ministry relating to the protection and management of the environment;
     Review and advise the Minister on any environmental plans, environmental
        impact assessment of major projects and activities to which environmental impact
        review is necessary;
     Review the achievement by the Council (NEMC) of objectives, goals and targets
        set by the Council and advise the Minister accordingly;
     Review and advise the Minister on any environmental standards, guidelines and
        regulations;
     Receive and deliberate on the reports from Sector Ministries regarding the
        protection and management of the environment;
     Perform other environmental advisory services to the Minister as it may be
        necessary.

3.6.3   Minister Responsible for Environment

The Minister is responsible for matters relating to environment, including giving policy
guidelines necessary for promotion, protection and sustainable management of
environment in Tanzania. The Minister approves EIS. The Minister may also delegate the
power of approval for EIA to the DOE, Local Government Authorities or Sector
Ministries. The Minister also:
    Prescribes (in the regulations) the qualifications of persons who may conduct
       ElA;
    Reviews NEMC reports on the approval of EIS;
    Issues EIA certificate for project subject to EIA;
    Suspends EIA certificate in case of non-compliance.




                                          59
3.6.4   Director of Environment

The Director of Environment (DoE) heads the Division of Environment and is appointed
by the President of the United Republic of Tanzania. The functions of the Director of
Environment include:
    Coordination of various environmental management activities undertaken by
       other agencies;
    Promotion of integration of environmental considerations into development
       policies, plans, programmes, strategies, projects;
    Undertaking strategic environmental risk assessment with a view to ensuring the
       proper management and rational utilization of environmental resources on a
       sustainable basis for the improvement of quality of human life in Tanzania;
    Advise the Government on legislative and other measures for the management of
       the environment or the implementation of the relevant international environmental
       agreements in the field of environment;
    Monitoring and assessing activities undertaken by relevant Sector Ministries and
       agencies;
    Preparation and issuing of reports on the state of the environment in Tanzania
       through relevant agencies;
    Coordination of issues relating to articulation and implementation of
       environmental management aspects of other sector policies and the National
       Environment Policy.

3.6.5   National Environment Management Council (NEMC)

The NEMC's purpose and objective is to undertake enforcement, compliance, review and
monitoring of EIA and to facilitate public participation in environmental decision-
making.

In reference to EMA (2004), NEMC:
     Registers experts and firms authorized to conduct EIA;
     Registers projects subject to EIA;
     Determines the scope of the EIA;
     Set-ups cross-sectoral TAC to advise on EIA reviews;
     Requests additional information to complete the EIA review;
     Assesses and comments on EIA, in collaboration with other stakeholders,
     Convenes public hearings to obtain comments on the proposed project;
     Recommends to the Minister to approve, reject, or approve with conditions
        specific EIS;
     Monitors the effects of activities on the environment;
     Controls the implementation of the Environmental Management Plan (EMP);
     Makes recommendations on whether to revoke EIA Certificates, in case of non-
        compliance;
     Promotes public environmental awareness;
     Conducts Environmental Audits.




                                          60
3.6.6   Sector Ministries

Under the existing institutional and legal framework the Sector Ministries are required to
establish Sector Environmental Sections headed by the Sector Environmental
Coordinator.

The Sector Ministries Environmental Sections:
      Ensure environmental compliance by the Sector Ministry;
      Ensure all environmental matters contained in other written laws falling under
      sector ministry are implemented and report of their implementation is submitted
      to the DoE;
      Liaise with the DoE and the Council (NEMC) on matters involving environment
      and all matters with respect to which cooperation or shared responsibility is
      desirable or required;
      Ensure that environmental concerns are integrated into the ministry or
      departmental development planning and project implementation in a way which
      protects the environment;
      Evaluate existing and proposed policies and legislation and recommend measures
      to ensure that those policies and legislation take adequate account of effect on the
      environment;
      Prepare and coordinate the implementation of environmental action plans at
      national and local levels;
      Promote public awareness of environmental issues through educational
      programmes and dissemination of information;
      Refer to the Council (NEMC) any matter related to the environment;
      Undertake analysis of environmental impact of sectoral legislation, regulation,
      policies, plans, strategies and programmes through strategic environmental
      assessment (SEA);
      Ensure that sectoral standards are environmentally sound;
      Oversee the preparation of and implementation of EIA required for investments in
      the sector;
      Ensure compliance with various regulations, guidelines and procedures issued by
      the Minister responsible for environment and;
      Work closely with Ministry responsible for local government, to provide
      environmental advice and technical support to district level staff working in the
      sector.

In terms of the Institutional arrangements for implementation of the ASP in general and
the CESA in particular, responsibilities for mitigation and monitoring should be defined
along with arrangements for information flow, especially for coordination between
agencies responsible for mitigation. This is especially important since this project
requires cross-sector and cross-institutional integration.




                                           61
3.7     Institutional and Implementation Arrangements for Project Implementation

3.7.1   National Project Steering Committee (NPSC)

At the national level, a National Project Steering Committee (NPSC) has been established
to act as main advisory body for the project. The NPSC membership composition consists
of senior representatives of key government departments involved in the project. The
NPSC is responsible for:

        Monitoring of overall project progress based on regular reporting by the PMU and
        reviewing progress reports to be provided to the World Bank;
        Confirming the implementation of the various activities by agencies to the project
        as per operational manual and work plan and verifying appropriate allocation of
        resources;
        Ensuring that agencies responsible for implementation of key activities provide
        regular reports to the PMU;
        Ensuring adherence to World Bank and Tanzania safeguard/EIA requirements
        including environmental assessment, risk assessment and health and safety
        aspects; and,
        Review and recommend actions that enhance inter-agency coordination and
        collaboration.

The NPSC is chaired by the Permanent Secretary of the Vice President’s Office (or a
nominee) and comprises of the following Government institutions: NEMC, MAFS&C,
Vice President’s Office, Tropical Pesticides Research Institute (TPRI), Chief
Government Chemist, Occupational Safety and Health Authority (OSHA), Division of
Environment (Focal point for Basel Convention), Tanzania Ports Authority (TPA),
Department of Environment - Zanzibar and Department of Agriculture – Zanzibar.

NEMC is the overall Project executing agency and its responsibilities in cooperation with
relevant government institutions, including Ministry of Agriculture and Food Security
(MAFS&C), the private sector, NGOs and other key organizations. NEMC has
established a Project Management Unit for the day-to-day operation of the project.

3.7.2   Stakeholder Advisory Forum (SAF)

This Forum is open to representatives from donors, International Organizations, NGOs,
organized agriculture and other groups interested in the project. Through the SAF these
stakeholders are provided with an opportunity to review project progress and to advise
the NPSC on implementation issues and how they can assist in this regard especially with
outreach and communication activities in order to make the project a success. The SAF
is a non-executive body and has no decision making powers. It is accepted, however, that
the recommendations of the SAF should be given due respect and consideration by the
NPSC.




                                            62
3.7.3   The Project Management Unit (PMU)

The PMU has been established in line with Government and WB policy. The project is
as far as possible being implemented using existing government staff and structures with
as little creation of separate project structures and management systems as possible. The
PMU is accountable to the Director General of NEMC. The PMU is responsible for
coordinating and monitoring the overall project, with actual implementation largely
undertaken by zonal staff from Ministry of Agriculture and Food Security (MAFS&C)
supported by two Field Managers. The PMU comprises of the following positions:

               National Project Coordinator (NPC)
               Prevention Coordinator (PC) from the Ministry of Agriculture, Food
               Security and Cooperatives (MAFSC)
               Technical Advisor for Disposal (TAD)
               Field Managers (FM) from NEMC
               Project Accountant
               Procurement Officer
               Communication officer
               Monitoring and Evaluation officer


3.7.4   Project Implementation Arrangements

For day to day project implementation, the PMU has formed Task Teams (TT). Under
the disposal component, two main TTs have been established namely the
Inventory/Safeguarding team and the CESA task team. The inventory TT focused on the
completion of a comprehensive national inventory of all obsolete pesticides and
associated wastes according to existing FAO technical guidelines.

The CESA Task Team is to oversee the conducting of CESA studies. More complex
safeguarding/repackaging at high risk locations identified during inventory will form a
part of the disposal tender. It is estimated that a total of 30 staff from the zonal, regional
and district levels will be needed on a part-time basis during the life of the project.

3.8     Involvement of the Stakeholders

All relevant stakeholders should be involved for ensuring that the pesticide safeguarding
and disposal exercise is successfully conducted. Key stakeholders will be Government
institutions, especially Ministry of Agriculture and Food Security, Tropical Pesticides
Research Institute, Department of Environment in the Vice President’s Office, NEMC,
Disaster Management Department in the Prime Minister’s Office, Ministry of
Infrastructure Development, Government Chemist Laboratory Agency, Regional
Secretariats, Local Government Authorities at district, ward, sub-ward and village levels.
These Government institutions will be responsible for overseeing that the project
activities (repackaging, storage at MCC, transportation by road and sea and disposal of


                                             63
the OP) are done in accordance to the Government laws and regulations. PMU will play
the role of coordination of the project in liaison with the ASP Focal Points.


Civil Society Organisations (CSOs) in vicinity of the pesticide storage sites and the
NGOs will work closely with PMU in undertaking the project activities. Since CSOs and
NGOs will not participate directly in the project activities, they are the right groups to
carry out an independent monitoring of the project activities. However, basic training in
safeguarding and disposal of obsolete pesticides is required for staff of CSOs and NGOs
to be able to play their role effectively and efficiently.

The success of the safeguarding and disposal of obsolete pesticides activities will also
depend on the awareness and involvement of pesticide end users such as workers, farm
owners, small scale farmers and local communities. CSOs and NGOs can reach a
significant number of end users through already established networks.

                                    CHAPTER FOUR

4.0     BASELINE DATA

4.1     Characterization of Obsolete Pesticide Stocks and Sites

Based on Inventory carried out under ASP Tanzania Project, there are 149 obsolete
pesticide stores containing free standing obsolete pesticides and associated waste
amounting to 700 tons. Surveys at pesticide contaminated sites and subsequent sampling
and analysis of samples of contaminated soil were also carried out in 2009 by
independent consultants. The contaminated soil remains in place at the original
contaminated sites; neither excavation nor storage of the contaminated soil have been
done so far. Except for heavily contaminated soil, all OPs and associated wastes are
found inside stores.

Out of 22 sites surveyed and sampled by the independent consultants for pesticide
contamination, 14 sites were found to have pesticide contaminated soil. Detailed
information on the characteristics and attributes of the 22 sites surveyed for pesticide
contamination are available in a series of separate comprehensive reports prepared by the
independent consultants (Kishimba, 2009; McDowall, 2009a-d). A corresponding
summary of the pertinent information on the 22 sites is given in Table 4.1a. Upon
subsequent risk analysis incorporating key pesticide and site characteristics and
attributes, a list of nine (9) priority sites was derived. Summarized profiles of the nine (9)
priority (high and medium priority) pesticide contaminated sites are presented in Table
4.1b.

                                                66
As expected, Table 4.1a indicates that some sites were found to have no soil
contamination. Table 4.1b indicates that, of the nine high priority pesticide contaminated
sites, the NHC site in Morogoro must be accorded the highest priority while the
Murbadaw site can be given the lowest priority.

With a view to fulfilling the objectives of this CESA, a selection of high priority risk,
medium priority risk, and low priority risk sites were visited between January and
February 2010 by joint teams of experts from the client (NEMC) on the one hand and the
consultant on the other. The main objectives of the field visits were to conduct physical
and socio-economic surveys at the OP store and OP contaminated sites of interest and
collect water and soil samples for laboratory analysis. The laboratory analysis needed to
be done in order to get an indication of water and soil contamination related to the OP
storage premises. Samples of soil from contaminated sites located at two of the visited
sites were collected for analysis to gauge the extent and severity of contamination. The
sampling and the subsequent analyses carried out during this project followed up on an
earlier study carried out on buried pesticides and contaminated soils at these sites. The
sites covered by the field visits are distributed in eight regions of Mainland Tanzania and
in Zanzibar.




Figure 4.1: Obsolete pesticides store locations in Tanzania


                                            67
Locations of the obsolete pesticides stores including the ones that were not covered
during the field visits are shown in Figures 4.1. Figure 4.1 serves to give visually
assessable spatial relationships among the stores.

The contaminated sites that were eventually visited were jointly selected by NEMC and
the consultant on the basis of risk categories determined using the PSMS database and
accessibility during the scheduled visits within the timeline of this project. Time and
resource constraints within the framework of this project permitted visits to only seven
high priority risk sites, six medium priority risk sites, and three low priority risk sites. In
spite of the constraints, the selection of the sites to be visited ensured that the selected
sites were fairly representative of all other sites in their respective risk category
countrywide. Characterisation information on the obsolete pesticides found during the
site visits and condition of the respective stores and sites is conveniently outlined along
with information on their environmental and social impacts in Chapter Five.

It was considered uneconomical and impractical to analyse the water and soil samples for
all possible OPs and their metabolites. For this reason, the analysis focused on a narrow
range of selected representative contaminants. DDT and its metabolites were selected as
representative contaminants because of the following reasons: 1) DDT has been used in
proportionately higher quantities than other pesticides, 2) findings from past studies
indicate that DDT is the most dominant pesticide at the sites of interest, and 3) DDT is
among the pesticides with the most severe and far reaching public health and
environmental implications.

Results of analysis of water samples taken from various locations within and around three
of the high risk OP store sites (Vikuge site in Pwani Region, Morogoro Farmers
Cooperative Union (MOFACU) site in Morogoro, and Mbarali and Uyole farms in
Mbeya) are presented in Appendix 4A. Comparing the data for all of the sites, it is
evident that Vikuge site has the most elevated levels of pesticide water pollution. The
MOFACU site in Morogoro is a distant second to the Vikuge site. The sites in Mbeya
have levels that, for the purpose of this report, can be neglected.

The DDT analysis data for the Vikuge site suggest the presence of all the forms of DDT
analyzed (pp-DDT, pp-DDD, op-DDT, and pp-DDE) both upstream and downstream of
the store location. On the whole, the presence of DDT in the water samples analysed
indicates the potential for serious environmental and health risks in the affected areas and
this reinforces the need to take action. The detection of traces of DDT upstream as well
as downstream of the Vikuge OP site suggests that the store site is not the only source of
pesticide pollution around the Vikuge area. In turn, while this does not absolve the
Vikuge store site regarding environmental and health problems in its vicinity, it indicates
the need for caution when drawing conclusions, including attributing all observed
pollution problems to the site. Soil pollution assessment, which was carried out at Vikuge
and other sites in 2009, supports the water quality analysis results just discussed with
respect to the Vikuge site. In contrast, the soil pollution assessment for Mbarali Rice
Farm, also carried out in 2009, indicates that the farm area and its vicinity are not



                                              68
contaminated by pesticides. As such, the soil pollution assessment supports the water
quality analysis results carried out for this report.

The absence of DDT and its metabolites in many of the samples collected from other
sites, for example the Mbarali Rice Farm and Uyole ARI sites, suggests lower
environmental and public health risks within and in the vicinity of the respective sites.

Results of analysis of soil samples taken in early 2010 from Utete Boma in Pwani and
Tumbi ARI in Tabora indicate the presence of DDT and its metabolites in soil within the
premises of the stores at these locations. At the Utete site, concentrations of pp-DDT
were the highest followed by those of pp-DDD. On the other hand, concentrations of both
op-DDT and pp-DDE were generally below detection limits. At the Tumbi site, soil
contamination levels were generally high for both pp-DDT and pp-DDD. Contamination
levels for both op-DDT and pp-DDE were generally low. For both sites, contamination
levels within the 0 – 15 cm and 15 – 30 cm soil depths were generally comparable. On
the whole, concentrations of DDT and its metabolites at the Tumbi site were higher than
at the Utete site. At the Tumbi site, concentrations of pp-DDT were 1.10(SD=0.04) mg
per kg of soil (dry soil weight) and those of pp-DDD were 0.82(SD=0.19 mg per kg of
soil (dry soil weight). The corresponding figures for the Utete site were 0.21(SD=0.02)
mg/kg and 0.12(SD=0.02) mg/kg.

The presence of DDT and its metabolites at the surveyed sites indicates the presence of
public health and environmental risks at these sites. The higher soil contamination levels
exhibited at the Tumbi site imply that the health and envieonrmental risks are higher at
the Tumbi site than at the Utete site. The findings just discussed to the effect that obsolete
pesticide stores and contaminated sites exhibit different levels of soil contamination are
in agreement with the findings from past contammination assessment efforts. Notably,
based on the PSMS database, the risk level for the Tumbi site is high while that for the
Utete site is merely medium (See Table 4.2). Therefore, the soil sample analysis data are
in agreement with the PSMS database information. This adds credence to the findings
from soil sample analysis.

Since there are other obsolete pesticide storage sites in Tanzania which share many
features and characteristics with the Tumbi and Utete sites, the public health and
environmental concerns observed with respect to the Tumbi and Utete sites should also
be of concern with respect to the other sites.


4.2    Previous Waste Management and Pesticide Disposal Initiatives

Tanzania has a history of accumulations of obsolete pesticides, with the earliest known
stocks dating back from the 1970’s. The stocks have arisen as a result of poor pesticides
management practice in the country, primarily from:

       lack of legislative frameworks;
       incorrect needs assessment and procurement;


                                             69
       donations of pesticides that were not required;
       poor storage conditions; and
       poor stock management.

A number of projects have over the years been implemented to deal with the problem.

In 1986, SIDA provided assistance to repack a large quantity of pesticides at Vikuge
(Coastal Region). Over 200 tons, including a large amount of DDT, was lying scattered
in the open, severely polluting the environment. With assistance of SIDA the pesticides
were repackaged and stored in a new pesticide store which was built at the site. The
repackaged pesticides now need to be disposed of and the previous dump site needs to be
cleaned up and decontaminated.

In 1995, the Government of the Netherlands funded the removal of all hazardous waste
from the islands of Zanzibar and Pemba. An estimated quantity of 300 tons were
removed and incinerated in the UK. Experience gained during the Zanzibar operation
provided a solid base for the subsequent inventory of stocks on the mainland of Tanzania.
The clean-up operations in Zanzibar involved local labourers and participation of the
Department of Environment, while an international expert sponsored by the donor
supervised the actual project execution. The collection, international shipment and
incineration operations were carried out by a commercial UK-based hazardous waste
company. The Government of Zanzibar supported the operations, amongst others, with
local staff, clearance letters for arriving/departing equipment and materials, clearance
procedures with customs and immigration departments allowing easy access for
international staff with essential equipment etc.

Also in 1995, the German Agency for Technical Cooperation (GTZ) provided technical
assistance to incinerate 57 tons of Dinitro-o-Cresol (DNOC) in a cement kiln at Wazo
Hill in Dar es Salaam. A special unit was constructed to feed in the pesticide with the
fuel. No contaminants were detected in the exhaust emissions from the kiln or in the final
cement produced during the disposal exercise. After the project completion this unit was
handed over to the Government for future use.

In 1997-1998, the Government of the Netherlands supported Tanzania in conducting a
nation-wide inventory of OP and veterinary waste. Twelve Government staffs were
trained in conducting the inventory and eight of them implemented the inventory
covering more than 350 different sites in a 15-month period.

NEMC was responsible for the overall project supervision (through a Project Coordinator
and a Project Manager) with support of an experienced international Technical Advisor.
The execution of the actual inventory was carried out in collaboration with other
stakeholders i.e. 7 national institutions and various waste owners. The collaborating
institutions included:

o      Ministry of Agriculture,
o      Ministry of Finance,


                                           70
o      Tropical Pesticides Research Institute
o      Factories Inspectorate.
o      Department of Environment
o      Ministry of Industries & Trade
o      Ministry of Health (Government Chemist Laboratory Agency - GCLA).

The inventory identified about 1,000 tons of OP and some 200 tons of veterinary waste,
mainly drugs and vaccines. The found OP included large volumes of organo-chlorinated
products, notably DDT. There were also found large quantities of organo-phosphate
pesticides with high toxicity for people and livestock scattered over more than 300 stores.
The majority of the stores do not meet the basic requirements for environmentally sound
storage of hazardous chemicals.

In 1999, a study was commissioned by NEMC under NORAD funding to evaluate the
feasibility of Obsolete Pesticide destruction in Cement kilns. Two cement plants were
analysed, namely the Tanzania Portland (Wazo Hill) and the Tanga Cement Company.
The study concluded that from a technical point of view, the plants could in principle be
suitable for pesticide burning, on condition that the electrostatic precipitators at the Wazo
Hill facility be replaced. Recent surveys and consultations with the Wazo Hill cement
plant management have revealed that the electrostatic precipitators have not been
replaced and there are no plans to replace them in the near future.

It is important to note that, despite the technical feasibility of using cement kilns to
destroy OPs as discussed above, the practical feasibility of such an endeavour rests on,
among other factors, the following:

       Willingness of the kiln owners to use them for destroying OPs and make the
       necessary adjustment to accommodate public health and environmental protection
       needs for a one time OP destruction operation;
       Acceptability of the option by environmental regulatory authorities in Tanzania;
       Acceptability of the option among communities who live close to the kilns – a
       separate environmental and social impact assessment (ESIA) would be needed to
       establish this.

From a risk point of view, the Wazo Hill Plant in Dar es Salaam could cause
environmental problems because of the densely populated areas near and around the
plant.
In recognition of the persistent OP problem Tanzania made an official appeal for
assistance from the international donor community during the FAO Third Consultation
on Prevention and Disposal of Obsolete Pesticide Stocks, which was held in Rome in
March 1998. Tanzania was subsequently included among the first seven countries to
benefit from the financing through the Africa Stockpiles Program (ASP) funded by the
Global Environment Facility (GEF) through the World Bank as implementing agency.

In 2003 FAO completed a “training of trainers? workshop for the safeguarding of
obsolete pesticides. This was followed by emergency intervention at six highly



                                             71
contaminated sites previously identified as posing a direct risk to public health and the
environment.

Under the ASP programme NEMC in 2009 verified the national inventories of the OP
and the resulting data have been entered into the FAO web based Pesticide Stock
Management System (PSMS). All the identified sites were characterised using the PSMS.
The new inventory identified that Tanzania has 149 stores where OP is stockpiled
including 14 sites that are heavily contaminated with pesticides. The OP stocks in the
country total 700 tons with a further 2,755 tons of contaminated soils and 382 tons of
contaminated empty pesticide containers and pieces of equipment. Building materials and
components contaminated with pesticides amound to 2,000 kg of hazardous waste that
will require special handling alongside the OPs.

It is noteworthy that some of the sites that have heavily contaminated soil do not have
free standing, publicly held obsolete pesticides and associated wastes. Generally,
wherever there is contaminated soil, it is found outside the corresponding stores.

The coverage of the OP inventory undertaken in 2009 by NEMC under the ASP – P1 as
discussed above encompassed OP stocks stored in stores. This implies that the inventory
implicitly as well as directly covered the buildings that served as OP stores. Indeed, a
condition survey of the store buildings was done alongside or after the OP stock
inventory. The surveys undertaken for this report covered some condition survey of the
OP store buildings too.

Regarding the quality status, management needs, and management options of buried
pesticides and contaminated soils at the stores and sites covered in this report, extensive
work was done by McDowall (McDowall, 2009a-d). Kishimba (Kishimba, 2009)
collaborated with McDowall (2009) in these efforts by way of carrying out sampling and
analysis of soils from various sites of interest. The buried pesticides and contaminated
soils are currently in their original places in the vicinity of the OP storage premises.

McDowall’s study efforts on the buried pesticides and contaminated soils are presented in
a number of reports that are publicly accessible (McDowall, 2009a – d). Among other
things, the reports cover characterisation of the buried pesticides, pollution status of the
contaminated soils, risk assessment, final environmental management plan (EMP), and
disposal options.

OPs = obsolete pesticides; cont. material = contaminated material (this refers to any pesticide associated
waste inside store that is not pesticides, contaminated building sections and materials, spraying equipment
or contaminated containers) ; Cont. building = contaminated buildings; Cont. equipment = contaminated
equipment; Cont. soil = contaminated soil.

The FAO Environmental Management Tool Kit (FAO EMTK) for Obsolete Pesticides
was used for gathering and interpreting information on obsolete pesticide stocks. It was
also used to develop a coherent, risk-based strategy for the environmentally sound
management of those obsolete pesticides (FAO, 2009). The FAO EMTK is composed of
two tools namely Tool A and Tool B. Tool A aims to gathering the essential information
for appraising the environmental and public health risks associated with any stock of
obsolete pesticides and identifying the stores with the worst conditions in terms of current
or potential impacts on public health and/or the environment. Tool B is used to identify


                                                     73
the stores that pose a comparatively high level of risk to the general public and the
environment. It aims to provide a methodology for recognizing the most critical stores
and prioritizing them based on risk.

The FAO EMTK was used for the following purposes:

i)     Ranking the stores according to the level of risk associated with the pesticides
       contained in each store, their toxicity and their packaging conditions: The
       more pesticides contained in a store, the more toxic those pesticides are and/or the
       worse the condition of the packaging materials in terms of leakage, the higher the
       associated risk. This tool can be applied to calculate the risk factor FP , which
       represents the risk related to conditions associated with the pesticides (i.e., the
       conditions prevailing inside the store). FP has a linear progression; the greater the
       value of FP, the higher the associated risk.

ii)    Ranking the stores according to the conditions of each store structure and to
       the storage conditions inside and the environmental conditions outside the
       store: The worse the conditions associated with the store structure and the greater
       the store’s relationship with or proximity to critical areas, the higher the risk or
       potential risk to public health and the environment in case of an accident at the
       store. The risk factor FE is calculated for each store, based on the results of a
       simple questionnaire completed for each location.

iii)   Characterizing the general situation prevailing in each store by combining
       the two risk factors FP and FE and plotting them on a single graph, divided
       into four quadrants: This provides a comparative analysis based on the sample
       population of stores included in the survey. Depending on the position of a store
       on the graph (Figure 4.2), the situation prevailing in that store relative to the other
       stores in the survey can be characterized as lower-priority, problematic (when
       either FP or FE is high) or critical (when both FP and FE have high values).

The stores were classified into three categories of high, medium and low risk based on
toxicity, quantity, surrounding environmental conditions and the security of the store.
The categorization of the stores followed the FAO Environmental Management Toolkit
in which environmental factor (Fe) values higher than 50 were considered problematic.
Relating to the risk posed by the type and quantity of the OP, an Fp criterion value of
above 50,000 was considered to be a high risk factor. In addition, the WHO class, the
condition of the packaging, and whether the active ingredient is known, was considered
during the evaluation. Information on OP stores and their corresponding Fe and Fp data
are presented in Table 4.3.
