The diversity of the sizes of containers shown in Table 4.6 indicates the degree of
difficulty involved in handling the containers. It also gives an idea about the reasons the
need for consolidating the OP stocks through repackaging OPs in small sized containers
into larger ones.


                                            90
4.4    Assessment of Capacity for a National Safeguarding Operation

The capacity of the country to undertake a nationwide safeguarding operation of the
obsolete pesticide stocks and contaminated soil sites can conveniently be assessed on the
basis of: availability of financial resources, human resources (expertise) required,
availability of appropriate technology, availability of appropriate experience, availability
of appropriate equipment and facilities, availability of appropriate safeguarding premises
judged on the basis of site capacity and suitability of premise characteristics, types and
condition of the obsolete pesticide stocks and contaminated soils that need safeguarding,
locations of the pesticide stocks and contaminated soil sites, and quantities of obsolete
pesticide stocks and contaminated soil that need to be safeguarded. These criteria are
discussed in detail in the following subsections.

Availability of financial resources
The country faces financial constraints, which means inadequate financial capacity of the
government presents a major constraint to national obsolete pesticide safeguarding
efforts. As such, in the foreseeable future such efforts will only be possible with donor
assistance as is the case now with the ASP-P1.

Availability of human resources (expertise)
The safeguarding operations carried out by and under the direction of NEMC so far
suggest that, to a great extent, appropriate expertise and experience are available. The
number of appropriately qualified and experienced personnel is smaller than that required
to carry out a nationwide operation. This implies that training of additional personnel will
need to be carried out to raise the number to an appropriate level. An appropriate needs
analysis will obviously need to be carried out to establish the personnel requirement. It is
recognised that NEMC has in the past carried out training of personnel who were later
involved in assisting with on-site safeguarding operations.

Availability of appropriate technology
Previous safeguarding efforts undertaken and spearheaded by NEMC are a pointer to the
appropriate and applicable safeguarding technology. The technology is embodied in the
methods, tools, and practices employed.

Availability of appropriate experience
Similarly to the discussion on the availability of expertise, the safeguarding operations
carried out by/and under the direction of NEMC in the recent past suggest that
appropriate experience is available. However, because the previous safeguarding
operations were on a small scale, the number of appropriately qualified and experienced
personnel is correspondingly small. There is no doubt that the number of appropriately
qualified staff is smaller than that required to carry out a nationwide operation.




                                            91
Availability of appropriate equipment and facilities
Previous safeguarding operations used appropriate equipment and facilities, including
personal protective equipment (PPE). As such, for safeguarding activities similar to the
ones that have been carried out in the past, only quantities need to be increased for non-
consumable equipment and materials. However, new supplies that match the projected
needs would be needed for the consumable materials.

Availability of appropriate safeguarding premises
Judged on the basis of site capacity and suitability of site and store characteristics,
appropriate safeguarding premises are available. Improvements required to be done on
the stores and their environs to make them more suitable and secure would need to be
carried out as part and parcel of the safeguarding efforts.

Obsolete pesticides stock types and conditions
As evident in Table 4.4, a significant portion of the OP stocks whose constituents are
known are classified as extremely hazardous (3,286 kg) or highly hazardous (539 kg). On
the other hand, almost a half (47%) of all OP stocks contain yet unidentified chemicals.
The presence of large quantities of extremely and highly hazardous OPs plus the huge
quantity of OPs whose identities are not known pose serious challenges that are beyond
the currently available local coping capacity.

Obsolete pesticides stock store and site locations
As evident from Figure 4.1, the locations of the pesticide stocks and contaminated soil
sites are scattered all over the country (Mainland Tanzania and Zanzibar). Obviously, this
complicates and magnifies safeguarding needs. However, the stores and contaminated
sites are located in areas that are generally accessible. Moreover, as demonstrated in
sections that follow, many of the locations of individual stores and premises can be
conveniently clustered so that their needs are attended to as groups rather than individual
stores and sites. Notably, the individual premises as well as the potential clusters are
connected by reliable transport means among themselves as well as to shipping outlets.

Obsolete pesticides stock and soil quantities
As shown in Tables 4.2 – 4.3, quantities of obsolete pesticide stocks stored at individual
stores that need to be safeguarded have a large range. Obsolete pesticide containers and
pesticide contaminated empty containers come in different sizes as well. Sizes of areas of
contaminated soil also vary in a similar manner. The fact that pesticide container sizes
vary widely reinforces the need for repackaging to consolidate the stocks. The differences
in stock sizes reinforces the need for clustering as pointed out earlier. With clustering,
stocks from stores that have small stocks can be moved to the stores with large stocks
where they can be more conveniently handled. Obviously, the clustering outlined here is
contingent upon the large stores having sufficient capacity. This issue is addressed again
later.

Taking into account all major determinants and influencing factors, the capacity of the
country to carry out national obsolete pesticides safeguarding operations can be summed
up as shown in Table 4.8.



                                            92
Table 4.8: Assessment of country’s capacity for a national safeguarding operation
 Assessment bases/criteria                          Safeguarding operation capacity
                                                    scale
                                                    Small             Large operation
                                                    operation
 Availability of financial resources,                     Low                Low
 Availability of human resources (expertise)              High               Low
 Availability of appropriate technology                   High               Low
 Availability of appropriate experience                   High               Low
 Availability of appropriate equipment and                High               Low
 facilities
 Availability of appropriate safeguarding                 High              High
 premises
 Obsolete pesticides stock types and condition            Low                Low
 Obsolete pesticides stock store and site locations       High               Low
 Obsolete pesticides stock and soil quantities            High               Low

In line with what was pointed out earlier, on the whole, Table 4.8 suggests that any
national obsolete pesticide safeguarding operation is generally not possible without
external assistance. Even with external financial assistance, only small scale safeguarding
operations are really feasible if external contractors are not involved at all. Evidently,
large scale safeguarding operations can be meaningfully carried out only with the
involvement of appropriately qualified and experienced external contractors. Obviously,
it is possible to combine internal and external capacities to conveniently carry out the
safeguarding operation with a view to achieving economies of scale, facilitating transfer
of technology from external contractors and personnel to internal ones, and enabling the
safeguarding operations contribute to the local economy.


4.5    Assessment of Current National Transport, Treatment and Disposal
       Capacity

Similarly to the capacity for a nationwide safeguarding operation, the current national
obsolete pesticide stock disposal capacity can conveniently be assessed on the basis of:
availability of financial resources, human resources (expertise) required, availability of
appropriate technology, availability of appropriate experience, availability of appropriate
equipment and facilities, availability of appropriate disposal premises judged on the basis
of capacity and suitability of site characteristics, types and condition of the obsolete
pesticide stocks, locations of the pesticide stocks sites, and quantities of obsolete
pesticide stocks that need to be disposed of. An assessment of the current national
capacity to properly dispose of the OP and associated wastes including pesticide
contaminated empy containers based on the criteria listed above is presented in Table 4.9
and discussed in detail in subsequent subsections. A broader assessment, which
encompasses transport and treatment as well as disposal criteria, is summarized in Table
4.10. A discussion thereof is presented later.


                                            93
Table 4.9: Assessment of national OP and OP contaminated waste disposal capacity
 Assessment bases/criteria                     Disposal operation capacity scale
                                               Small operation     Large operation
 Availability of financial resources,                 Low                 Low
 Availability human resources (expertise)             Low                 Low
 Availability of appropriate technology               Low                 Low
 Availability of appropriate experience               Low                 Low
 Availability of appropriate equipment and            Low                 Low
 facilities
 Availability of appropriate disposal premises        Low                 Low
 Obsolete pesticides stock types and condition        Low                 Low
 Obsolete pesticides stock store and site             Low                 Low
 locations
 Obsolete pesticides stock and soil quantities        Low                 Low

Availability of financial resources
As indicated with respect to the assessment of the country’s capacity to carry out a
national safeguarding operation, financial constraints present one of the most serious
impediments to an internally funded pesticide disposal operation. There is no doubt that
in the foreseeable future, funding for disposal of obsolete pesticides will need to come
from largely external sources as is the case with the funding of ASP – P1 in Tanzania.

Availability of human resources (expertise)
Expertise for general waste management is available in the country. For example, at least
one local university runs an undergraduate level environmental engineering programme
and a graduate level environmental technology and management programme that produce
engineers capable of addressing waste management needs including those pertinent to
hazardous wastes. These constitute a pool of potential waste management experts.
However, with respect to specific needs of obsolete pesticide management, human
resources are limited by both types of expertise and number of experts. Although
appropriately qualified and experienced individual experts, such as the members of the
consultant team involved in undertaking this project, are available, these are not many
and the expertise range may be limited for some of them.

Availability of appropriate technology
The issue of availability of appropriate technology is related to and parallels that of
availability of appropriate expertise as well as availability of appropriate experience. As
is the case with many developing countries, there is no available proven home-grown
pesticide disposal technologies. As such, technologies developed elsewhere that are
available need to be resorted to. An exhaustive review on the pesticide management
technologies that are relevant to this discussion was undertaken by McDowall (2009c) as
part of a previous study on contaminated soil and buried pesticides commissioned by
ASP Tanzania Project.




                                            94
Although the the review by McDowall (2009c) looked at a wide range of technologies, it
understandably did not practically test the technologies for their applicability and
suitability to the situation at hand. Therefore, experiences gained through actual practices
can provide more insights on technology feasibility.

On the whole, previous waste disposal efforts present feasible options for obsolete
pesticide disposal. As outlined earlier, in 1995, the Netherlands Government funded the
removal of 300 tons of hazardous waste from the Island of Zanzibar and Pemba. The
waste was shipped and incinerated in the UK. On the other hand, in the same year, The
German agency for Technical Cooperation (GTZ) provided assistance to incinerate 57
tons of Dinitro-o-Cresol (DNOC) at Wazo Cement kiln. Although the fact that Tanzania
has three well established cement kilns which can potentially be used to incinerate
pesticides presents a potentially feasible option for disposal of obsolete pesticides, past
experiences rule this option out, at least in the short term.

A recent study specifically carried out for this report to validate and update previous
findings on the feasibility of using cement kilns to destroy OPs revealed that using the
cement kilns to destroy the obsolete pesticides in this project is not possible. Owners and
operators of the cement kilns categorically stated that using their kilns to destroy OPs
would not be possible mainly because of the extensive modifications required to make
the kilns capable of undertaking the OP destruction as well as because of uncertainities
regarding the performance and compliance of the kilns with environmental regulations.

Additionally, the likely strong public opposition and political sensitivity surrounding the
development of such a facility would make the adoption of such an option difficult and
unsustainable, irrespective of any technical and economic considerations.

Availability of appropriate experience
The issue of availability of appropriate experience is related to the issues on availability
of appropriate expertise and availability of appropriate disposal technology as discussed
earlier. Therefore it can be judged in a similar way.

Availability of appropriate equipment and facilities and availability of appropriate
disposal premises
The issues of appropriate equipment and facilities and availability of appropriate disposal
premises are related to and parallel those of availability of appropriate disposal
technology, human resources, and experience, and can be judged as outlined above.

Types and condition of the obsolete pesticide stocks, locations of the pesticide stock sites,
and quantities of obsolete pesticide stocks
The issues on types and condition of the obsolete pesticide stocks, locations of the
pesticide stock sites, and quantities of obsolete pesticide stocks that need to be disposed
of can be judged as discussed with respect to safeguarding issues. It is noteworthy that, in
the long term, there is a need to support efforts on development of hazardous waste
management national capacity. One way to achieve this is by way of supporting local




                                             95
academic institutions that offer pertinent training programmes. Another is by using
projects like this as training opportunities.

It is important to note that the country’s capacity to carry out disposal of the obsolete
pesticides being assessed here will actually involve: i) transportation of the pesticides, ii)
treatment and final disposal of the obsolete pesticides, iii) cleaning and management of
empty pesticide containers, and iv) management of washwater and trace quantities of
pesticides resulting from washing the used pesticide containers. Transportation of the
pesticides has a local as well as an external component, but both require appropriately
outfitted transportation vehicles. Marine as well as land transport requires drivers who are
specially trained to transport dangerous goods. Although, both the appropriately outfitted
vehicles and specially trained drivers are currently not available in Tanzania, Tanzania
has a national institute of transport (NIT). NIT specialises in transport training, including
training of drivers and mechanics. Therefore, it is conceivable that NIT can run tailor-
made training for project drivers.

Incineration of the pesticides in local cement kilns is not considered for managing the
large stocks because, judged on previous experiences, such operations are likely to incite
resistance and protest from communities nearby. It is noteworthy that these facilities are
located in rather densely populated areas.

Used metal and plastic containers can be washed using water plus approriate solvents and
the resulting clean containers can be crushed or shredded so that they can be recycled in
the local metal and plastic recycling industry. Notably, Tanzania has a sizeable metal and
plastic recycling capacity. It also has a healthy market for recycled metal and plastic
products.

Paper or cardboard containers such as boxes can be incinerated. It is envisioned that
traces of pesticides in the washwater resulting from washing used pesticide containers
will be concentrated and incinerated in kilns. The resulting relatively pesticide-free water
will be treated as wastewater. Facilities for concentrating the pesticide traces, which
constitute mainly tailor made columns packed with appropriate sorption media, can be set
up relatively easily even using largely local components.

In spite of the assertions made in the preceding discussion regarding the treatment and
disposal of OP containers and washwater, consultations with OP management
stakeholders have indicated that management of the empty containers within Tanzania is
not favoured and is likely to attract resistance. As such, its inclusion in further
discussions is only for completeness of such discussions.

Taking into account all major determinants and influencing factors, the current obsolete
pesticides transport, treatment, and disposal capacity of the country can be broadly
assessed as shown in Table 4.10.




                                             96
Table 4.10: Broad assessment of country’s current obsolete pesticide transport, treatment
and disposal capacity
 Disposal operation component         Required/preferred               Capacity
                                        activity location
                                        In          Outside     Internal     External
                                    Tanzania       Tanzania
 Pesticide land transport              Yes            Yes         Low           High
 Pesticide marine transport            No             Yes         Low           High
 Pesticide treatment and disposal      No             Yes         Low           High
 Washing of used containers            Yes            No          High          High
 Size reduction of used containers     Yes            No          Low           High
 Used container transport              Yes            No          High          High
 Used container treatment/disposal     Yes            No          High          High
 Management of washwater               Yes            No          High          High

The marine transport component in Tanzania is neglected because it is in small scale.

Generally, Table 4.10 indicates that land and marine transport of the obsolete pesticides
must be carried out both locally and internationally by an international contractor. On the
other hand, pesticide treatment must be done outside Tanzania by an international
contractor. In contrast, washing of used containers, size reduction of used containers,
used container transport, used container treatment and disposal, and management of
washwater can be done in Tanzania. It is also conceivable that these activities can be
carried out by Tanzanian contractors and consultants too. Inspite of the preceding
conclusion, it is important to note that the feasibility of management of pesticide
contaminated empty containers and washwater in Tanzania by national contractors faces
a number of challenges. One noteworthy challenge pertains to the length of time it will
take the national contractors to mobilize the required human and material resources and
be ready for full scale operations. Another challenge is the resistance expressed by OP
management stakeholders regarding management of pesticide contaminated empty
containers and washwater as discussed earlier.




                                            97
                                  CHAPTER FIVE
5.0 ENVIRONMENTAL AND SOCIAL IMPACTS

5.1. Introduction

Since the introduction of chemical pesticides in the 1940s and 1950s to control disease
and crop production, they have also caused many, varied and widespread adverse impacts
on human health and the environment. Examples of these adverse impacts include death
and disability among pesticide users and the communities around them, global transport
and bio-accumulation of pesticides, contamination of local water supplies and loss of
income to farmers whose produce contains unacceptable levels of pesticide residues.

Improved understanding of the health and environmental hazards associated with
pesticides has led to the development of sophisticated regulatory and control systems
designed to control pesticide trade, management and use. Examples include the
Rotterdam and Stockholm Conventions, the International Code of Conduct on the
Distribution and Use of Pesticides and the OECD Pesticides Working Group.
Furthermore many other mechanisms designed to address chemical management include
pesticides among their concerns.

Nevertheless, the problems caused by pesticide mismanagement persist with huge
impacts in developing countries. Where agriculture is the mainstay of the economy,
pesticides constitute the majority of chemicals in use. The infrastructure controlling and
managing pesticides throughout their life-cycle are often weak and under-resourced, and
the end users of pesticides are usually untrained and poorly equipped to use them safely.
As a result, 99 percent of the estimated three million people poisoned by pesticides each
year are in poor countries and the most serious and persistent problems associated with
environmental contamination from pesticides are also in the developing world.

Mismanagement has also led to the gradual creation of problems that appear to be
unsolved such as the accumulation of vast quantities of pesticides that have become
unusable over the years, and contamination from pesticides that cannot be removed from
the environment.

Stockpiles of obsolete pesticides (OP) are often in a severely deteriorated condition,
poorly stored and located close to habitation or water supplies, and thus represent a
serious risk to human health, ground and surface water, land use, and the environment in
general. The impact is often greatest on the poor as abandoned pesticide stockpiles and
dumps tend to be located in poorer communities where people scavenge for recyclable
and/or reusable items with no awareness of the dangers involved.




                                                                                       98
5.2    Obsolete Pesticides in Tanzania

According to the inventory database, Tanzania has about 700 tons of obsolete pesticides,
3.6 tones of contaminated materials, 1.2 tones of contaminated equipments, 2755.2 of
contaminated soils, 2000 kg of contaminated building materials, and about 382 empty
containers. The stores were classified into three categories of high, medium and low risk
based on toxicity, quantity, surrounding environmental conditions and the security of the
store. The categorization of the stores followed the FAO Environmental Management
Toolkit in which Environmental Factor (Fe) values higher than 50 have been considered
problematic. A criterion related to the risk posed by the type and quantity of the OP (Fp)
was also used whereby a value of above 50,000 was considered to be a high risk factor. In
addition to the WHO class, the condition of the packaging and whether the active
ingredient is known was considered during the evaluation. A total of 9 stores were
classified as high risk, which are found at Tropical Pesticides Research Institute (TPRI);
Morogoro Farmers Cooperative Union (MOFACU); Highland Mbarali Estates Farm;
Tumbi Agricultural Research Institute; Lushoto DALDO; Vikuge in the Coast Region;
Uyole Research Farm; Utete former agricultural store; Kingori village which include
Ngyani Cooperative Society in Arumeru district.

The overall obsolete disposal plan has three main components of safeguarding,
transferring to collection centers and transporting the obsolete pesticides from the
collection centers to the shipping port. Therefore, the environmental and social impacts
analysis will cover two main phases that is: (i) prevailing store conditions before project
execution; and (ii) store condition during safeguarding and disposal operation.

5.3    Impacts as per existing conditions of the stores before safeguarding

In order to establish environmental and social impacts a total of 16 stores were visited
which included seven high risk sites, which are at TPRI, Lushoto DALDO, Vikuge
Contaminated Site, Morogoro Farmers Cooperative Union, ARI Tumbi, Highland
Mbarali Estates and Uyole Research Farm. The Medium priority risk sites visited include
Tengeru Plant Health Services Centre, TCB Kurasini, Vingunguti PHS, Kimara Baruti,
Rufiji/Utete Site and Mahonda Sugar Factory in Zanzibar; while the low risk sites visited
were TBA Kilimo, KATC, and GBP (in Tanga). This section dwells on environmental
and social impacts from the OP stores and contaminated sites that prevailed before any
safeguarding under ASP. Detailed information on the GBP site in Tanga is given in
Appendix A. Summary of the impacts of the 16 visited stores including their Fp and Fe
risk factors is depicted in Table 5.1

5.3.1 Environmental Impacts in high risk stores

Tropical Pesticides Research Institute (TPRI)

The site is located at Tropical Pesticides Research Institute (TPRI) found in Ngaramtoni
area, Arusha region, about 12 km from the Arusha City. The site area is flat and covered



                                                                                        99
with exotic pine tree species. The TPRI site has a total of five stores but only store No.4
and No.5 were visited. TPRI has a total of 23 tons of obsolete pesticide of variety of
pesticides including DDT, Malathion, Actellic, Kloben, Difolatan, Dyban, Trimidan,
Dyfonate, Azosan, TEWCOR, Trysben, DNOC, Dynotox (UC), Risella (UC), Dieldrin
Solution Vesso (UC) and Arelon.

The first store visited is in very good condition in terms of roofing materials, floor, walls,
windows and doors (Plate 5.1). The pesticide containers are safely stacked on shelves or
pallets or in drums, jerry cans, bottles and boxes with varying capacities ranging from 0.5
to 200 litres for liquids; and 1kg to 100kg for solids respectively. About 4000 liters of
DNOC were repacked in salvage drums in the first store (Plates 5.1a). In the second store
that was visited most of the containers were not sealed intact and some damaged and
broken thus giving a bad smell. Pesticides in this store are kept haphazardly (Plate 5.1b).
In terms of security, the site where the store is located is completely fenced with a gate
that is lockable. The size of the first store is 20m long and 10m wide respectively. A
storekeeper assigned to manage the store regularly checks the pesticides containers at
least once a week. A security guard is available 24 hours a day. In terms of safety,
neither fire safety equipment were visible, nor the first aid kits or PPE for the storekeeper
to use.

As already indicated, the stocks are located within the TPRI complex within secure
stores. The main impacts will be on workers due to inhalation of gases that are released
by the obsolete pesticides. This is mainly attributed to leaking pesticides whereby
offensive smell could be felt in the vicinity of the stores especially when doors are open.




Plate 5.1: TPRI store as seen from outside




                                                                                          100
Plate 5.1a: Salvage drums at TPRI




Plate 5.1b: Variety of pesticides in the second store at TPRI.

Morogoro Farmers Cooperative Union (MOFACU)

MOFACU site is situated in Morogoro Municipality adjacent to Morogoro Railway
Station, about 200 km from Dar es Salaam. The site has two stores containing free
standing obsolete pesticides. In adjacent location, the site contains contaminated soil and
buried associated waste. Both sites are located within a gently sloping land towards the
residential blocks near railway station. Downstream from the site at a distance of about
300 metres is the Mji mpya stream that feeds into Ngerengere River. The visited store
was roofed, waterproofed and completely walled with iron sheets (Plate 5.2). The types
of pesticides found in the area include cotoran and unknown pesticides. Most of the
pesticides are kept in 5 litres plastic containers and some in boxes and bottles of various
sizes (Plate 5.2a). The floor is broken and the pesticides containers are randomly placed
on floor. Spillage of obsolete pesticides was observed due to deterioration of some
containers and improper storage of the containers inside store. A Security guard is
engaged to look after the store. Deep smell emerges from the store when the door is



                                                                                       101
opened. Faint smell is felt around the vicinity of the pesticides contaminated site. The site
is fenced and danger signs posted around the site.




Plate 5.2: MOFACU Store 1.

Human settlements could be found about 500 metres away. Railway station is about 50
metres away from the site. Vegetable cultivation takes place along the Mji mpya stream.
The closest garden downstream is situated at about 300 metres from the site.




Plate 5.2b: Vegetable farm at Mji mpya stream, 300m from the site.

Due to its proximity to human activities and a river system this site has a high potential
for environmental and social impacts. The environmental impacts include the pollution of



                                                                                         102
the Mjimpya stream, exposure of local community to the pesticides including railway
station workers through vapours released by the pesticides or by direct contacts with
leaked pesticides.

Tumbi Agricultural Research Institute

Tumbi site is found at the Tumbi Agricultural Research Institute, Urambo district in
Tabora Region. The pesticides are kept in a substandard store which is cracked as
demonstated in Plate 5.3. Pesticides are partly covered with polythene sheets (Plate 5.3a).
The leaking pesticides produce offensive smell that creates unfriendly environment
around the site. The store is not fenced and there is no security guard specifically
assigned to this site.




Plate 5.3: Tumbi pesticides store




Plate 5.3a Pesticides covered by plastic sheet at Tumbi store




                                                                                       103
The area around the store is covered with forests extending southwards from the site.
Tumbi College is situated on north-eastern side while college workers are residing in
south-western side of the store. Formerly, people used to graze cattle within forest area
around the store; a practice which is now prohibited.




Plate 5.3b: Tumbi store with a poster restricting people from grazing around

The site is a high risk area with a potential for adverse environmental and social impacts.
Leakage and generation of harmful vapours can expose the workers and other local
community members to toxic pesticides. Furthermore, leakage of pesticides can lead to
ecosystem contamination including ground water. Local communities within the vicinity
of the store keep livestock, which can easily graze on contaminated forage material
despite the restriction given not to graze livestock around the store.


Highlands Mbarali Estate

Mbarali pesticides store (at the Highlands Mbarali Estate) is situated about 800 km from
Dar es Salaam at the Mbarali rice farms in Rujewa District, Mbeya Region. The store has
a large quantity of pesticides contributing to 60% of the total amount identified in the
entire Southern Highlands Zone. The Mbarali store is fully walled and roofed with iron
sheets. Its floor however, was showing signs of deterioration before being upgraded. The
store is 9 m long, 4.5 m wide and 2.5m high. Mbarali store contains old stocks of various
pesticides some of which are unknown. The known pesticides are as follows: Ketazine
(4110 kg), BHD (12.50 kg) and BHC (2112.25 kg). The pesticides are kept on floor
(without being on pallets). Some obsolete pesticides are kept in salvage drums while
others are in plastic gunny bags, glass bottles and wooden boxes. Some of the pesticides
containers are leaking and contaminating the floor (Plate 5.4a-b).




                                                                                       104
Plate 5.4: Highland Mbarali Store




Plate 5.4a: Pesticides in salvage drums




                                          105
Plate 5.4b: Leaking pesticides in Mbarali store

The topography where the site is located is relatively flat plain with gentle gradient
towards the rice farm. The surrounding land is used for agricultural purposes mostly
paddy cultivation. The site is very close to irrigation canal and about 30 metres from fish
ponds.

Potential impacts related to this store include the exposure of workers and surrounding
community to hazardous chemicals due to leakage of the pesticides. However, inspite of
the close proximity of the OP store to the fish ponds, it is unlikely for the stored OPs to
get into it because: 1) the OPs are stored in a secure storage building 2) the fish ponds are
provided with a concrete liner, and 3) the OP storage building and the fish ponds are
separated by an irrigation canal which is lined with concrete slabs that are in good
condition.

Uyole Research Farm

The store at the Uyole Research Farm is located in the Uyole Agricultural Research
Institute and Training centre in Mbeya Region about 14 km from Mbeya City. The store
size is 20 m long and 10 m wide with impermeable floor. The site is fenced. The top half
of the walls are constructed using wire mesh material that allows light and air to circulate
in and outside the store. The store accommodates very old stocks of pesticides
comprising Didimac, Kynakil, Alandrin, Pentachlorophenol Natrium, 2,4-D and
Helmamine 720 EC. Some of the stocks are stacked in shelves and others are on floor
(Plate 5.5a) while other containers are leaking thus contaminating the materials and
equipments as well as the floor and walls of the store. The store also has repackaged
mixed powder pesticides stored in plastic sacks (Plate 5.5b).

Plate 5.5: Uyole farm store

                                                     107
Living quarters and office accommodation are found within the ARI- Uyole site,
including students hostels, teachers’ quarters and offices which are located not far from
the store. Agricultural activities are also conducted around the vicinity of the site. The
leaking pesticides produce offensive odour around the vicinity of the store. This can
affect the students and workers within the institute.

The potential impacts from the Uyole stores include direct health impacts to workers and
students who can be easily get exposed to the obsolete pesticides. Others are surface and
ground water pollution due to leaking obsolete pesticides and introduction of obsolete
pesticides into the food chain due to the farming activities that are carried out within the
vicinity of the improperly managed stores.

Lushoto DALDO

The site is located at the office of the Agriculture and Livestock Development Officer for
the Lushoto District in Tanga Region. The site contains buried pesticides. The burial site
is adjacent to the DALDO office.




Figure 5.6: Lushoto DALDO buried pesticide site


The store is generally in a bad condition and the pesticides, especially blue copper, are
not safeguarded. In view of this, the site has a high potential for environmental and social


                                                                                        108
impacts. Leakage and generation of bad odour can expose the workers and nearby local
community members to toxic pesticides. Sugarcane grown around the store is prone to
contamination by pesticides (Figure 5.6b). Since the soil is highly contaminated, there is
a possibility of pollution of groundwater from buried pesticides (Figure 5.6a).




Figure 5.6a: Blue copper as excavated from the buried site beside Lushoto DALDO store

             Lushoto.




                                                                                      109
Vikuge Contaminated site

Vikuge contaminated site is located in Kibaha District in Pwani Region. The site
originally had a stockpile of DDT amounting to 250 tons abandoned in the open yard.
The stock was later repacked and moved into a secure storage. The area which is highly
contaminated with DDT was subsequently fenced to prevent access.




Figure 5.7a: Vikuge store – front view




Figure 5.7b: Vikuge site – inside store



                                                                                  110
Figure 5.7c: DDT Contaminated soil at Vikuge




Figure 5.7d: Soil saturated with DDT at Vikuge site



                                                      111
The contaminated area, which is located upstream, is largely a flatland gently sloping
towards the north where Vikuge village is situated. At the contaminated site, strong smell
of DDT can be felt with visible DDT powder scattered on the soil surface. Many bottle
caps from DDT containers were also observed. The predominant type of soil is sandy
soil, which normally facilitate fast movement of contaminants since it has high hydraulic
conductivity. DDT bags were stored in the open air and clearly significant amounts of
DDT powder remain on the soil. McDowall (2009a) reported that DDT has been detected
in nearby drinking wells and therefore the contaminant and its metabolites have likely
entered into the food chain. Agenda (2005) observed that the level of contamination of
water and sediments by pesticides and metabolites appears to be related to point source
contamination by pesticide residues from the heavily contaminated Vikuge site. Vikuge
Village entirely depends on water from seasonal streams and wetlands, boreholes and
local wells even though there is tap water which is not reliable. The findings of
McDowall (2009a) and (Agenda, 2005) suggest potential high risks and cause for public
health concerns.

5.3.2 Environmental Impacts in Medium Risk Stores

Vingunguti Site

The site is located in Ilala Municipal Council within the City of Dar es Salaam, about 8
km from the city centre on the highway to Mwl Julius Nyerere International Airport. The
store is owned by MAFS&C and is still in use as intermediate storage site for pesticides
to be distributed to other regions. The safeguarded obsolete pesticides in drums are kept
in the roofed passage outside main store. Types of the pesticides found are Atrazine,
Fenvelarate, Pirimiphos- methyl and brodifacoum. The stocks were safeguarded and
packed in 2003 by NEMC. However, some drums containing pesticides and other waste
chemicals are rusting and leaking (Plate 5.6). The leaked chemical drains into the ground
and other furniture including electronic wastes such as computer parts. Other empty
packages of waste product are piled up outside the main store. Faint smell is detected
around the vicinity of the storage site. The site is surrounded by high density residential
area, Vingunguti market and industrial area.

This store is located in a very high density area close to an abattoir. The area has a high
water table and there are several shallow wells that are used by the residents.
Nonetheless, based on the Fp and Fe criteria, the Vingunguti site poses a medium risk.




                                                                                       112
Plate 5.6: Pesticides repacked in drums at Vingunguti store


Tanzania Cotton Board (TCB) Kurasini Site

The TCB store is found in Temeke Municipal Council, Mivinjeni area-Kurasini, within
the City of Dar es Salaam, about 7km from the city centre. The store is actually a
warehouse No.7, which formerly was used by the Tanzania Cotton Board (TCB) to store
cotton. The size of the store is 62m long and 38m wide with a concrete floor. At present,
there is no cotton in this godown except the pesticides and other associated wastes. The
types of pesticides found in the store are Bromadiolone, Atrazine, Fluometuron, Laddok,
Thiabendazole and other unknown obsolete pesticides and waste chemicals. Some of the
containers including paper bags and boxes are completely torn-out and the polythene
sheets that formerly covered the products are also completely deteriorated (Plate 5.7a and
5.7b).


The site is well protected as the entry is fully controlled. The site is surrounded by other
godowns, TCB offices and the commercial buildings. The impacts in this store are not
high due to the fact that the store is much secured and there is very little probability of the
OP finding its way into the water bodies or flora because the store floor is very secure.

Kimara Baruti Site

The site is found at Kimara area-Kinondoni Municipality within the City of Dar es

The two stores are of equal size, fenced and roofed with corrugated iron sheets and with
secure walls of cement blocks (Plate 5.8). The sizes of the two stores are 18m long and
11m wide. Store no. 58 contains chemicals while store no. 59 contains few pesticides
which are packed in steel drums (Plate 5.8b). In store No. 58 the following pesticides are
found: Fluazifop-P-butyl, BHC Lindane, Brodifacoum and other unknown pesticides and
waste chemicals. Store No. 59 contain pirimiphos-methyl and other unknown pesticides.
The two stores are well secured. However they are poorely ventilanted, resulting into
being very hot inside. There is also poor lighting inside. In store No 58 the pesticides are
kept in sisal sacks, boxes and glass bottles (Plate 5.8a). Deep smell was felt inside and
around the vicinity of the stores. Since the stores are well secured, the negative
environmental and social impacts are low.

Mahonda Sugar Factory (MSF) Site

litres drums suspected to contain sulphuric acid were also present. Plates 5.9 to 5.9b show

Kilimanjaro Agricultural Training College (KATC) Site

The KATC site is found in Chekereni village within the premises of Kilimanjaro
Agricultural Training College (KATC). The college is a government owned institution
situated about 16 km from Moshi Town. The site has three stores where pesticides are
kept. In addition there is one location where drums containing pesticides are buried. Two
of the stores at the KATC are well built and secure while the third store is crumpling
down. The three stores at KATC, which include a steel container, contain a variety of
known and unknown pesticides (Plate 5.12). Pesticides are contained in steel drums and
some in aluminum drums and polythene bags. Some drums are broken and rusting and
therefore leaking their contents on the floor as seen in Plates 5.12a. Smell was detected
at the buried site as well as around the vicinity of the store and the steel container. The
site is fenced and guarded, therefore not easily accessible by outsiders. The access road
has rough surface but passable in all seasons. Agricultural activities are being conducted
in the surrounding area.

Tanzania Building Agency (TBA) Kilimo Site

The Tanzania Building Agency (TBA) store situated off road in Moshi Town is owned by
TBA. The store is roofed with corrugated iron sheets and walled with cement blocks. The
floor of the store is crumpling down. The size of the store is 13 m in long and 7 m wide.
Apart from containing obsolete DDT, the store also contains timber, furniture and
different kinds of solid waste such as scrap metal, boxes and paper (Plate 5.13a and
5.13b). The store is not well ventilated and there is irritating smell of DDT and a lot of
dust. There are a lot of activities going on adjacent to the store, including those associated
with a container that was formerly used to keep a mixture of urea and mollases, as well as
furniture manufacturing and timber selling. In addition, food vendors cook and sell their
food just outside the store and the residential houses are hardly ten meters from the store.
The workers close to the store claimed that they sometimes feel headache when they
spend a long time in close proximity to the site. Despite the gloomy picture painted by the
preceding descriptions, Fp and Fe values classify the TBA site as a medium risk one.
Nonetheless, it is recommended that this site is given a high priority among the medium
risk site.

5.4      Disposal Operation Impacts

A variety of environmental and social impacts are likely to occur during safeguarding,
transportation and final disposal of the obsolete pesticides. These are appropriately
presented in Chapter Six.

5.5      Views and Concerns of Local Communities on Obsolete Pesticides

5.5.1 Overview

In order to gauge concerns of the local communities and the potential of triggering the
World Bank Operational Policy OP 4.12 on Involuntary Resettlement a special field visit
was organized using four high risk sites as samples. The sampled sites were Vikuge in the
Coastal zone, Morogoro Farmers Cooperative Union in Central zone, HighlandMbarali
Estates Store and Agriculture research Institute in Southern Highlands zone. The main
stakeholders consulted at each site were a composite of ordinary members of the
communities that included farmers, livestock keepers, school teachers, health and local
government officers. However the actual composition of community members who
attended the meeting differ from one site to another depending on the local conditions
and the status of the site. The main method used for the data gathering at each site was
public meetings in which the communities were initially given a brief explanation on the
clean up and disposal programme for ASP and thereafter invited to give their views. On
average each meeting took about four hours. Plate 5.15, 5.15a and 5.15b show public
meetings at Vikuge, MOFACU and Uyole respectively. Based on the public consultations
the findings can be grouped into general and specific.

5.5.2 General findings

Two main general findings were identified, firstly on awareness of the existence of
obsolete pesticides and fatigue of public consultations on obsolete pesticides effects. The
level of awareness on obsolete pesticide problems differ in the four sites. The level of
awareness was highest at the Viguke site followed by both MOFACU and Uyole sites. It
was lowest at Mbarali site probably due to the fact that the site is privately owned and
local community is not directly exposed to it. The awareness is high at Viguke because
the site has been widely discussed in the local press and some NGOs are involved in
creating awareness on obsolete pesticides in the area. However, most of the communities
interviewed indicated that they have limited knowledge on pesticides, their usage and
effects. Due to lack of knowledge among community members, many could not give
concrete recommendations arguing that they believe the government has enough
knowledgeable people who can propose on how best to handle the problems of obsolete
pesticides. However, few of them were fairly informed of the effects of pesticides and
suggested that the following should be considered during safeguarding and disposal
operations:

      o Use of appropriate PPE



                                                                                       127
   o Workers be given milk to reduce the effects from dust (Not all kind of toxicity can
     be neutralized by milk).
   o Awareness campaign prior to commencing safeguarding operation

At Vikuge site community were aware on some mitigation that could be taken to avoid
OP contamination. These included avoidance to pass through or grazing on the pesticide
contaminated area. However, they admitted that before the area was fenced they used to
pass and even graze in the contaminated area. At Uyole, Mbarali and Morogoro the
communities did not take particular precautions as obsolete pesticides were confined in
closed stores.




Plate 5.15: Public meeting at Vikuge




                                                                                    128
Plate 5.15a: Public meeting at MOFACU in Morogoro




Plate 5.15b: Public Meeting at Uyole


It is worth mentioning that during the meetings it became clear that some participants
were tired of being consulted on the same issue for many years now. They reported that
the Government in the past has informed them on the plan to remove obsolete pesticides
from their areas (about more than 10 years ago). The plan is not implemented as yet and
instead more and more meetings are still being held on the same issue. Due to this, some
people could not come to the meetings because of being tired of hearing the same issue
repeated over-and-over again without any action.




                                                                                    129
5.5.3 Specific findings

Several specific issues were raised by the communities, which include immediate
removal of the OP, compensation of the properties, environmental impacts operations and
employment opportunities during safeguarding. On quick removal of the OP, the
communities are looking forward for the OP to be finally removed. However, all the
communities interviewed are worried about the mpacts of the disposal operations on their
properties. Especially if project could lead to the demolitions of houses and some public
facilities including water supply. The communities would like the government to
compensate all the properties that will be affected during disposal operations and that has
been affected over years due to the presence of obsolete pesticides. On environmental
impacts, the communities were concerned with the dust that could be generated during
the implementation of the project. They proposed that the contractors must provide means
of protection from the generated dust. The communities also wanted to benefit from
employment opportunities that will arise from the project activities. This means that the
contractor should provide employment opportunities for the local communities. Finally,
the communities showed readiness to temporarily shifting to other locations during the
implementation phase if it will be necessary. They recommended that they should be
given prior notice of at least three months.

Based on the public consultations it is apparent that the safeguarding operations will not
lead to resettlements as long as the mitigation measures are fully adhered to during the
implementation phase. The main issue especially at Vikuge site relates to actions that
should be taken on the environmental elements that have already been affected by the
presence of the obsolete pesticides.

5.6    Impacts on natural habitats

Surveys have shown that areas in vicinity of the OP stores and contaminated sites have
already been disturbed by human activities and as such have lost their natural habitats
status. Therefore, this project will not trigger OP 4.04 on Natural Habitats.




                                                                                       130
                                   CHAPTER SIX

6.0 ENVIRONNEMENTAL MANAGEMENT PLAN (EMP)

6.1    General Context

Information and specific data on quantities of OPs, OP associated waste, and pesticide
contaminated sites in Tanzania have been presented and discussed in detail in Chapter
Four (See Tables 4.2 – 4.7 and Figures 4.1 – 4.2f). Evidently, the amount of OPs is
substantial and as such requires intervention for safeguarding public health and the
environment. However, as presented in Chapter Four, the analysis of the OP data show
that only a small amount (maximum 0.5%) of the total amount of OPs in Tanzania is
classified as Class 1a and1b. This fact will allow for easier intervention and demand
much less stringent safety precautions during safeguarding. Regarding the 2,000 kg of
contaminated building material and components (Table 4.2), the highly contaminated
portion will be removed from its respective sites and packed for incineration which will
be undertaken by a disposal contractor. The 600 tons of heavily contaminated soil could
also be considered for disposal in the same way as the OPs by the disposal contractor.
The portions of the contaminated soil and contamination building materials and
components that are not highly contaminated can be disposed of in an engineered landfill.
In addition to being properly lined and having multiple provisions for pollutant migration
prevention, the site for the landfill should not be prone to natural or anthropogenic agents
that might compromise the integrity of the landfill.

Chapter Two and Three provide the relevant background information on Tanzania in
terms of biophysical and socio-economic, policy, legal and institutional frame work
respectively. The biophysical and socio-economic background information shows that the
stores containing the OPs are found in all 26 regions of the country and can affect
different ecological systems and population centers. A close look, however, shows that
most of the obsolete pesticides are concentrated in the coastal zone, in regions along the
highways from Dar es Salaam through Morogoro, Iringa and Mbeya, plus the regions in
the north eastern part of the country. Small quantities are found in the regions around
Lake Victoria and in the southern and western zones of the country. The stores are
located in different types of local environments including built-up areas of high
population density and in isolated rural settings. In addition, the biophysical background
also shows that some areas in the country where stores are located do not have very good
roads and other important infrastructure including specially equipped hospitals that can
be used for emergency operations. Therefore, in the preparation of the EMP these
constraints were taken into account.

Chapter Three shows that Tanzania has the policy, legal and institutional framework for
dealing with OPs. The critical aspect is the limited capacity to fully implement the policy,
legal and administrative requirements. Chapter Five shows the environmental and social
risks that can be associated with the disposal of the OPs. The environmental risks include
the pollution of surface and ground water, exposure of flora and fauna partly through



                                                                                        131
farming or grazing activities and direct health impacts to workers and the local
communities through indirect or direct exposure to OPs.

6.2      Objective

The objective of the Environmental Management Plan (EMP) for this CESA is to provide
a strategy for the management of OPs with a specific focus on safeguarding and final
disposal of the OPs and pesticide contaminated empty containers in order to reduce the
impacts and risks posed by poor management of OPs throughout Tanzania.

The EMP covers all aspect related to the safeguarding and disposal operations such as the
prioritisation of sites to be safeguarded, the identification of suitable main collection
centres; repackaging operations including registration; transport; storage and also
addresses the final disposal options (local/international) of the OP including compliance
with relevant international chemical conventions, codes of practice and agreements (e.g.
the Basel Convention; IMDG Code and ADR Agreement; among others).

To ensure that all current and potential future impacts of the OP stores and contaminated
sites are covered, the EMP will be implemented in two steps. Step 1 will focus on
mitigation of effects of the current situation while Step 2 will focus on mitigation of
effects of safeguarding and disposal operations.

To prevent public health and environmental hazards from getting out of hand at OP stores
and contaminated sites that are in critical conditions, emergency safeguarding will be
carried out while awaiting the main safeguarding and disposal measures.


6.3      Environmental Management Plan Background and Considerations

The EMP builds up on the general approach described in the ASP Operational Manual
particularly on sections under Safeguarding and Disposal; together with the findings of
Chapters One to Four of this report and especially the analysis of the data on OPs in
Chapter 4. Important details on the development of the EMP are presented in Section 1.6.

The EMP has been prepared by adopting an interactive approach including the following
steps. The first stage is to take the information presented under Chapters One to Five of
this report and provide details on how the potential risk posed by obsolete pesticides will
be reduced or eliminated.

This is presented with the following:

      (i) Safeguarding Strategy
      (ii) Disposal Strategy
      (iii)Activities related to safeguarding




                                                                                       132
6.4     Mitigation Measures for Current Impacts

Before undertaking the main operation of safeguarding and disposal of Ops, and its
associated waste, it is a matter of urgency to implement emergency measures in specific
sites among the high risk sites identified. These measures include the fencing off the area
and posting of warning signs to keep passersby off the site.

Emergency and safeguarding measures

Before the main planned safeguarding operations commence, emergence safeguarding
measures need to be taken to put an end to health and environmental risks from
deteriorated OP storage facilities.The emergence safeguarding measures will be similar to
the ones previously carried out by NEMC at various OP stores in the country. They
include repackaging of chemicals whose containers are deteriorated; fencing off OP
stores and pesticide contaminated areas; and rehabilitation of seriously dilapidated OP
stores. The types and extents of the emergence safeguarding measures will be assessed on
a case by case basis using data obtained from surveys carried out by NEMC nationwide
previously.

As previously done, the emergence safeguarding measures will be undertaken by ASP
PMU staff and done by trained operators. Table 6.1 illustrates the emergency
safeguarding measures that can be taken with respect to a selection of stores.

6.5       Strategy

6.5.1 Safeguarding activities

The below proposed strategy for management of the OP follows the approach which has
been introduced and FAO and the World Bank in similar cases in Africa and Eastern
Europe. It includes the following activities:

      o   Safeguarding – moving OP from current stores to central stores
      o   Repackaging – if required repackaging will take place
      o   Transportation of containers packaged in accordance with international standards
      o   Disposal – disposal through high temperature incineration at approved
          installations

Ideally, safeguarding of OP stocks must involve proper containerization and labeling of
OPs, cleaning these containers off the pesticides, and placing these containers in a safe
and secure place that would meet the international requirements for safe storage of
hazardous wastes/pesticides.

The decision on whether or not to repackage OPs, will be based on:

          Condition of containers
          Size of containers


                                                                                                  134
       Type and quality of containers (for example, OPs in containers made of delicate
       materials that are liable to leak will be repackage whereas OPs in robust
       containers will not necessarily be repackaged).

However, where information on the condition and quality of OP containers is not
conclusive, the OP has to be repackaged. It should be recognized that new containers will
need to be provided to replace leaky containers. Consequently, the pesticide contaminated
leaky containers will need to be disposed of alongside the pesticide contaminated empty
containers.

The main activities that will be involved in safeguarding will be: verification of the
stocks, repacking (liquid, solid), transportation (loading, transport, unloading), storage at
central store, containerization, and final transport.

Regarding the current situation of stocks in stores, repackaging is planned for stocks
currently stored at high risk stores depicted in Table 6.2.

6.5.2   Safeguarding strategy

It was considered advantageous to establish OP main collection centres (MCC) to which
OP stocks from other stores would be transferred for safer storage and convenient
transportation during subsequent management stages of the OP stocks. The rationale for
moving the OPs from the individual stores to the central stores (or collection centres) can
be summed up as:

- To facilitate security because it is easier to secure few than many stores.
- To facilitate final transportation of the OPs by consolidating their storage.
- To transfer OPs from stores that are not suitable for storing OPs because of unsuitable
  location and environmental or public health concerns.
- To reduce costs of improving the stores, because it is cheaper and easier to improve
  fewer stores than all the individual stores.

The evaluation of the potential of the available individual stores to be used as main
MCCs was based on a number of key criteria derived from technical and techno-socio-


                                                                                                 136
economic considerations with a view of protecting people and the environment from
being exposed to the hazardous OPs.

The final list of the OP MCCs was derived based on the considerations listed below.
These considerations were used to both qualify suitable stores and eliminate unsuitable
ones:
       Location characteristics of the potential store, including the store not being close
       to water bodies.
       The potential of the store to trigger the World Bank’s OP 4.12 on Involuntary
       Resettlement and OP 4.04 on Natural habitats.
       Accessibility of the store for both transfer of OP stocks from the other stores and
       transfer of OP stocks stored at the MCCs to the shipping port. Accessibility was
       judged on the basis of availability and passability of roads connecting the OP
       source stores and the potential MCCs. To be considered passable, the roads had to
       be accessible throughout the year.
       Centrality of the MCC locations with a view to minimising the overall transport
       distance (roadmaps for the selected MCCs are presented in Appendix 6.1).
       Security of the OP stocks during transport through all transport routes pertinent to
       the MCC.
       Current condition of the store and its potential for improvement to the standard
       suitable for an MCC (for example, having non-leaking roof; impervious floor,
       having walls with no cracks, and having unbroken/fully closable windows).

After evaluating all potential OP stores based on the above considerations, only six (6)
stores listed in Table 6.3 were found to be suitable as MCCs. As is obvious from the table
entries, the selected MCCs were assigned to six geographical zones covering the whole of
Tanzania.

Details on the determination of free space in the stores are available in the FAO
Environmental Management Tool Kit (EMTK) Vol. 2 Toolkit D. The example given
below serves to illustrate the assumptions made and procedure followed.

Solid obsolete pesticides will be packed in flexible intermediate bulk containers with a
capacity of 1m3 (750 kg). Liquid obsolete pesticides will be filled in 200 litre drums.
These packaging could be placed on a standard pallet which occupies an area of 1.25 m2.
We can take the case of Mahonda major collection center as an example. The Mahonda
MCC has a total floor area of 50 m2 and an OP stock of 2.5 tons.

2.5 tons =2500 kg, and 750 kg of OP take up 1.25 m2.

To determine the floor area that will be occupied by 2500 kg of OP, we proceed as
follows:


2500 kg will occupy Af floor area;



                                                                                       137
To allow for free movement for inspection and safety, a margin of 60% of the total area is
recommended. Factoring the 60% margin into the floor area derived above, a total floor
area of approximately 5.33 m2 is obtained in the following way:

sufficient for storing 2.5 tons of OPs, which require about 5.33 m2 of floor area. Similar
calculations for the other MCCs can be done in a similar manner.

Information on OP storage capacities of the MCCs is given in Table 6.3 above. Other
relevant information needed to determine quantities of Ops to be moved to MCCs is
available in the FAO EMTK Volume 2.

Because during the CESA study it was not possible to carry out detailed groundwater
investigations in the vicinity of potential MCCs, the criterion on groundwater protection
was not used in the selection of the MCCs. Therefore, it is imperative that site specific
groundwater protection measures be taken at all MCCs where groundwater quality is
likely to be compromised by the presence of the MCCs. The establishment of the need for
such groundwater protection should be determined before all the other measures are
implemented.

                                                                                                                                                                 142
6.7    Safeguarding Management Aspects

The following activities should be considered when performing the safeguarding of the
OP.

Organisation

    Management
The overall management of the safeguarding operation shall rest with the PMU. The
PMU will be responsible for all liaisons with all relevant stakeholders who are not part of
the safeguarding team. The Project Coordinator will liaise with the Director General of
NEMC to ensure all national legal obligations are met.

    Technical support and supervision
The national safeguarding team shall be supported by the Technical Advisor for Disposal
(TAD). The TAD shall work with the national staff to develop the necessary resource
plan and work plan to ensure safe implementation of the project. Instructions on how to
proceed shall be developed based on FAO procedures and training modules for the
environmentally sound management of obsolete pesticides. The TA will work with the
Safeguarding Team to develop the necessary managerial and supervisory capacity within
the national team to ensure safe implementation of the safeguarding project.

    Site Supervision
The day-to-day supervision of the safeguarding activities shall be undertaken by the
leader of the safeguarding team under the supervision of the Project Manager and the
TAD.

The safeguarding team leader shall be responsible for establishing safe working area
(zones) as described below the maintenance of safe working practices through on-the-
spot supervision of the safeguarding team.

Implementation

The safeguarding activities shall be conducted by an experienced disposal service
contractor in close collaboration with a team of staff from institutions that were
previously involved in the inventory and safeguarding. Further training related to entry
and exit to the working area, zoning of the work place, use of equipment and general
safety procedures shall be conducted before the work commence. During the operations a
nurse with necessary first aid material shall be on site.

The staff members shall be briefed daily on the activities to be completed and allocated
roles and responsibilities by the Safeguarding team leader.




                                                                                       143
Description of work procedures shall be prepared before commencing the safeguarding
operations in order to have a complete picture of what is required during the works.
These shall as a minimum include the following:
   Establishment of site
   Zoning of site
   Repackaging of solid pesticides into new drums
   Pumping of liquid pesticides into new drums
   Sampling of unknown pesticides.
   Labeling and registration
   Store decontamination.

All containers which are used for the safeguarding shall be UN approved. Repackaging
for safeguarding or consolidation for eventual transport shall be done according to the
standard operating procedures specified in the FAO Environmental Management Tool Kit
(EMTK Vol II Tool F).

    Zoning of Site
In order to ensure the safety of workers during the safeguarding operation a procedure for
dividing the area into zones shall be established.

As a minimum the area shall be divided into 3 zones as described below.

Zone 1: Commonly termed the “Hot? or “Dirty? zone

   Characterised by close proximity to hazardous materials;
   Typically high risk of exposure when working;
   Activities focus on repackaging of waste into new containers;
   Control measures for decontamination of workers;
   High levels of supervision and control;
   Close monitoring of workers and work methods based on operating procedures;
   High levels of PPE and environmental protection.

Zone 2: Commonly termed the “Intermediate? or “Buffer? zone

   Lower risk of exposure when working;
   Typically focus on interim storage of repackaged containers;
   May include removal of residual contamination on outside of containers;
   Labelling of new containers;
   Lower levels of PPE and environmental protection.

Zone 3: Commonly termed the “Clean? zone

   No or minimal risk of exposure;
   Typically focus on storage of materials pending removal from site;
   PPE related to handling of new, clean packages;


                                                                                      144
   Use of drum handling equipment to move items to reduce risk.

In connection with the safeguarding activities the following measures shall be taken:
    All management and supervisory staff plus all labourers shall have complete pre and
    post medical inspection including blood analysis. This action provides a documented
    record of the health of all project staff both before and after the implementation of
    safeguarding activities. The medical check also provide a method of assessing the
    application of safe working methods during implementation including the correct use
    of personal protective equipment (PPE) and supervision of labourers by competent
    staff.
    A medical inspection should be carried out on a monthly basis from the beginning of
    the safeguarding
    The site shall be operation.established in such a way that it provides workers’
    facilities for showering, taking breaks and storing their non working clothes.

Alternatively, operation with a Workers’ Decontamination Enclosure System and Waste
and Equipment Decontamination Enclosure System can be adopted. In this option, the
first enclosure will be for worker entry into and exit from the work area whereas the
second enclosure is for decontamination of wastes (containers) and equipment taken out
of the work area.


Management of stores

Management of the stores is important to maintain safe storage of the OP. It shall involve
security, routine inspections, maintenance of the building and the containers.

It is recommended that all stores are equipped with security guards and that a local
representative is appointed as store keeper. The store keeper shall be given the obligation
to report to PMU on a monthly basis regarding the condition and situation at the store.
The estimated manpower cost for the six MCCs is US$ 72,000.

Communications

It is recommended that the purpose and activities related to the safeguarding operations is
communicated to the communities and organizations around the stores to create
awareness on the risks and hazards posed by the stored OP. The PMU shall develop a
communications package to brief the town administration and the surrounding
community of the ongoing operations.

The package should include details such as:
       Description of the work;
       instructions to keep away from the site throughout the period of the work;
       a map showing the area of the work, and the area of restricted access;
       safety instructions of what to do in an emergency (such as a fire at the site)
       emergency contact details of the PMU.


                                                                                        145
Routine and emergency telecommunications will normally be undertaken by mobile
phone. The PMU, TAD and the Safeguarding team leader shall all have mobile phones. A
satellite phone has also to be available during the safeguarding activities in case of
emergency where there is no available mobile network.

Transport

All movement of hazardous waste between the stores will be coordinated and supervised
by the PMU. Specific transport rules shall be applied following ADR rules including:

       Safety Equipment in vehicles
       Driver Training
       Transport documentation including information on hazards and emergency
       actions
       Vehicle Inspections
       Driver Briefing
       Escort vehicle with response equipment
       Speed limit 40 kph on urban routes
       Planned route based on risk assessment

It is noteworthy that, under ideal condition where potential transport routes differ only in
terms of distance, the selection of transport routes could make use of linear programming
with a view to optimising the selection. The use of linear programming, which is
theoretically sound, is practically untenable in the context of this project mainly because
the potential transport routes need to consider security (safety), quality and reliability of
the roads, likelihood of resistance by communities that live along the routes to their use
for OP transportation, availability and reliability of supporting services, and familiarity
with the routes based on available information.

The selection of appropriate routes which do not pass through highly sensitive areas is a
factor which shall be considered when moving the OP. However with the limited number
of roads in the countryside the options are limited.

Disposal

Local Disposal

Disposal operations will involve different types of wastes which may require different
types of treatment to render them harmless. Such wastes include obsolete pesticides,
contaminated soils, old containers, and old equipment and contaminated building
materials. As discussed in Section 4.5, currently, Tanzania does not have a hazardous
waste disposal facility suitable for obsolete pesticides in compliance with the required
emissions standards as defined by European Union and US EPA legislation. Under such
conditions, the only alternative for the disposal of obsolete pesticides and associated



                                                                                         146
waste is to export and dispose of them at a licensed facility overseas in compliance with
the requirements of the Basel and Stockholm Conventions.

International Disposal

Inclusions of international disposal companies in OP disposal operations have been
applied in a number of cases in Africa and Eastern Europe. In Tanzania, NEMC have
such experience from previous disposal operations.

There are a number of mainly European companies who have taken on the task of
collecting, transporting and disposal of OP at dedicated high temperature facilities in
compliance with international regulations. It is considered that all potential companies
provide a safe disposal option.

Environmental and social management activities, their impacts as well as their preventive
and mitigations measures, institutional responsibilities, implementation schedules, and
pertinent cost estimates are outlined in Table 6.5.
