                                                                                                                                                                        151
As a general observation in connection with Table 6.5, all personnel entering the OP
management work areas must have gone through health and safety training, and must
have read the material safety data sheets (MSDS) for the pesticides and learned the
related hazards, the preventive measures, and what to do in case of exposure to the
pesticides. All this is in accordance with EMTK Vol. II.

After safeguarding and removal of OPs, all the OP stores will need to be cleaned and/or
decontaminated as soon as their use comes to an end. In the same vein, vehicles and
equipment used in safeguarding and transportation activities that will remain in the
country should be decontaminated. The MCCs will be cleaned up and decontaminated
using standard practices. Sodium hypochlorite (NaOCl), which is the oxidizing agent of
choice during clean up operations, can be used. MCCs are relatively clean, and as such
can be put to many different uses after clean up and decontamination. It is anticipated that
the cleaned and decontaminated MCCs will be used for pesticide storage.

It is important to note that the OP stores earmarked as MCCs, whose number has already
been determined and is fixed, were evaluated by a consultant with a view to determining
the extent of upgrading required. Except for the one at ARI Tumbi, most stores
containing OPs are in good condition.

The importance of the MCCs cannot be overemphasized. For example, small quantities of
obsolete pesticides from remote sites need to be moved to centralized location for ease of
repackaging into shipping containers in trucks that would otherwise not be able to reach
those stores. On the other hand, large stocks in single stores will be moved directly to the
port of exit by the disposal service contractors.


6.8    Adopted Implementation Strategy

Taking into account all the issues discussed in the preceding subsections of this section
and building up on Chapters Four and Five, the safeguarding, transportation, and final
disposal of the OP will have to encompass a number of features that need to be clearly
spelled out. On the whole, as noted earlier, there will be six (6) main collection centres
(MCCs) to which OP stocks collected from the rest of the OP stores will be transferred.
From the MCCs all stored OP stocks will be hauled to the port to wait for ocean
freighting to their final disposal destination outside the country.

However, since some OP stores are located very close to the Dar es Salaam port, it makes
sense to transport the OP stocks from these stores straight to the port. Additionally, since
some OP stores have OP stocks that are large enough to fill one or more shipping
containers, it also makes sense to transport the OP stocks from these stores straight to the
port as well. Table 6.6a presents information on the Coastal Zone stores whose OP stocks
will be hauled directly to the DSM port. Table 6.6b presents similar information for the
stores in other parts of the Coastal Zone from which OP stocks cannot be taken directly
the the Dar es Salaam port for shipping. Tables 6.6c – 6.6g presents the information on
the stores located in the other zones.

Taking into consideration the chemical nature and current conditions of the OP stocks
and based on findings from Chapter Four, it is apparent that the safeguarding,
transportation, and final disposal of the OP stocks will need to involve several public and
private authorities and entities. Of these the most important are the PMU together with its
teams, international contractors, and local contractors.

The distribution of the roles between the PMU together with its working teams on the one
hand and the disposal service contractor on the other regarding various OP safeguarding
and transportation obligations will be guided by the relationship schematically illustrated
in Figure 6.1.

It is apparent from Figure 6.1 that the PMU together with its working teams are largely
responsible for small OP stocks and even with these the PMU’s responsibility stops at
transportation of the OP to the MCCs. The disposal service contractor is responsible for
handling large OP stocks right from their original stores through the MCC to the final
destination.

The decision to apportion the responsibilities between the PMU and the disposal service
contractor regarding the safeguarding, transportation, and disposal of the OP has
considered the technical competence and manpower capacity of the PMU as well as
logistical needs of this kind of undertaking.

                                CHAPTER SEVEN

7.0 ANALYSIS OF ALTERNATIVES

7.1    Pesticide Safeguarding Alternatives

Safeguarding is required for free standing obsolete pesticide stocks and its associated
wastes; namely, contaminated empty containers, contaminated equipment and pesticide
contaminated soil including buried pesticides three alternatives were considered and
analysed. The alternatives are: i) no safeguarding action, ii) safeguarding at the current
location of each store, and iii) use of collection centres to safeguard pesticides moved
from the currently dispersed individual stores to main collection centres (MCCs). Results
of the Strengths, Weaknesses, Opportunities, and Threats (SWOT) analysis is
summarised in Table 7.1.

It is noteworthy that the option of no safeguarding action does not present any

maintains the status quo whose deficiencies are the very reason for carrying out this

collection centres for safeguarding is the option that is most favourable. This is the one
recommended for adoption in subsequent decisions in this project.

7.2    Pesticide Transport Means Alternatives

To come up with the best option for transporting the obsolete pesticides within the
country from their eventual safeguarding locations to their disposal place or final
shipping port, three alternatives were identified and analysed. The alternatives are: i)
railway transport, ii) road transport, and iii) a combination of railway and road transport.
Results of the analysis are summarised in Table 7.2. For the transportation needs of the
obsolete pesticides outside the country, two alternatives were considered and analysed: i)
air freight and ii) ocean freight. Results of this analysis are summarised in Table 7.3.

The SWOT analysis presented in Table 7.2 indicates that, on the whole, road transport is
the most favourable option for transporting obsolete pesticides from the original stores to
the shipping port in this project. It is noteworthy that the SWOT analysis recognises the
fact that, when railway transport services become reliable, railway transport as well as a
combination of railway and road transport could have advantages for some stores.
However, based on the current state of the two railway lines in Tanzania, railway
transport services are not likely to become reliable in the near future. The central railway
line is short of locomotives and passenger and goods wagons and faces serious
maintenance problems. After a long ownership conflict, it is now under receivership and
as such hardly operational. The Tanzania – Zambia Railway (TAZARA) faces a shortage
of locomotives and wagons. In addition, TAZARA is experiencing serious liquidity
problems that have incapacitated it. In view of the preceding discussion, railway transport
is not a preferred option at the moment. The only option that is currently reliable and
likely to remain so during the execution of this project is road transportation.

7.3     Pesticide and Associated Waste Disposal Alternatives

With a view to objectively determining the best options for the disposal of the obsolete
pesticides and their associated waste, identification of disposal alternatives was done on
the basis of where rather than how the OPs and their associated waste could best be
disposed of. It was assumed that proven and established disposal technologies as well as
appropriately competent and experienced disposal contractors would be employed. The
options for disposing OPs within the country or shipping outside the country for eventual
disposal were considered and analysed as summarised in Table 7.4. Similarly, the options
for disposing of pesticide containers within the country or shipping overseas for eventual
disposal were considered and analysed as summarised in Table 7.5.

The lack of suitable hazardous waste processing and destruction technology and
equipment asserted in Table 7.4 is attested to by the fact that incinerators suitable for
destroying hazardous wastes such as OP stocks, especially on the scale required in this
project does not exist in Tanzania. Although there are incinerators used for destroying
medical wastes, these are generally small and designed to suit only relatively small
quantities of medical wastes. Additionally, as noted in Chapter 4 of this report, the use of
cement kilns for destroying the OP stocks is not appropriate at the moment. Apart from
the technical limitations associated with this option, recent consultations with the owners
of the kilns indicate that they are firmly against the idea of using their kilns for destroying
OP stocks.

Further to the above, it must be noted that the destruction of some quantities of OP stocks
in cement kilns previously done in Tanzania was done on experimental basis with
external technical assistance. This implies that the effort cannot be taken as a
demonstration of the country’s institutional, human resource, or technological capacity to
handle the disposal of current stocks of OPs and its associated waste.

In view of the preceding discussion and the SWOT analysis summarised in Table 7.4, it
is more advantageous to carry out the final disposal of obsolete pesticides outside the
country than within the country. It is important to note that, as discussed later with
respect to Table 7.5, the advantages of disposal outside the country apply to the disposal
of the free standing obsolete pesticides and associated waste; excluding contaminated
empty pesticide containers.

The SWOT analysis summarised in Table 7.5 leads to the conclusion that it is better to
dispose of pesticide containers within than outside the country. It can be noted that the
advantages of internal disposal option outweigh those of external disposal despite the fact
that disposal of obsolete pesticide containers has not been done in the country before.

In spite of the conclusion drawn from Table 7.5, consultation with relevant stakeholders
on this issue indicated that the majority of the stakeholders were opposed to any attempt
to destroy pesticide contaminated containers and pesticide contaminated washwater in
Tanzania. The main arguments cited to support the opposition were: a lack of suitable
technology and facilities, insufficient human resources, inexperience, and uncertainities
regarding capacity and commitment of local institutions towards the provision of
effective oversight. It was further pointed out that, much as it is desirable to build the
kind of capacity that would be offered by this project component, the urgency associated
with this project far outweighs the potential benefit. In summary, the best option would
be to dispose the OPs and its associated waste in licensed high temperature facilities for
destruction of hazardous waste outside the country

7.4    Cost Considerations for OP Safeguarding and Disposal Options

Table 7.6a: Option 1: A clean sweep of inventorised OP and safeguarding of
            contaminated soils

 Waste stream component                      Quantity (tones)  Estimated cost (USD)
 Obsolete pesticides                                       700             2,800,000
 Contaminated soils                                       2755            11,020,000
 Total                                                    3455            13,820,000




                                                                                       167
Table 7.6b: Option 2: Risk reduction strategy combining safeguarding and destruction
of OP and contaminated soil based on PSMS risk profiles

 Waste stream component                       Quantity (tones) Estimated cost (USD)
 Obsolete pesticides                                        700            2,800,000
 Contaminated soils                                        2755           11,020,000
 Other contaminated material                                 3.6              14,400
 Total                                                   3458.6           13,834,400

In Tables 7.6a and 7.6b, ‘other contaminated material’ includes pesticide contaminated
containers and all wastes generated during the safeguarding operations. The costs are
based on all inclusive average unit rates accounting for all costs involved from the OP
stock sources in Tanzania to the final disposal place outside Tanzania. The average unit
rate is derived from past experience with adjustments that take into consideration
prevailing conditions.

From the analysis summarised in Tables 7.6a and 7.6b, none of the options considered is
significantly more advantageous than the other. Based on this analysis, therefore, neither
Option 1 nor Option 2 deserves preferential treatment to the other.

                                CHAPTER EIGHT

8.0 ENVIRONMENTAL AND SOCIAL MONITORING PLAN

8.1    Overview

Monitoring is needed to check if and to what extent the impacts are mitigated, benefits
enhanced and new problems addressed. Plans for monitoring have been included in the
Environmental and Social Monitoring Plan (Table 8.1). The monitoring plan as for EMP
also assigns responsibilities for monitoring activities.

Monitoring of safeguarding activities shall be undertaken by members of the Project
Management Unit (PMU) including the National Project Coordinator (NPC), and
Technical Advisor for Disposal (TAD). Others could be the Field Managers (FMs), M&E
officer, and the Zonal agriculture coordinators (ZC) from MAFS&C.

Where resettlement will be necessary, local government officials will be involved in the
monitoring exercise. Non-governmental Organisations and other Community Services
Organisations will constitute the monitoring team for the safeguarding activities. It is
anticipated that use of NGO monitors will provide an impartial assessment of the
effectiveness of the proposed mitigation measures. The PMU may identify the most
appropriate national NGO to fill this role.

                                                                                                                                                         175
8.2    Project Team Monitoring and Reporting

8.2.1 Field Managers (FMs)

The day to day monitoring of the field activities shall be undertaken by the FMs and
M&E officers. A variety of standard forms exist which will be used to communicate and
monitor field activities. The forms are maintained within the project office both
electronically and in hard copies for reference, follow-up and auditing. The content of
the various forms is as given below.

Forms and details concerning:
      Daily Briefing
      Project checklists
      Daily Progress Reports
      Weekly Progress Reports
      Work Plans

The blank forms for these documents are included in Appendix 8.1 and are summarized
below:

Daily Briefings

The field managers provide daily briefings to the operational staff in the field. These
briefings include the principal activity for the day, a review of progress, Task Based Risk
Assessment, and PPE requirements and issues that need addressing.

Project Check lists

The field managers have a project check list to ensure that all items are in place to
undertake the day to day activities safely.

The list includes sections on:
        Documentation required;
        Working areas;
        Worker training;
        Daily briefing given to Regional Administration, counterparts, hired labourers,
        drivers;
        PPE (correct use) by counterparts, hired labourers, and visitors;
        Storekeeper (on time, co-operative);
        Variations to work plan;
        Packages and labeling;
        Health monitoring.




                                                                                       176
Daily Progress Report

       List of project staff;
       Planned activity for the day;
       Actual activity undertaken.

Weekly Progress Report

       Principle tasks undertaken;
       Progress against work plan;
       Safety issues;
       Update risk assessment
       Variation;
       Instructions issued;
       Operations planned for the following week.

Work Plans

       Weekly plan with detail for each day;
       Zone work plan with summary detail.

Forms and details related to each of the activities are given in Appendix 8.1 of this
document to show a better picture of what is going on in addition to the above generic
type of listing.

8.2.2 NPC and TAD Monitoring

The National Project Coordinator (NPC) and the Technical Advisor for Disposal (TAD)
should undertake regular visits to the field to observe the teams’ activities. The frequency
of these observation missions should be such that each team is observed as often as
necessary. To ensure that normal behaviour is observed, the observations should be
unannounced. The NPC also monitors the effectiveness of the Disposal Service
Contactor.


8.3    Monitoring of the PMU

It is recommended that a suitably qualified independent consultant be procured to
monitor and evaluate the performance of the PMU. The consultant should necessarily be
well qualified in important aspects of OP management based on both academic training
and experience acquired through undertaking similar assignments.




                                                                                        177
                                    CHAPTER NINE
9.0 ENVIRONMENTAL AND SOCIAL MANAGEMENT TRAINING
    REQUIREMENTS

Tanzania currently has some capacity in the area of management of obsolete pesticides.
This capacity has been developed as a result of training and field experience gained from
the implementation of past initiatives in Tanzania. One of the main objectives of the ASP
in Tanzania is to build on the existing capacity and to establish new capacity in each of
the key components of the project. Specifically, those who will be involved in
safeguarding and disposal activities as elucidated in this CESA document need to be
properly trained to enable them discharge their duties effectively, safely and in an
environmentally friendly manner. In view of this, capacity needs to be developed in the
following areas:

 (vii)    Health and safety,
 (viii)   Environmental protection,
 (ix)     Equipment supply,
 (x)      Public consultation, management,
 (xi)     Environmental policies and programs
 (xii)    Management, planning and budgeting

Training objectives will be achieved by organising induction courses and training
workshops as detailed in Table 9.1.

Table 9.1: Environmental and social management training requirements

                                              210
APPENDIX A: ENVIRONMENTAL ASSESSMENT AND ENVIRONMENTAL
MANAGEMENT PLAN FOR SULPHUR STOCKPILE IN TANGA

1. Background information

The inventory of obsolete pesticides in Tanzania revealed that there was about 600 tons of pure
sulfur at GBP site in Tanga. Additionally, there is about 250 tons of contaminated sulphur at the
GBP premises. The GBP site was formerly owned by the defunct Tanganyika Fertilizer
Company (TFA). The stockpile of sulphur in Tanga is privately owned. A large portion of the
pure sulphur was stored inside of a large godown. The contaminated sulphur is mostly lying
outside of the godown. Plates 1.0 show the sulphur that was stored inside godown while Plate
2.0 depicts the contaminated sulfur that is lying outside outside the godown respectively. The
identified sulphur is classified as of low risk product based on the risk profile provided by the
Pesticide Stock Management System (PSMS).




Plate 1.0 : Sulphur inside the GBP Godown




                                              211
Plate 1.2: Contaminated Sulphur outside the GBP Godown


The consultant was requested to prepare an environmental assessment and EMP for sulphur in
Tanga to be included as an Appendix to the CESA report. The following were the main terms of
reference.

   1.   Prepare safeguarding disposal options for the sulphur
   2.   Indicate strategic Environmental Mitigation Plan for the site
   3.   Develop an estimated budget for the remediation of the site
   4.   Prepare a report for the sulphur stock at Tanga site as separate Appendix to the main
        CESA report

2. Exiting situation
During field survey in Tanga it was found that the owner of sulphur had secured a market for the
chemical. Minjingu Fertilizer Company located in Manyara Region has bought the whole
uncontaminated sulphur stockpile for fertilizer production.           During February 2010,
uncontaminated sulphur inside the godown was being repackaged into 50 kg bags ready to be
transported to Minjingu Fertilizer Company. Plate 3 shows sulphur inside the GBP store being
repackaged into 50 kg bags while Plate 4 shows sulphur sacks neatly stacked ready for
transportation to Minjingu Fertilizer Company.




                                              212
Plate 3: Sulphur placed on the floor inside GBP store in the process of being repackaged




Plate 4: Sulphur already repackaged in 50 kg sacks in GBP store


After the completion of repackaging process all Sulphur inside the godown except for about 20
bags were transported to Minjingu Fertilizer Company to be used for fertilizer production.
However, about 250 tons of contaminated sulphur, mostly outside the godown still remains at the
site. Plate 5 shows an empty section of the godown after the removal of the sulphur. Plate 6
shows the remaining bags of repackaged sulphur and Plate 7 and 8 shows contaminated sulphur
inside and outside the godown.




                                              213
Plate 5: An empty section of the godown after the removal of sulphur




                                             214
Plate 6: Contaminated sulphur mixed with packaging material inside the godown




Plate 7: Remaining contaminated sulphur outside the godown




                                            215
Considering the recent development of the removal of sulphur from the site, the activities
proposed in the TOR cannot fully be implemented. The measures outlined in the following
section are the proposed for the safe disposal of the remnants of the contaminated sulphur.

3. Disposal options

The options for the disposal of what remains of the sulphur stocks originally stored at the GPB
site in Tanga have been considered in the light of the fact that what needs to be disposed of is not
pure sulphur but rather sulphur – contaminated soil and packaging materials. The practical
implication of this is that the safety, public health, and environmental needs for the disposal
technology and the accompanying strategies and operations will not be as stringent as they would
have been for pure sulfur.

The technology and practical options which are most appropriate and cost-effective for disposing
of the contaminated sulfur and packaging materials are: i) on-site containment by land filling and
capping; and ii) off-site containment by landfilling and capping.


3.1 On-site containment by land filling

The on-site containment option constitutes the following technological aspects and operations:

STEP 1
Excavation of the area containing contaminated sulfur at the GPB site.

STEP 2
Lining the bottom and sides of the containment facility with appropriate encasement and capping
materials.

STEP 3
Placing the contaminated sulphur into the containment facility.

STEP 4
Covering contaminated sulphur with appropriate encasing and capping materials.

STEP 5
Paving the top part of the containment site with asphalt or Portland cement concrete so that the
site can continue to be used as driveways or parking lots. The covered containment site can even
be built upon provided appropriate safety measures are observed during use.




                                                216
3.2    Off-site containment by landfilling and capping.

The off-site containment option constitutes using an available naturally formed land fill area or
making one away from the GPB site. Other aspects of the off-site containment option are similar
to the ones for the on-site containment option.

In both options (that is; on-site containment and that of off-site containment, the following
encasing and capping specifications are recommended:

Bottom encasement and capping provision: A 300 mm thick inner layer (in contact with the
buried sulphur – contaminated materials) of compacted clay and crushed limestone mixture plus
a 300 mm thick outer layer made up of a mixture of sawdust and crushed limestone.

Sides encasement and capping provision: A 300 mm thick inner layer (in contact with the
buried sulphur – contaminated materials) of compacted clay and crushed limestone mixture plus
a 300 mm thick outer layer made up of a mixture of sawdust and crushed limestone.


Top encasement, capping and pavement provision: An inner layer (in contact with the buried
sulphur – contaminated materials) of 300 mm thick compacted clay, a 150 mm thick middle
layer of fine aggregate (sand), and 150 mm thick Portland cement or asphalt concrete paved top
layer.

The containment provision will cover a single site with area dimensions of about 8.35 m by 20 m
or two separate sites each covering 8.35 m by 10 m. As such, the buried materials will extend to
an effective depth of only 1m. The whole containment structure will extend to a total depth of
2.2 m.

The main advantage of on-site containment by landfilling and capping is that it precludes the
need for acquiring a separate containment site and the attendant hassle of addressing initial
environmental assessment and operational environmental management needs. The main
advantage of off-site containment is that it offers several options from which the eventual site for
the containment facility can be chosen.




                                                217
APPENDIX B:
     TERMS OF REFERENCES FOR COUNTRY-PROJECT ENVIRONMENTAL AND
     SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (CESA), AND ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT PLAN
                                   (EMP)

1.      Background

Tanzania is one of the countries in Africa that is critically affected by accumulation of obsolete
pesticides. An indicative inventory was conducted by the Government of the United Republic
of Tanzania in 1997/98, through the National Environment Management Council (NEMC).
About 1200 metric tons of obsolete pesticides were identified. The stocks are scattered in over
350 stores countrywide, and in most cases these stocks can be considered as posing a direct
threat to human health and the environment.

The obsolete pesticide stocks include over 30% of organo-chlorinated products, notably DDT,
which are classified among the Persistent Organic Pollutants (POPs).

The Government of the United Republic of Tanzania is fully aware that a definite and
environmentally sound disposal solution is required for the obsolete pesticides stocks in the
country. The cost of a full disposal plan is beyond the country’s financial and technical capacities
for the full implementation of an international disposal operation.

In view of this, an official appeal for assistance from the international donor community was
made by the Government during the FAO Third Consultation on Prevention and Disposal of
Obsolete Pesticides Stocks which was held in Rome in March 1998.

Tanzania was subsequently included among the first seven countries to benefit from the
financing through the Africa Stockpiles Programme (ASP) funded by GEF and other multilateral
donors; other countries being Ethiopia, South Africa, Mali, Morocco, Tunisia and Nigeria.

Tanzania has been selected for support under ASP–P1 because it meets the following ASP-P1
agreed upon readiness criteria: (a) ratified the Stockholm and Basel Conventions (b) is a priority
country in terms of the volume of obsolete pesticides (c) has established a Project Coordination
Team and completed an Operational Manual to implement the project and (d) project finance has
been secured for project implementation.

The National Environment Management Council (NEMC) which is within the Vice President’s
Office (Environmental Affairs) is the executing agency for ASP – Tanzania. The Programme is
managed through a Project Management Unit (PMU) headed by Project Coordinator. Other key
staff members include two field managers, a prevention coordinator seconded from the Ministry
of Agriculture, Technical Advisor for disposal, Monitoring and Evaluation Officer and
Communication Officer. Six agriculture zonal coordinators coordinate with the ASP PMU in
project implementation on part time (or on as needed) basis. A procurement specialist is also
available on call basis.

                                                218
A steering committee is set up to oversee ASP implementation in the country. The Committee
comprises members from the following key institutions: (1) Vice Presidents Office (Chair); (2)
Ministry of Agriculture and Food Security (Vice Chair), (3) NEMC (Secretary) (4) Occupational
Safety and Health Authority (OSHA); (5)Tropical Pesticides Research Institute (TPRI); (6)
Government Chemist Laboratory Agency; (7) Department of Environment (Zanzibar) (8)
Tanzania Ports Authority (9) Commission for Agriculture, Research and Extension, Zanzibar;
and (10) Focal Point for the Basel and Stockholm Convention based at the Division of
Environment. A parallel Forum is set up to include members from NGOs, Civil Society
Organizations and Intergovernmental organizations.

As a key part of the implementation of the ASP, a Country-project Environmental and Social
Assessment (CESA) is required as a condition for disbursement for obsolete pesticides disposal
services.

These TORs were prepared in accordance to the Guidance Note on the Preparation of the
Country-Project Environment and Social Assessments (CESA), November 2008, which include a
detailed presentation of the World Bank safeguard policy requirements. The project’s potential
adverse environmental impacts are principally related to pesticide storage, handling,
transportation, disposal and site remediation activities in country operations. Because of their
significance, the project is classified as Environmental Category A. The project triggers two of
the World Bank’s safeguard policies: OP 4.01 on Environmental Assessments, which requires
the preparation of a CESA, and OP 4.09 on Pest Management which require a Pest Management
Plan (PMP). Since the project itself meets the requirements of a PMP, there will be no need to
prepare a self-standing document. The applicability of two additional policies OP 4.12 on
Involuntary Resettlement and OP 4.04 on Natural Habitats will need to be evaluated on a case by
case basis, as it is conceivable that project implementation may affect established access rights of
people to contaminated sites, or include activities that represent a significant risk on natural
habitats. The CESA will determine whether or not these policies need to be triggered, and if so,
incorporate adequate mitigation measures.


2.     Aims and Objectives

The CESA is a critical component of all Africa Stockpiles Programme (ASP) country projects.
In accordance with the GEF Trust Fund Grant agreement (TF056693), there is a legal
requirement for Tanzania to complete a CESA and Environmental Management Plan (EMP)
before any funds can be released for the safeguarding and disposal of any obsolete stocks. The
CESA and the EMP should be considered as tools to assist Tanzania during project
implementation.

Whereas the CESA aims to clarify what needs to be done at the country level to address the
problems faced from obsolete pesticides and associated wastes, the EMP provides detailed
information on how any safeguarding and disposal will be completed and who will do it. Put in
another way, the CESA allows Tanzania to define the detailed scope of the problem faced from
obsolete pesticides and to develop the strategy for addressing the problems. The EMP should

                                                219
provide the detailed methodologies for removing the problem in a safe and environmentally
sound manner.

3.     Aims of the CESA

To achieve the aims as stated above it is necessary to provide clarification on the aims and
objectives of the CESA. The CESA is a decision making tool to allow the government of
Tanzania to make objective decisions in key areas related to the implementation of ASP. In
respect to the ASP the CESA aims to:

       Provide data on the inter-relationship between the locations where pesticides are stored
       and the broader environment in the country;
       Prioritise storage locations based on the risk posed to public health and the environment;
       Allow the government to make decisions on the strategy to be adopted for the
       safeguarding of obsolete pesticides (repackaging, transport and storage);
       Allow the country to select the most appropriate disposal strategy for the waste identified
       at the inventory stage based on an assessment of national capacity to treat wastes in an
       environmentally sound manner;
       Factor in any social issues as determined during the assessment of the inventory data and
       identify any locations where resettlement of inhabitants may be required (temporary or
       long term).

These Terms of References provide a minimum requirement for the government to comply with
the World Bank safeguards policy. Additional studies and information related to improving
general environmental awareness and chemicals management may be proposed subject to
availability of additional funding.

4.     Scope

The CESA can be considered as a tool to illustrate the inter-relationship between the human and
physical geography of the country and the locations where obsolete pesticides and associated
contaminated materials are found. In addition to demonstrating the relationship between these
factors the CESA also looks to examine the institutional setting in which the problem is set. The
CESA should consolidate a very diverse set of factors influencing the implementation of a
safeguarding and disposal project and then allow the government to make objective decisions on
how to address the risks posed by obsolete pesticides in a safe and environmentally sound
manner.

Section 1: Country Setting

Data indicating “sensitive? areas should be presented and complement the
information already collected by the PMU (see below).     This to include
information on aspects such as:

       World and National Heritage sites;
       National Parks;
       Wetlands;
       Sensitive areas due to topography;
       Areas of importance with regard to biodiversity (both fauna and flora);
       Centres of population (with size);
       Distribution of major hospitals and clinics with capacity to deal with
       poisoning;
       Any other “special? areas.

Tanzania administratively is divided into 26 regions of which 21 are found in Tanzania Mainland
while 5 are in Zanzibar. The country geographically is characterized by varying climatic
conditions from semi arid, grasslands to heavy rainfall areas. Moreover the country has different
topographic conditions. This varies from low land areas to range of mountains. This situation is
vital to be taken under consideration during the ASP operation especially during transportation
of obsolete pesticides, taking under consideration the road condition which is not all tarmac.
                                              221
In all the 21 regional headquarters and districts in Tanzania Mainland, there are government
hospitals which can be used during the operation in case there is accident of exposure to
pesticides for operational team.

WORLD/NATIONAL HERITAGE AREAS AND NATIONAL PARKS

Tanzania is one the countries in the world that has a large number of heritable areas including
national parks and game reserves.

The presence of these areas has to be taken into account during the ASP operation so that it
should not be disturbed and/or in case of spillage or accident during transportation of obsolete
pesticides, emergency measures should be taken. Some of the roads pass through the National
parks, like Dar es Salaam to Mbeya road which passes through Mikumi National park.

Some of the heritable national parks and Game reserves are as elaborated below.




                                              222
Arusha National Park

Arusha National Park covers an area of about 137 km2. It is located near Arusha town
between the peaks of Mountains Kilimanjaro and Meru. The park and town derive their name
from the Wa-Arusha people who traditionally lived in this area. The Ngurdoto Crater,
Momela Lakes, the highland montane forest, and the rugged mount Meru (about 4575 m
above the sea level) are the four distinctive features of the park. The Park has a wealth of
wildlife including colobus monkeys, velvet monkeys, bushbacks, buffaloes, red forest duikers,
hippos, elephants and giraffes. Bird life especially waterfowl is abundant and interesting.


Gombe National Park
Situated 16 km, north of Kigoma town in western Tanzania. Gombe National park occupies
52 Km2 of land. The beauty of Gombe National Park is unique; it is a park without roads,
where you can walk and experience nature with all your senses. Due to its altitude, the park's
vegetation varies from evergreen forests of tall trees to open woodlands and grassland.
Common mammals found in the park are forest species mostly primates. These include
chimpanzees, baboons, blue monkeys, red tailed monkeys and red colobus monkeys. In a
sense, the park is indeed an island of wilderness surrounded on three sides by cultivation and
on the north by the lake.

Katavi National Park
Covering an area of 2253 km2 Katavi National Park is located in Mpanda Districts, Rukwa
Region about 40 km south east of Mpanda town. The park is famous for its undisturbed
natural face compared to other parks in the country. Major features of attraction include Lake
Katavi with its vast short grass flood plains in the north, palm fringed Lake Chada in the
Southeast and Katuma River. Animals found in the park: include zebras, sable and roan
antelopes, elands, leopard, elephants, buffalos and lions. Waterfowls also abound.

Kilimanjaro National Park
With an area of 756 km2, Kilimanjaro National Park exists to preserve Mount Kilimanjaro's
outstanding scenic features and its flora and fauna. At an altitude of 5895 m above mean sea
level, Mount Kilimanjaro is the highest mountain in Africa, the highest freestanding mountain
in the world and one of the largest volcanoes ever to burst through the earth's crust.
Attractive features include one extinct volcano, Shira (3962m) and two dormant volcanoes
peaks, Mawenzi (5149 m) and Kibo (5895 m).

Also, significant numbers of mammals, birds and insects inhabit the area. Mammals include
elephants, buffalos, elands, duikers, leopards, baboons, blue monkeys and black and white
colobus monkeys. The park is also frequented with colorful birds.

Lake Manyara National Park
Located 125 km west of Arusha town, under the wall of the Great Rift Valley. The park is
about 330 km2 in size of which Lake Manyara covers 230 km2. It contains a large variety of
habitats such as the rift wall, the ground water forest, Acacia woodland, area of open
grassland, to mention but a few; thus being able to support large number of species. The most

                                               223
famous spectacle in the park is the tree-climbing lions, which are occasionally seen along
branches of Acacia trees. Other animals found in this area include elephants, leopard, impala,
hippos as well as millions of birds (pelicans and flamingos).
Lake Manyara National Park has the highest population of elephants per square km in the
country.

Ngorongoro Crater
This remarkable and world famous geographic feature is a "caldera"- a collapsed volcano.
There are several prides of lion, and some of the last surviving rhino to be found in Tanzania.
The alkaline soda lake is home to huge flocks of flamingos, a remarkable and unforgettable
sight. The whole crater has a quite unique almost magical atmosphere.

Also there is place called Oldvai gorge found in Ngorongoro crater, which is a historical site
considered as a seat of humanity after the discovery of the earliest specimen of the human
genus, Homo habilis

Tarangire National Park
Covering approximately 2600 km2, Tarangire National Park is second, only the Ngorongoro
Crater, in the concentrations of wildlife to be seen, in the dry season. It lies to the south of
large open grass plains of southern Maasai land, 115 km from Arusha along the Arusha-
Dodoma road. The features of Tarangire which correlate with the type of animals residing in
the area are grassland and flood plains. Tarangire is commonly famous for its tree-climbing
pythons, zebras, hartebeests, elephants, buffalos, waterbucks, gazelles, oryx and abundant
birdlife.

Ruaha National Park
Covering an area of 10,300 km2, Ruaha National Park is the second largest park in Tanzania.
Ruaha National Park lies in the central part of Tanzania just west of Iringa highlands is also
the second largest elephant sanctuary in the country, next to Selous Game Reserve. The game
animals found here are herds of elephant and buffalo. The exciting Great Ruaha River features
are larger number of hippos and crocodiles. During the dry season the river attracts great
number of game; giraffe, warthog and eland. In the plains, ostrich, cheetah and Grant's
Gazelle can be seen. The park offers a paradise of the outstanding natural world as it lies
within a belt which interface species of Fauna and Flora of southern and northern Africa.

Serengeti National Park
The Serengeti National Park with 14763 km2 is one of the largest wildlife sanctuaries in the
world and it is the site of one of the most breathtaking events in the animal kingdom, the
migration of thousands of wildebeest. It was accepted by the World Heritage Convention as a
World Heritage Site and Biosphere Reserve in 1981. about 320 km from Arusha town. Park
has large number of species of plain animals, from a hare to the elephant and a wonderful
selection of birdlife.




                                                224
Mikumi National Park

The park occupies an area of about 3237 squre km . It is found im Mkata river plain bordered
by the Ukuguru mountain range to the esat and Rubebo mountains to the west.The most
attractive wild life animals includes: buffaloes, elephants, giraffe, zebra, wildebeestsa nad
wild dogs to mention the few. The park is passed by the main road, the Dar es Salaam
Tunduma. Therefore great precausions has to be taken during the transportation of stocks
from the Southern highlands zone to Dar es Saalaam port which will be used as an exit point.

Selous National Reserve

This reserve is one of the most remote and least visited game park in Africa. It occupies an
area of about 15 000 square miles and it is the largest world game reserve. Interesting
uniqueness of the game is that can be explored by boat sailing,through swamps and lagoons. It
has the world’s largest big games which includes, elephants, buffaloes, rhinoceros and it
contains African’s concentration of hippopotamus, crocodiles and wild dogs



WETLANDS

Tanzania has a great number of wetlands which serves as a source of different resources to
the communities like water, food, herbs. This wetlands includes rivers, lakes and swamps.

Some of the important wetlands of Tanzania includes, Lake Victoria, Tanganyika, Manyara
and Lake Nyasa. Rivers include River Ruvu, Pangani, Kagera and Kilombero, just to mention
a few. In view of this, great precaution should be taken to avoid spillage or any other accident
which can cause a lot of problems to the community during ASP implementation especially
when transporting the stocks.


5
Section 2: Policy, Legal and Institutional Framework

Much of this work is already included in the ASP Operational Manual. The Consultant in
consultation with the ASP-Project Management Unit (PMU) should compile a complete set of all
relevant government policies, decrees and regulations that could potentially impact on the
implementation of the ASP. Areas of focus should include:

       National waste management legislation;
       National transport of hazardous goods regulations;
       Environmental assessment / impact regulations;
       Permitting of works; and so forth.

The text should note that the CESA is being prepared in strict compliance with requirements of
the country environmental regulations regarding Environmental Assessment, World Bank

                                               225
Operational Policy (OP 4.01) on Environmental Assessment, and requirements of any co-
financing institutions.

In addition, the relevance of the three Chemical Conventions (Basel, Rotterdam and Stockholm)
to the proposed works should be explained. This should include a statement indicating that the
disposal process will assist the country in meeting its obligations to remove POPs pesticides as
required under the Stockholm Convention.

Here under is the status of the three chemical conventions which has been ratified by the
Government of the United Republic of Tanzania.

                       RATIFICATION

                                        Office

                                                Security and Cooperatives
                                                For consumer and industrial chemicals:
                                                Government Chemist Laboratory Agency

4     Stockholm        2004                 Division of Environment in the Vice President’s

Tanzania has also relevant legislations applicable to CESA. Among others, include the
following:
       Environmental Management Act (2004)
       Plant protection Act (1997)
       Public Health ordinance (1954)
       Occupational health and Safety Act (2003)
       Industrial and consumer chemicals Act (2003)
       Urban authority Act (1982)
       Local government Act (1982)
       Water utilization control and regulation (1974)
       Surface and Marine Transport Authority (SUMATRA)
       Merchant and shipping Act (1967)

In terms of the Institutional arrangements for implementation of the ASP in general and the
CESA in particular, responsibilities for mitigation and monitoring should be defined along with
arrangements for information flow, especially for coordination between agencies responsible for

                                              226
mitigation. This is especially important since this project requires cross-sector and cross-
institutional integration. The CESA shall identify the key stakeholders responsibilities for the
mitigating and monitoring measures, of implementation, training, financing, and reporting.
Institutional arrangements should also be crafted to maintain support for agreed enforcement
measures for environmental protection. (see table C in annex 1)


Section 3: Baseline Data

The section should include:

       A summary of any previous waste management and pesticide disposal initiatives;
       A statement on the causes of accumulation as identified by previous studies;
       All PSMS data related to the pesticide stores in the country, including the plot of
       Environmental and Pesticide Risk Factors (FE and FP);
       A preliminary register of all contaminated sites;
       An assessment of national management capacity to complete a national safeguarding
       operation;
       An assessment of current disposal capacity in the country to determine the potential for
       local treatment of the wastes as identified at inventory.


Previous disposal initiatives: The government of Tanzania has taken successful initiatives with
regard to disposal of obsolete stocks. The following are among the initiatives:

       In 1986, SIDA provided assistance to repack a large quantity of pesticides at Vikuge, in
       Coastal Region, where over 200 tons including large amount of DDT were repacked.
       In 1995, The Netherlands Government funded the removal of 300 tons of hazardous
       waste from the Island of Zanzibar and Pemba which was incinerated in the UK.
       In 1995, The German Agency for Technical Cooperation (GTZ) provided assistance to
       incinerate 57 tons of Dinitro-o-Cresol (DNOC) at Wazo cement kiln
       During 1997/98 The Government of the Netherlands funded a national-wide inventory of
       obsolete pesticides and veterinary wastes, whereby about 1200 metric tons were found
       scattered in about 350 sites all over the country.

       In 2003, FAO and USAID supported Tanzania in emergency safeguarding of six high
       risk sites. The safeguarded sites are highlighted below:

       Vingunguti
       The store is located in Dar es Salaam, about 8 km from the city centre. The site was
       posing a potential threat to human health and the environment, since it is close to a
       densely populated residential area.

       Korogwe
       Korogwe site is located at Old Korogwe in Tanga Region about 400 km from Dar es
       Salaam. The store was very close to the river Pangani and 100m from a bore hole which

                                              227
      is a source of water for Old Korogwe residents, hence was posing threat to human health
      and environment. About 85metric tones of DDT was safeguarded.

      In January 2008, The Germany Agency for Technical Cooperation (GTZ), cleaned up the
      Korogwe site, where by 85 tons of DDT and 25 tons of contaminated materials was
      removed and shipped for incineration in Germany. This exercise was also used as a field
      training for ASP PMU members and casual labour.


      TPRI
      The site is found in Arusha region about 700 Km from Dar es Salaam. It is located within
      the premises of Tropical Pesticides Research Institute (TPRI). About 4000 lts of DNOC
      was repacked in salvage drums. The site was posing threat to population within the TPRI
      offices and residents.

      Mbarali Site
      This is found in Mbarali rice farm in Mbeya, about 800km from Dar es Salaam and 16
      km from the Dar es Salaam- Tunduma road, junction from Igawa. The site was very close
      to irrigation canal and about 30 meters from fish ponds. The site was posing a serious
      threat to human health and the environment. About 4110kg of ketazine, 12.50kg of BHD
      and 2112.25kg of BHC was repacked in salvage drums and stored.

      Morogoro Site
      This is found in Morogoro region about 200km from Dar es Salaam. This contains three
      stores as shown here under:

      Store A.
      Owned by Morogoro Agriculture office. 20 empty containers of 50lts size were crushed
      and repacked in salvage drums.

      Store B.
      Also owned by Morogoro Agriculture office. About 25 empty containers of 50lts size
      were manually crushed and repacked in salvage drums.

      Site C, Tanzania Link
      The site is very close to a railway station in Morogoro, owned by the National Housing
      Corporation. The site contained two main stores and one old formulation plant. About 18
      tons of technical DDT was found but safeguarding was not completed due to lack of
      equipment and appropriate PPE.


Linking CESA to inventory taking:

The critical aspects of obsolete stock identification require knowledge of products,
manufacturers, trade names and synonyms, when they were manufactured, how they were used
and who used them.

                                            228
For the purpose of the environmentally sound disposal of these obsolete stocks, a more specific
and complete inventory is being finalised in Tanzania. The TSU of FAO developed a database,
Pesticide Stock Management System (PSMS), an interactive process that will allow each country
to record all obsolete pesticides storage sites, contents and characteristics. Tanzania, as one of
the participating ASP countries, will have access to this database and will get the necessary
training by the TSU, FAO. The following paragraphs provide more detailed guidance.

Inventories are an important tool for identifying, quantifying and characterizing wastes. A
national inventory may be used to:

   (a)        Establish a baseline quantity of products
   (b)        Establish an information registry that assists with safety and regulatory inspections;
   (c)        Obtain accurate information needed to draw up a plan for Safeguarding and site
              stabilization;
   (d)        Assist with the preparation of emergency response plans; and
   (e)        Track progress to minimize and phase-out these chemicals.


Section 4: Environmental and Social Impacts

Based on the General Country Setting presented in Section 1 and the baseline pesticide data
presented in Section 3, the Consultant shall provide the following assessment of the
Environmental and Social Impacts:

         A list of the high risk locations with a summary analysis of the principle factors which
         make them a risk to either public health and / or the environment;
         An assessment of the potential impacts from the locations based on their proximity to and
         inter-relationship with the broader environment;
         Indication of any locations where permanent or temporary resettlement may be needed to
         allow the safeguarding operation to proceed.

Particular attention shall be paid to discussing potential health and safety impacts to local
populations and workers involved with project implementation.


Section 5: Environnemental Management Plan (EMP)

The World Bank’s Operational Policy (OP4.01) identifies EMPs as an essential feature of
Category A projects. Specific requirements relating to EMPs are set out in Annex C of the
Bank’s OP 4.01. A two stage approach will be adopted for the preparation of the EMP.

The first stage shall be to take the information collected in Sections 1 – 4 and provide details on
how the potential risk posed by the obsolete pesticides will be reduced / eliminated. This will be
presented as the following outputs:


                                                229
   i.      Safeguarding Strategy

The safeguarding strategy will need to address the following issues:

           The risk based prioritisation of the stores and geo-political regions:
           Based on the data and information obtained and gathered during inventory, the PMU
           in collaboration with the CESA Consultant shall make risk prioritization of the stores.

           Who will complete the safeguarding (national staff, contractor or both),
           according to the level of risks (low, medium and high risk sites):
           The consultant shall evaluate the options, i.e. safeguarding for low risk sites by the
           national staff members coordinated by PMU, while high risk sites would be
           completed by the disposal service contractor in close collaboration with the PMU, or
           all safeguarding by the disposal service contractor. This will depend much on the
           information and data obtained during the ongoing inventory undertaking and CESA.
           The consultant will need to note the gaps in the analysis.

           Where will the safeguarding be completed (at the original location, or at a collection
           centre);
           Where will the safeguarded stocks be stored and how will the stores be managed;
           How and who, will transport the stocks to the collection point, and transport from
           collection centre to point of export, and the final disposal facility.

        The consultant should note that:
        Depending on the condition of the stocks and the site, safeguarding activities will be
        undertaken starting from the original location by repackaging and, if need be at the
        collection centres. The stocks will be stored at the collection centres to be identified at
        each zone. The number of collection centres will depend on the amount of stocks found
        in each zone. The store at the main collection centres will be guarded using security
        guards to be contracted by PMU. Stocks awaiting final disposal will be managed by the
        disposal service contractor. Based on the option retained above, the PMU may or may
        not be responsible for coordinating the repackaging of stocks at original locations and
        transport them to the collection centres. From the collection centres up to the point of
        export, and hence to the final disposal facility, the work will be done by the disposal
        company in close supervision and monitoring by the PMU, NGOs and other relevant
        governmental institutions.

        During repackaging and transportation of the obsolete pesticides, maximum safety and
        security will need to be observed. The consultant will define the measures needed to
        ensure that potential impact to health, public safety and environment is minimized and
        that appropriate safeguarding equipment is identified in case of emergency caused by
        accident or spillage.

        Support and training will be provided to the country team by the TSU and the World
        Bank Safeguard Unit to allow them to make objective decisions. The Consultant should
        provide recommendations that will guide the government to make decisions on the level

                                               230
         of involvement necessary during the safeguarding stage i.e. to actually implement the
         safeguarding with national government staff or to supervise the completion of the works
         by external contractors. The consultant will conduct with the PMU a SWOT (Strengths,
         Weaknesses, Opportunities and Threats) analysis of various options which must be
         considered in Tanzanian context as defined by the inventory.

   ii.      Disposal Strategy

The disposal strategy will need to address the following issues:

              A detailed analysis of the waste streams to be dealt with as part of the ASP;
              An assessment of national capacity to deal with waste streams;
              Selection of preferred disposal options based on the waste streams and their
              respective volumes / amounts, current national capacity to deal with each waste
              stream and barriers to implementation of a national disposal option;
              Development of tender bid documents to allow for selection of an environmentally
              sound disposal option.

The process of developing a disposal strategy does allow for prioritisation of waste streams as
defined at the inventory stage. The risk based assessment of all locations and assessment of the
potential for materials to adversely affect human health and / or the environment is a key factor
in the ASP CESA process.

The second stage will be to prepare the actual EMP, by assessing the risks related to the
implementation of the safeguarding and disposal strategy above, and making sure that adequate
mitigation measures are in place.

The Environmental Management Plan will be developed by the consultant in close collaboration
with the PMU and include: (a) the potentially significant risk associated to the critical activities
(repackaging, transport, storage and disposal) (b) the proposed mitigating actions; (c) when the
action is to be taken (start and finish dates or ongoing); (d) who will be responsible for
implementing the mitigating action during project operation; and (e) associated costs for the
mitigation measures.

The assessment of risks and mitigation actions should focus on following major areas: a) Health
and safety, b) Environmental Protection, c) Equipment, d) Public Consultation, e) Management
and Planning, f) Sustainability (capacity building and skills transfer), g) Budget planning

An EMP for each risk category site will be prepared: low risks, medium risks and high risks, and
presented in the table format provided in annex 1. The consultant shall also mention any risk and
mitigation measure that would be specific to certain risk sites.

Based on the above it is important that the EMP shall be prepared by personnel who are:

   a. Independent EA experts not affiliated with the project
   b. Competent to complete the preparation of the document;

                                                231
   c. Able to train the operational staff who will implement the plan as presented in the EMP;
   d. Familiar with the implementation of projects in the developing country context.

Finally, the EMP should be considered as a tool to enable project managers, supervisors and
technicians to implement difficult projects under challenging conditions with no impact on
human health and the environment.

The EMP will provide a basis for the development of the detailed and site specific operational
health and safety plans which will be the responsibility of the disposal contractor.

The Health, Safety and Environment Plan (HSE Plan) aims to provide the detailed
methodologies and process to be completed to safely eliminate the problem. In short the HSE
Plan will:

    Provide a detailed, site specific methodology for the safe handling of the stocks as
     identified at inventory;
    Make objective decisions regarding worker and environmental protection during field
     operations based analysis of the inventory / PSMS data;
    Detail the system of worker supervision and training to ensure all field operations are
     completed to the highest standards of Health, Safety and Environment set at the ASP
     Programme level;

The ASP PMU may wish to complete a site specific HSE Plans for additional stores identified at
inventory if the country team in consultation with the TSU decide that they pose a specific risk
which needs a detailed management plan. All other sites will be safeguarded based on the
principles of the EMP and HSE Plan at a generic level. Typically, such lower risk locations will
be safeguarded after all the high risk locations are finished. As such, the management and
workers responsible for safeguarding should have developed a level of competence to apply the
EMP and HSE Plan methodologies as a standard approach.

Any proposed course of action and mitigation plan will have alternatives. This section of the
CESA should outline the rationale behind selecting the Safeguarding and Disposal strategies
presented in Section 5 in preference to any alternative scenarios. All options will be presented in
terms of Strengths, Weaknesses, Opportunities and Risks and must be considered in the
Tanzanian context as defined by the inventory.

                                               232
Section 7: Environmental and Social Monitoring Plan

Implementation schedule and reporting procedures:

The timing, frequency and duration of mitigation measures and monitoring should be included in
an implementation schedule, showing phasing and coordination with procedures in the overall
project implementation /operations manual. Linkages should be specified where implementation
of mitigation measures is tied to institutional strengthening and to the project legal agreements,
e.g. as conditions for Grant effectiveness or disbursement. Procedures for providing information
on the progress and results of mitigation and monitoring measures should also be clearly stated.
Recipients of such information should include those with responsibility for ensuring timely
implementation of mitigation measures and for undertaking remedial actions. In addition, the
structure, content and timing of reporting to the Bank should be designed to facilitate supervision
and the Task Managing Team should establish arrangements for the timely receipt of monitoring
reports and their forwarding to the FAO TSU and to the Bank’s environment specialists for
review and comments (see table C in annex 1).

Environmental monitoring plan

The consultant shall prepare an Environmental monitoring plan due to that environmental
monitoring during implementation of the safeguarding and disposal strategies is critical. The
activities of the ASP should in no way result in a worsening of the environmental situation in a
country. This is linked to the key ASP indicators as presented in the Grant Agreement. A
combination of indicators and milestones will need to be developed to ensure that the overall
effect of implementing the ASP reduces risks on the environment and on public health. Typical
indicators will include:
         Tracking of the national risk factor from obsolete pesticides (as defined in the FAO
         EMTK Volume 1);
         Reporting on spills, leakages, accidents, injuries during safeguarding and disposal.

By setting a clear set of indicators for the disposal component of the ASP it will be possible to
demonstrate that the project has complied with all standards set at the programme level. The
implementation of the monitoring plan will need to include supervision missions and audits from
a number of key players including the TSU and World Bank, and possibly an independent
monitor to be hired by the PMU.

Social Monitoring Plan

The consultant shall prepare a social monitoring plan since potential impacts on local
communities need to be regularly monitored. Although current information indicates that
pesticides are usually stored away from people, the project may identify situations when
proximity of pesticide stores to people would raise concerns for their health and safety over time.
Similarly, a need may arise during implementation to condemn certain sites or structures
(warehouses, district back offices that might have been used for storage, etc.) as being too
contaminated to merit clean-up. A related issue may be dealing with the historically

                                               233
contaminated water sources, particularly wells. In those situations, compensation and re-housing,
or the reconstruction of certain offices or other buildings, may be needed, regardless whether the
need arose from legacy contamination from before the project period or from project activities.


Section 8: Environmental and Social Management Training Requirements

Any training requirements associated with implementing and maintaining the mitigation and
monitoring plans should be presented in detail including associated costs (see table format in
annex 1). In addition, the overall institutional capabilities for environmental and social
management should be evaluated, and if necessary, a broader program of institutional
strengthening should be prepared (e.g., laboratory facilities and training for pesticide
trace/residue analysis, implementation of Resettlement Plans).

6. Qualification of personnel and proposed team
    - The CESA will be prepared by a consultant firm with the following experience:
    - Environmental management,
    - Hazardous waste management, esp. related to POPs,, and pesticides
    - Disposal technology assessment
    - Container management
    - If resettlement is triggered an expert on this topic is needed.

The firm shall have to prepare the final CESA report, and EMP, which will be reviewed by the
PMU to ensure that it meets the country requirements. The final report will be presented to
relevant stakeholders through a national workshop as a part of the public consultation process,
including any national approval requirement and to the World Bank. After consultation, the
consultant will incorporate comments and complete all edits to prepare a final draft, which will
be submitted to the WB for non objection. The consulting firm will therefore be expected to
provide personnel with the necessary report writing and presentation skills.

A minimum of 8 years of experience is required, as well as similar experience in Africa.
Collaboration with national experts will be endeavoured.

The total experts’ inputs for the study should not exceed 18 staff weeks during a 4 months
period.


7. Proposed Methodology for the study


The CESA will be prepared in accordance with the guidance provided in the Guidance Note on
the Preparation of the Country-Project Environment and Social Assessments (CESA), November
2008.

The preparation of the CESA will build upon the completed inventory, which has been carried
out in the country. The inventory identified 700 tons of free standing, publicly held obsolete

                                               234
pesticides scattered in 140 stores/sites. Data from the completed inventory has been compiled in
a database “Pesticide Stock Management System (PSMS)?, which will be made accessible to the
consultant. The PSMS includes information about pesticides, storage sites, environmental
conditions, etc.

For the formulation of the CESA and EMP, the consultant will:

   (a) Carry out a desk review and analysis of the detailed inventory.
   (b) Sort sites by risk category (high, medium and low) and select representative sites in each
       group
   (c) Visit all selected sites (all high risk sites should be visited where time limitations make
       this feasible)
   (d) Carry out environmental sampling (related to soil and water contamination) and other
       testing where necessary) at high risk sites where this has not been completed as part of
       the contaminated site assessment. Sampling to be limited to the minimum possible.
   (e) Make use of the FAO Environmental Management Toolkit (EMTK) to guide the process
       of identifying risks and prioritizing sites.
   (f) Describe potential mitigation measures based on the identified risks and compare
       alternatives and justify the selected measures.
   (g) Formulate the mitigation measures for each risk category sites and estimate the associated
       costs for the selected measures. Establish performance indicators for monitoring the
       implementation of these measures.
   (h) Provide options with cost estimates, taking into account the available budget, for: (i) a clean
       sweep of inventoried obsolete pesticides and safeguarding of contaminated soils or (ii) a risk
       reduction strategy that would focus on a combination of safeguarding and destruction of obsolete
       pesticides and contaminated soil based on PSMS risk profiles.

Based on the CESA recommendations, Government would inform the Bank of the option it
would prefer to pursue.

The EMTK deals with determining the risk associated with individual pesticide storage
locations. This methodology is automated in the PSMS. The EMTK spells out a series of risk
management /mitigation strategies to (a) transportation; (b) selection of storage locations; (c)
safeguarding; and (d) store/stock management.

The Consultant should note the following:

Soil assessment

ASP PMU in Tanzania conducted a rapid assessment for contaminated soils and buried
pesticides countrywide through FAO support under ASP, where 22 sites were identified. The
rapid assessment report was handed over to international and national consultants to proceed
with development of a sampling plan. The consultants did soil sampling and analysis and
                                                 235
produced an Assessment Report including an Environmental Management Plan (EMP) for the
contaminated sites. About 600 tons of heavily contaminated soil has been estimated based on the
Soil Assessment Report. This amount has to be included in the disposal/remediation plan by the
CESA consultant.

The Soil Assessment Report that explain and propose the mitigation measures required to
remediate those mostly affected sites will be made available to the CESA consultant to review
and use in preparation of the final CESA document.


Management of Empty Containers
For the case of empty container information, data obtained during the inventory exercise will
determine the way forward. If the problem is significant, a separate study will be required to be
undertaken and the final report which will define disposal options. Depending on the timing of
this study, it could be used to inform the CESA final document. If the problem is found to be
insignificant, the CESA consultant in collaboration with the PMU shall identify and propose
suitable disposal alternative/options for empty containers.

Resettlement:
Should the screening process identify a possibility of changes to access rights to land or water a
Resettlement Policy Framework (RPF) will complement the CESA document. This Framework
will outline the handling of issues associated with resettlement, land acquisition, restriction of
access to resources, and similar situations associated with ASP-P1. For specific sites, additional
resettlement instruments may need to be prepared.

Consultations:
The consultations process will reflect the CESA process of screening of all sites and additional
assessment of hot spots. For all sites and their disposal and mitigation measures, the
consultations will take place at a national level with the key stakeholders, e.g., those represented
in the NSCs. For high risk sites and any sites where input of local communities will be
important, e.g., sites with resettlement or significant social impacts, consultations will also take
place at the local level. The social data collected during pesticide inventories and consultations
with communities will be used to determine the level of consultation effort appropriate for each
site. Where local consultations will be carried out, NGOs and civil society organizations (CSO)
will be involved in facilitating the process. At all sites with inhabitation and along sensitive
transportation routes, the project will carry out an information and awareness campaign to
enhance public safety, again involving NGOs and/or CSOs as much as practical.


8. Deliverable

The CESA Consultant shall report as follows:

       A preliminary report shall be submitted not later than 6 weeks after signing the contract,
       and following the desk review, the analysis of inventory data and preparation in close
       collaboration with the PMU of the outline of the safeguarding and disposal strategy,

                                                236
      A draft final report, following the field visits and final consultation of the PMU that will
      be presented to a Consultative workshop organized by the PMU within 2 weeks after
      submission of the draft final report (approximately 4-6 weeks after completion of the
      preliminary draft report).
      A Final report shall be submitted after incorporating comments from the consultative
      workshop not later than 2 weeks after receipt of comments from the PMU, and the
      comments from the World Bank.


9. Duration of the assignment

The CESA/EMP is scheduled to be carried out over a 4 month period of time, beginning
approximately on 9th November 2009 and concluding around 8th March 2010.

                                        Annex 2

         Environmental Assessment and EMP for Sulfur Stockpile in Tanga
The Inventory of obsolete pesticides has revealed about 660 metric tons of unwanted
sulfur in one site at Tanga. The sulfur stock was discussed at length during several
sessions at the Joint Mid-Term Review Mission that was held during October 5 – 16,
2009. It was concluded that the sulfur cannot be disposed of or treated under the ASP
Tanzania project due to the following reasons:
       The sulfur was never registered as a pesticide (the application was rejected by the
       Registrar) and has never been in a condition to be used as a pesticide.
       The sulfur is privately owned, not part of the publicly held stocks for which ASP
       is responsible.
       The sulfur presents a much lower risk to environment and human populations than
       the soil contamination or obsolete stocks found elsewhere. As such, even if it
       were eligible for the programme (which it is not), it would not be of a sufficiently
       high priority to be dealt with within current resources.
However, the sulfur stock at Tanga is of understandable concern to the local population
and the Government of Tanzania. Therefore, the ASP Tanzania Project will support
government efforts to find a solution to the problem by offering specialist advice on
options for disposal within Tanzania.
In order to expedite these efforts, this annex has been included in the TORs for the CESA
in order to formulate options for safeguarding and disposal of sulfur within Tanzania.
.
In view of foregoing, the CESA Consultant shall;

   1. Include the Tanga sulfur as a separate site under CESA;

   2. Prepare Safeguarding and disposal options for the sulfur;

   3. Indicate strategic Environmental Mitigation Plan for the site;

   4. Develop an estimated budget for the remediation of the site; and

   5. Prepare a report for the sulfur stock at Tanga site as a separate Annex to the Main
      CESA Report.
